################################################################################
#                          PHASE 1: GIT                                        #
################################################################################

================================================================================
1. FOUNDATION — Basic Concepts
================================================================================

CONCEPT: What is Git?
-----------------------
Git is a distributed version control system (DVCS). Unlike centralized VCS
(SVN, CVS), every developer holds a FULL copy of the repository history locally.
This means:
  - No single point of failure
  - Offline commits and branching
  - Extremely fast operations (no network round-trips for most commands)

Git stores data as a series of SNAPSHOTS of a filesystem, not diffs.
Each commit points to a tree of file-system snapshots.

KEY OBJECTS in Git's object model:
  blob   → stores raw file content (no filename)
  tree   → directory listing (maps filenames → blobs/subtrees)
  commit → snapshot pointer (tree + parent + author + message)
  tag    → annotated label on a commit

All objects are stored by their SHA-1 (now SHA-256 in newer repos) hash under
.git/objects/. Git is essentially a content-addressed filesystem.

CONCEPT: The Three Trees (Working Tree, Index, HEAD)
------------------------------------------------------
  Working Tree  → your local files on disk (untracked / modified)
  Index (Stage) → snapshot being prepared for the next commit
  HEAD          → pointer to the last commit on the current branch

Understanding how data flows between these three areas is fundamental to
understanding every Git command.

  git add <file>      → Working Tree → Index
  git commit          → Index → Repository (new commit)
  git checkout <file> → HEAD → Working Tree (discard local change)
  git reset HEAD <f>  → Index ← HEAD (unstage without touching disk)

CONCEPT: .git Directory Anatomy
---------------------------------
  .git/
  ├── HEAD           → current branch ref (e.g., ref: refs/heads/main)
  ├── config         → repo-level config (remote URLs, user, merge strategy)
  ├── index          → binary staging area file
  ├── COMMIT_EDITMSG → last commit message
  ├── objects/       → all blobs, trees, commits, tags (content-addressed store)
  │   ├── info/
  │   └── pack/      → packed objects (after git gc)
  ├── refs/
  │   ├── heads/     → local branch tips
  │   ├── remotes/   → remote-tracking branch tips
  │   └── tags/      → tag refs
  ├── logs/          → reflog entries (HEAD, branches)
  ├── hooks/         → client-side hook scripts
  └── packed-refs    → compressed refs after git pack-refs

HANDS-ON TASKS (Foundation):
  1. git init myrepo && cd myrepo
  2. Create a file, stage it, commit it
  3. Run: git cat-file -t <sha>   → inspect object type
  4. Run: git cat-file -p <sha>   → inspect object content
  5. Walk .git/objects manually — see blobs, trees, commits
  6. git log --oneline --graph --all
  7. git diff, git diff --staged, git status

REAL-WORLD SCENARIO:
  A junior dev asks: "Why did git say my branch diverged from origin/main?"
  Answer: Their local HEAD and origin/main both moved forward independently.
  They need git fetch + git rebase origin/main or git merge origin/main.
  Understanding the three-tree model and remote-tracking refs makes this
  instantly clear.

================================================================================
2. CORE CONCEPTS — Intermediate
================================================================================

CONCEPT: Branching Internals
------------------------------
A branch is just a lightweight movable pointer (a 41-byte file in refs/heads/)
to a commit SHA. Creating a branch is O(1) — no file copying.

  git branch feature/login    → creates .git/refs/heads/feature/login
  git checkout -b feature/x   → creates + switches HEAD
  git switch -c feature/x     → modern equivalent

HEAD is a symbolic ref. When on a branch: HEAD → refs/heads/main → <sha>.
In "detached HEAD" state: HEAD → <sha> directly. Commits made here are
orphaned once you switch away (unless you create a branch or tag).

CONCEPT: Merging Strategies
-----------------------------
Fast-Forward Merge:
  When the target branch has not diverged — Git simply moves the pointer.
  No merge commit. Linear history.
  git merge --ff-only feature/x

3-Way Merge:
  When both branches have diverged, Git finds the common ancestor (merge base)
  and creates a merge commit with two parents.
  git merge feature/x

Squash Merge:
  Flattens all commits from the feature branch into ONE commit on the target.
  Does NOT preserve history of individual commits.
  git merge --squash feature/x && git commit

CONCEPT: Rebasing
------------------
git rebase rewrites commit history by replaying commits on top of another base.

  git rebase main         → replays current branch commits on top of main
  git rebase -i HEAD~5    → interactive rebase: squash, reorder, edit, drop

Rebase vs Merge:
  Rebase → linear history, cleaner log, REWRITES SHA hashes
  Merge  → preserves full history, non-destructive

GOLDEN RULE: Never rebase commits that have been pushed to a shared remote
branch. Rewriting shared history causes diverged repos for teammates.

Interactive Rebase Operations:
  pick   → use commit as-is
  reword → use commit but edit message
  edit   → pause and amend the commit
  squash → meld into previous commit (keep messages)
  fixup  → meld into previous commit (discard message)
  drop   → remove the commit entirely
  exec   → run a shell command between commits

CONCEPT: Remote Repositories & Tracking
-----------------------------------------
  git remote add origin <url>
  git remote -v
  git fetch origin          → download objects/refs, DON'T merge
  git pull origin main      → fetch + merge (or rebase if configured)
  git push origin main
  git push -u origin main   → set upstream tracking

Remote-tracking branches (origin/main) are read-only local bookmarks of the
remote state. They update only on git fetch/pull.

  git branch -vv            → show tracking relationships

CONCEPT: Tags
--------------
Lightweight tag:   git tag v1.0.0           → just a ref pointer
Annotated tag:     git tag -a v1.0.0 -m "Release"  → full tag object
Signed tag:        git tag -s v1.0.0         → GPG-signed annotated tag
Push tags:         git push origin --tags
Delete remote tag: git push origin :refs/tags/v1.0.0

In production: always use ANNOTATED tags for releases. They contain tagger
identity, date, and message. Semantic Versioning (SemVer): MAJOR.MINOR.PATCH.

CONCEPT: Stashing
------------------
Stash temporarily shelves changes that aren't ready to commit.

  git stash                      → stash working tree + index
  git stash push -m "WIP: login" → named stash
  git stash list                 → list all stashes
  git stash pop                  → apply latest + remove from stash
  git stash apply stash@{2}      → apply specific, keep in list
  git stash drop stash@{0}
  git stash branch feature/x     → create branch from stash (avoids conflicts)
  git stash -u                   → include untracked files
  git stash -p                   → interactive patch stash

CONCEPT: .gitignore
--------------------
  Patterns:
    *.log             → all .log files
    /build            → only root-level build dir
    build/            → any dir named build
    !important.log    → negate: don't ignore this
    **/*.class        → any depth

  Precedence (highest → lowest):
    1. Command line patterns
    2. .gitignore in same dir
    3. .gitignore in parent dirs (up to repo root)
    4. $GIT_DIR/info/exclude
    5. ~/.gitignore (global)

  git check-ignore -v <file>    → debug why a file is ignored

HANDS-ON TASKS (Core):
  1. Create two branches, make diverging commits, perform 3-way merge
  2. Use git rebase -i to squash 4 commits into 1
  3. Simulate a detached HEAD, make commits, recover with git branch
  4. Use git stash -p to partially stash changes
  5. Create annotated + signed tags, push to remote
  6. Configure a .gitignore for a Node.js project
  7. git log --graph --oneline --all → visualize branch history

REAL-WORLD SCENARIO:
  Production hotfix workflow: main branch is in prod. A bug is found.
  1. git checkout -b hotfix/CVE-2024-xyz main
  2. Fix, commit, test
  3. git checkout main && git merge --no-ff hotfix/CVE-2024-xyz
  4. git tag -a v2.3.1 -m "Security patch"
  5. git push origin main --tags
  6. git checkout develop && git merge hotfix/CVE-2024-xyz
  Clean, traceable, no drift between environments.

================================================================================
3. ADVANCED — Deep Topics
================================================================================

CONCEPT: Git Internals — Pack Files & Garbage Collection
----------------------------------------------------------
Loose objects (one file per SHA) are efficient for writing but not reading.
Git compresses them into PACK files (.pack + .idx) using delta compression.

  git gc              → run garbage collection (pack loose objects, prune)
  git gc --aggressive → deeper delta compression (slower)
  git count-objects -vH → count loose objects and pack sizes
  git verify-pack -v .git/objects/pack/*.idx → inspect pack file contents

In large repos, pack files are critical for performance. GitHub's "alternates"
mechanism lets multiple repos share pack files on disk.

CONCEPT: The Reflog — Your Safety Net
---------------------------------------
The reflog records every movement of HEAD and branch tips, even after
destructive operations. It persists for 90 days by default.

  git reflog                  → show HEAD movements
  git reflog show main        → show movements of main branch
  git checkout HEAD@{3}       → go to where HEAD was 3 moves ago
  git branch recovered HEAD@{5}  → recover "lost" commits

Scenario: You accidentally ran git reset --hard and "lost" commits.
  git reflog → find SHA before the reset
  git reset --hard <sha>  → restore

CONCEPT: Cherry-Pick
----------------------
Apply specific commits from one branch to another without merging the whole
branch.

  git cherry-pick <sha>             → apply single commit
  git cherry-pick a1b2..d3e4        → range (exclusive start)
  git cherry-pick a1b2^..d3e4       → range (inclusive start)
  git cherry-pick -n <sha>          → apply changes without committing
  git cherry-pick --continue        → after resolving conflicts
  git cherry-pick --abort

Real-world: Backporting a security fix from main to a release/1.x branch
without pulling in unrelated features.

CONCEPT: git bisect — Binary Search for Bugs
----------------------------------------------
Automates finding which commit introduced a bug using binary search.

  git bisect start
  git bisect bad HEAD             → current commit is broken
  git bisect good v2.1.0          → this tag was working
  → Git checks out a midpoint commit
  git bisect good / git bisect bad → mark each checkpoint
  git bisect reset                 → return to HEAD when done

Automated bisect:
  git bisect start HEAD v2.1.0
  git bisect run ./run_tests.sh    → script returns 0=good, non-0=bad
  → Git automatically finds the first bad commit

CONCEPT: git blame & git log Power Usage
------------------------------------------
  git blame -L 40,60 src/app.go     → show who changed lines 40-60
  git blame -w                       → ignore whitespace changes
  git blame -M                       → detect moved lines
  git blame -C                       → detect copied lines from other files

  git log -S "function_name"         → pickaxe: find commits adding/removing string
  git log -G "regex_pattern"         → commits where diff matches regex
  git log --follow -- path/to/file   → follow renames
  git log --diff-filter=D -- file    → find commit that deleted a file
  git log --author="Jane" --since="2 weeks ago"
  git log --merges / --no-merges
  git log --format="%H %s %ae" --no-walk $(git tag -l "v*")

CONCEPT: Submodules & Subtrees
--------------------------------
Submodules:
  A repo inside a repo. The outer repo stores a pointer (commit SHA) to the
  inner repo, not the files themselves.

  git submodule add <url> path/to/sub
  git submodule update --init --recursive
  git submodule foreach git pull origin main
  .gitmodules → config file for submodule paths/urls

  Pain points: teammates forget to init; detached HEAD in submodule; updating
  submodule SHA requires a commit in the parent.

Subtrees:
  Merges another repo's history directly into a subdirectory. No extra files.
  git subtree add --prefix=lib/foo <url> main --squash
  git subtree pull --prefix=lib/foo <url> main --squash
  git subtree push --prefix=lib/foo <url> main

CONCEPT: Worktrees
-------------------
Check out multiple branches simultaneously into different directories.
No need to stash/switch — each worktree is independent.

  git worktree add ../hotfix hotfix/CVE-2024-xyz
  git worktree list
  git worktree remove ../hotfix

Real-world: Running tests on main while developing on feature/x, both live,
no context switching.

CONCEPT: Git Hooks
-------------------
Scripts that run automatically on Git events. Stored in .git/hooks/.

CLIENT-SIDE HOOKS:
  pre-commit       → runs before commit is recorded (lint, format, secret scan)
  prepare-commit-msg → modifies default commit message
  commit-msg       → validates commit message (enforce Conventional Commits)
  post-commit      → notification after commit
  pre-rebase       → prevent rebasing published commits
  pre-push         → run tests before push; block if failing

SERVER-SIDE HOOKS:
  pre-receive      → runs before refs are updated; can reject pushes
  update           → per-ref version of pre-receive
  post-receive     → triggers CI, sends notifications after successful push
  post-update      → legacy; use post-receive instead

Sharing hooks: .git/hooks/ is NOT committed. Use:
  - Husky (Node.js projects): npx husky install
  - pre-commit framework (Python): .pre-commit-config.yaml
  - git config core.hooksPath .githooks → point to committed directory

CONCEPT: Conventional Commits
-------------------------------
A specification for commit messages that enables automated changelogs and
semantic versioning.

Format: <type>(<scope>): <description>

Types: feat, fix, docs, style, refactor, perf, test, build, ci, chore, revert
Breaking change: add "!" after type or "BREAKING CHANGE:" in footer.

  feat(auth): add OAuth2 support
  fix(api): handle null pointer in user endpoint
  feat!: drop support for Python 3.8
  ci(github-actions): add trivy image scan step

Tools: commitlint, commitizen, standard-version, semantic-release

CONCEPT: Git Configuration Layers
-----------------------------------
  System:  /etc/gitconfig                   → all users on machine
  Global:  ~/.gitconfig or ~/.config/git/config → current user
  Local:   .git/config                      → current repo (overrides global)
  Worktree: .git/config.worktree

  git config --global user.name "Jane"
  git config --local core.autocrlf false
  git config --list --show-origin           → see all config with source

Key configs:
  core.autocrlf         → line ending handling (true/input/false)
  pull.rebase           → true = git pull rebases instead of merges
  push.default          → simple/current/upstream/matching
  merge.ff              → only/false/true
  rerere.enabled        → true = record + replay conflict resolutions
  diff.algorithm        → patience/histogram (better diff readability)
  core.hooksPath        → shared hooks directory

HANDS-ON TASKS (Advanced):
  1. Use git bisect run to automatically find a regression
  2. Set up a commit-msg hook enforcing Conventional Commits format
  3. Use git log -S to find when a specific function was removed
  4. Add a submodule, update it, and handle a detached HEAD situation
  5. Set up git worktrees for parallel branch work
  6. Recover "lost" commits using git reflog after git reset --hard
  7. Use git rerere to record and replay a conflict resolution
  8. Write a pre-push hook that blocks if go test ./... fails

REAL-WORLD SCENARIO:
  A developer ran git push --force on a shared branch and teammates' local
  branches diverged.
  Recovery:
    1. git reflog origin/main (if using git log on remote)
    2. Force-push to restore: git push --force-with-lease origin <sha>:main
    3. Teammates: git fetch; git reset --hard origin/main
  Prevention: Configure branch protection rules + use --force-with-lease
  instead of --force (--force-with-lease checks that nobody else pushed).

================================================================================
4. EXPERT LEVEL — Production-Grade Knowledge
================================================================================

CONCEPT: Branching Strategies
-------------------------------
GITFLOW:
  Branches: main, develop, feature/*, release/*, hotfix/*
  Best for: versioned releases, longer release cycles
  main    → always deployable, tagged with versions
  develop → integration branch
  feature → branch from develop, merge back to develop
  release → branch from develop, merge to main + develop + tag
  hotfix  → branch from main, merge to main + develop

TRUNK-BASED DEVELOPMENT (TBD):
  Single long-lived branch (main/trunk). Feature branches live < 2 days.
  Feature flags hide incomplete work. Engineers push frequently.
  Best for: CI/CD pipelines, high-velocity teams, SaaS products.
  Forces: small commits, short-lived branches, continuous integration.

GITHUB FLOW:
  Simplified: main is prod. Branch, PR, review, merge to main, deploy.
  Best for: small teams, web services with continuous deployment.

GITLAB FLOW:
  Adds environment branches (main → staging → production).
  Merge requests flow downstream through environments.

PRODUCTION CHOICE GUIDE (not comparison, just context):
  Microservices + CI/CD + fast releases → Trunk-Based Development
  Enterprise + scheduled releases + compliance → Gitflow
  Small SaaS teams → GitHub Flow

CONCEPT: Merge Request / Pull Request Best Practices
------------------------------------------------------
  - Keep PRs small (< 400 lines diff is the industry sweet spot)
  - One logical change per PR
  - Draft PRs for early feedback
  - Required reviewers + CODEOWNERS file
  - Require linear history (enforce rebase-before-merge)
  - Protected branches: no direct push to main/develop
  - Require status checks (CI must pass before merge)
  - Signed commits for audit trails

CODEOWNERS file:
  # .github/CODEOWNERS
  /infrastructure/    @platform-team
  /src/auth/          @security-team
  *.go                @backend-team
  Automatically assigns reviewers based on changed files.

CONCEPT: Git at Scale — Monorepos
-----------------------------------
Monorepos contain multiple services/packages in one repo.

Challenges:
  - Slow git operations on huge repos
  - Irrelevant CI triggers (changes in service-A shouldn't test service-B)
  - Noisy blame/log

Solutions:
  - Sparse checkout: git sparse-checkout set path/to/service
  - Partial clone: git clone --filter=blob:none → skip blobs until needed
  - git clone --depth=1 → shallow clone for CI (no history)
  - Path-based CI triggers (only run pipelines for changed directories)
  - tools: Bazel (build), nx (JS), Turbo (JS), Pants (Python)

  git clone --filter=blob:none --no-checkout <url>
  git sparse-checkout init --cone
  git sparse-checkout set services/api services/auth

CONCEPT: Security — Preventing Secrets in Git
-----------------------------------------------
Secret scanning tools:
  - git-secrets (AWS patterns)
  - truffleHog (entropy + regex scanning)
  - gitleaks (comprehensive, SARIF output)
  - detect-secrets (Yelp; baseline file)
  - GitHub secret scanning (built-in)

Pre-commit integration:
  # .pre-commit-config.yaml
  repos:
    - repo: https://github.com/zricethezav/gitleaks
      rev: v8.18.0
      hooks:
        - id: gitleaks

If a secret IS committed:
  1. Immediately rotate the secret (assume it's compromised)
  2. git filter-repo --path secret-file --invert-paths (rewrites history)
  3. Force-push all branches
  4. All collaborators must re-clone (history is rewritten)
  NEVER use git filter-branch (deprecated, slow, dangerous).

CONCEPT: git filter-repo
--------------------------
The official replacement for git filter-branch. Rewrites entire repo history.

Use cases:
  - Remove a large binary file committed by mistake
  - Strip a directory from history before open-sourcing
  - Change all author emails after org migration

  pip install git-filter-repo
  git filter-repo --path passwords.txt --invert-paths
  git filter-repo --email-callback "return email.replace(b'old@co.com', b'new@co.com')"
  git filter-repo --strip-blobs-bigger-than 50M

CONCEPT: Signing Commits & Tags
---------------------------------
GPG-signed commits prove identity and are required in high-security environments
(government, financial, regulated industries).

  gpg --gen-key
  gpg --list-secret-keys --keyid-format LONG
  git config --global user.signingkey <KEY_ID>
  git config --global commit.gpgsign true
  git commit -S -m "Signed commit"
  git tag -s v1.0.0 -m "Signed release"
  git verify-commit <sha>
  git verify-tag v1.0.0

SSH signing (newer, simpler):
  git config gpg.format ssh
  git config user.signingkey ~/.ssh/id_ed25519.pub

GitHub: Upload GPG/SSH public key → commits show "Verified" badge.

CONCEPT: Git Server Administration
------------------------------------
Hosting options:
  Gitea    → self-hosted, lightweight, Go-based
  GitLab   → full DevOps platform (self-hosted or SaaS)
  Gitea/Forgejo → FOSS GitLab alternative
  Bare repos → git init --bare; push via SSH to raw server

Repository size management:
  git lfs (Large File Storage) → stores large binaries outside the repo
    git lfs install
    git lfs track "*.psd" "*.zip" "*.mp4"
    git lfs ls-files
    Large files stored on LFS server; repo stores pointer files.

  git lfs is critical for repos with assets, ML models, or binary releases.

CONCEPT: Advanced git log Formatting & Scripting
--------------------------------------------------
Custom log formats for automation and reporting:

  git log --format="%H|%an|%ae|%ai|%s" > commits.csv
  git shortlog -sn --no-merges         → commit count per author
  git log --since="1 week ago" --stat  → activity report
  git log --all --full-history -- "**/auth*"  → find deleted file history

Finding largest objects in repo history:
  git rev-list --objects --all | \
    git cat-file --batch-check='%(objecttype) %(objectname) %(objectsize) %(rest)' | \
    grep '^blob' | sort -k3 -rn | head -20

CONCEPT: Merge Conflict Resolution — Production Workflow
----------------------------------------------------------
Types of conflicts:
  Content conflict  → same lines changed differently in both branches
  Delete/modify     → one side deleted, other modified
  Mode conflict     → file permissions changed differently

Tools:
  git mergetool               → opens configured 3-way merge tool
  git config merge.tool vimdiff/vscode/intellij
  git checkout --ours file    → take our version entirely
  git checkout --theirs file  → take their version entirely

rerere (Reuse Recorded Resolution):
  git config rerere.enabled true
  Once you resolve a conflict, Git records the resolution. If the SAME
  conflict appears again (e.g., long-running feature branch), Git auto-resolves.
  git rerere diff   → see recorded resolution
  git rerere forget → clear a bad resolution

CONCEPT: Git Internals — Transfer Protocols
---------------------------------------------
Smart HTTP: Git speaks its own protocol over HTTPS.
  Push/pull negotiation: client sends "have" SHAs, server sends "want" SHAs.
  Server computes minimal pack to transmit. Efficient.

SSH: Git uses SSH for transport; authentication handled by SSH keys/agent.
  SSH agent forwarding lets CI servers use developer keys without storing them.

git bundle: Pack entire repo or range into a single file (offline transfer).
  git bundle create repo.bundle --all
  git bundle create partial.bundle HEAD~10..HEAD
  git clone repo.bundle myrepo
  Used in air-gapped environments.

HANDS-ON TASKS (Expert):
  1. Set up gitleaks as a pre-commit hook; test with a fake API key
  2. Use git filter-repo to remove a large file from entire history
  3. Implement GPG commit signing; verify signatures in git log --show-signature
  4. Configure git sparse-checkout for a simulated monorepo
  5. Set up git lfs for binary files; inspect pointer files
  6. Enable rerere; intentionally create + resolve a conflict twice
  7. Use git bundle to "transfer" a repo without network access
  8. Write a shell script that parses git log output into a CSV audit report
  9. Implement full Gitflow branching manually (no tools)
  10. Configure branch protection rules + CODEOWNERS in GitHub/GitLab

REAL-WORLD SCENARIOS:
  SCENARIO A — Secret exposed in commit history:
    1. Rotate the secret immediately (revoke API key, generate new one)
    2. pip install git-filter-repo
    3. git filter-repo --path .env --invert-paths
    4. git remote add origin <url> && git push --force --all
    5. Notify all team members to re-clone
    6. Add .env to .gitignore + add gitleaks pre-commit hook
    7. Enable GitHub secret scanning for future protection

  SCENARIO B — Monorepo CI optimization:
    PR touches services/payment/. CI should ONLY run:
      - Unit tests for services/payment/
      - Integration tests that depend on payment service
    Implementation: git diff --name-only origin/main...HEAD | grep "^services/payment/"
    If output non-empty → trigger payment pipeline; else skip.
    Used in tools like Nx affected, Turborepo, and custom GitHub Actions filters.

  SCENARIO C — Audit: Who deployed what and when?
    git log --merges --first-parent main --format="%ai %an %s" --since="30 days ago"
    Combined with signed commits and protected branches → full audit trail.
    Used in SOC2, ISO 27001, PCI-DSS compliance.

================================================================================
1. FOUNDATION — Basic Concepts
================================================================================

CONCEPT: Linux Architecture Overview
--------------------------------------
Linux is a monolithic kernel with modular design. The architecture layers:

  Hardware
    ↓
  Kernel Space
    ├── Process Scheduler
    ├── Memory Manager (Virtual Memory, Paging, Swapping)
    ├── VFS (Virtual Filesystem Switch — abstraction over ext4, xfs, etc.)
    ├── Network Stack (TCP/IP implementation)
    ├── Device Drivers
    └── System Call Interface
    ↓
  User Space
    ├── C Library (glibc / musl)
    ├── Shell (bash, zsh, sh)
    ├── System Daemons (systemd, journald, udevd)
    └── User Applications

System calls are the boundary between user space and kernel space.
Everything a program does (open a file, fork a process, send a packet) goes
through a syscall. Tools like strace reveal these calls.

CONCEPT: Filesystem Hierarchy Standard (FHS)
---------------------------------------------
  /           → root filesystem
  /bin        → essential user binaries (ls, cp, mv) — symlink to /usr/bin in modern distros
  /sbin       → system binaries (ifconfig, fdisk) — symlink to /usr/sbin
  /usr        → secondary hierarchy (most programs live here)
  /usr/bin    → non-essential user binaries
  /usr/lib    → shared libraries for /usr/bin
  /usr/local  → locally installed software (not from package manager)
  /etc        → system-wide configuration files (NEVER binary)
  /var        → variable data: logs (/var/log), spool, databases
  /tmp        → temporary files (cleared on reboot by default)
  /run        → runtime data (PIDs, sockets) — tmpfs, cleared on reboot
  /proc       → virtual FS: kernel/process info (not on disk)
  /sys        → virtual FS: kernel device/driver info (sysfs)
  /dev        → device files (block, char devices)
  /home       → user home directories
  /root       → root user home
  /boot       → bootloader, kernel images, initramfs
  /lib        → libraries for /bin and /sbin — symlink to /usr/lib
  /mnt        → temporary mount points
  /media      → removable media mount points
  /opt        → optional/third-party software packages
  /srv        → data served by the system (www, ftp)

CONCEPT: File Types in Linux
------------------------------
  -   → regular file
  d   → directory
  l   → symbolic link
  b   → block device (disk: /dev/sda)
  c   → character device (terminal: /dev/tty)
  p   → named pipe (FIFO)
  s   → socket (Unix domain socket)

  ls -la shows the type as the first character of permissions.
  file <path> → determines actual file type from magic bytes

CONCEPT: Users, Groups, Permissions
--------------------------------------
Every file has: owner (user), group, and permissions for owner/group/others.

  -rwxr-xr--  1  alice  devops  4096  Jan 1 10:00  script.sh
   |||||||||||
   ||||||||||└─ others: read only
   |||||||└─── group (devops): read + execute
   ||||└────── owner (alice): read + write + execute
   └─────────── file type (regular file)

Octal representation:
  r=4, w=2, x=1
  rwx = 7, r-x = 5, r-- = 4
  chmod 754 file.sh → rwxr-xr--

Special permission bits:
  setuid (4xxx): file executes as owner (e.g., /usr/bin/passwd runs as root)
  setgid (2xxx): files in dir inherit the dir's group; binaries run as group
  sticky bit (1xxx): on dirs, only file owner can delete (e.g., /tmp)

  chmod u+s binary   → setuid
  chmod g+s dir/     → setgid
  chmod +t /tmp      → sticky

  find / -perm -4000 2>/dev/null  → find all setuid binaries (security audit)

CONCEPT: Essential Command Mastery
------------------------------------
File Operations:
  ls -lah --color=auto
  cp -a src/ dst/       → archive mode: preserve permissions, timestamps, symlinks
  mv, rm -rf, mkdir -p
  ln -s target link     → symbolic link
  ln target hardlink    → hard link (same inode)
  touch, stat, file

Text Processing:
  cat, less, more, head -n 20, tail -f, tail -n +5
  wc -l/-w/-c           → lines/words/bytes
  sort -k2 -n -r        → sort by column 2, numeric, reverse
  uniq -c               → count consecutive duplicates
  cut -d: -f1,3         → cut fields from delimited text
  tr 'a-z' 'A-Z'        → translate characters
  paste -d, file1 file2 → merge files side by side

Search:
  grep -r -i -n -E "pattern" dir/
  grep -v "exclude"      → invert match
  grep -c                → count matches
  grep -l                → only filenames
  grep -A 3 -B 3 "err"   → context lines

HANDS-ON TASKS (Foundation):
  1. Navigate full FHS, understand purpose of each dir
  2. Create users and groups, set permissions with chmod/chown
  3. Find all setuid binaries on the system
  4. Practice ls -la and understand every field
  5. Use stat to see inode info, timestamps, permissions
  6. Create hard and soft links; understand inode sharing

================================================================================
2. CORE CONCEPTS — Intermediate
================================================================================

CONCEPT: Process Management
-----------------------------
Every process has:
  PID   → process ID
  PPID  → parent process ID
  UID   → real user ID running the process
  EUID  → effective user ID (can differ with setuid)
  Nice  → priority (-20 highest, 19 lowest)
  State → R(running), S(sleeping), D(uninterruptible sleep), Z(zombie), T(stopped)

Process commands:
  ps aux             → all processes, user-oriented format
  ps -eo pid,ppid,cmd,%cpu,%mem --sort=-%cpu
  top / htop         → interactive process viewer
  pgrep nginx        → find PID by name
  pkill -HUP nginx   → send signal by name
  kill -9 <pid>      → SIGKILL (cannot be caught; last resort)
  kill -15 <pid>     → SIGTERM (graceful shutdown; default)
  kill -1  <pid>     → SIGHUP (reload config without restart)
  kill -19 <pid>     → SIGSTOP (pause)
  kill -18 <pid>     → SIGCONT (resume)

Signal table (key signals):
  1  SIGHUP   → hangup / reload config
  2  SIGINT   → Ctrl+C (interrupt)
  9  SIGKILL  → force kill (cannot be caught or ignored)
  11 SIGSEGV  → segmentation fault
  15 SIGTERM  → graceful terminate
  17 SIGCHLD  → child process stopped/exited
  18 SIGCONT  → continue if stopped
  19 SIGSTOP  → stop (cannot be caught)

Process priority:
  nice -n 10 ./cpu-heavy.sh     → start with lower priority
  renice -n 5 -p <pid>          → change priority of running process

Background/foreground jobs:
  command &         → run in background
  Ctrl+Z            → suspend foreground job
  bg %1             → resume job 1 in background
  fg %1             → bring job 1 to foreground
  jobs -l           → list all jobs with PIDs
  disown %1         → detach job from shell (survives shell exit)
  nohup command &   → immune to SIGHUP (survives terminal close)

CONCEPT: Systemd — Service Management
---------------------------------------
systemd is init system (PID 1) + service manager + logging system + more.
It replaced SysVinit and Upstart. Uses unit files.

Key unit types:
  .service  → daemon/application
  .target   → grouping (like runlevels): multi-user.target, graphical.target
  .timer    → cron replacement (calendar/monotonic timers)
  .mount    → filesystem mounts
  .socket   → socket activation
  .path     → watch filesystem path for changes

Essential commands:
  systemctl status nginx
  systemctl start|stop|restart|reload nginx
  systemctl enable nginx          → start on boot (creates symlink)
  systemctl disable nginx
  systemctl daemon-reload         → reload unit files after editing
  systemctl list-units --type=service --state=running
  systemctl list-unit-files       → all units and their state
  systemctl cat nginx             → show unit file content
  systemctl show nginx            → show all properties
  systemctl edit nginx            → create override (drop-in file)

Unit file anatomy:
  [Unit]
  Description=My Service
  After=network.target
  Requires=postgresql.service

  [Service]
  Type=simple|forking|oneshot|notify|idle
  User=appuser
  Group=appgroup
  WorkingDirectory=/opt/myapp
  ExecStart=/opt/myapp/server --port 8080
  ExecReload=/bin/kill -HUP $MAINPID
  Restart=on-failure
  RestartSec=5s
  LimitNOFILE=65536
  Environment=APP_ENV=production
  EnvironmentFile=/etc/myapp/env

  [Install]
  WantedBy=multi-user.target

Targets (runlevel equivalents):
  poweroff.target   → runlevel 0
  rescue.target     → runlevel 1
  multi-user.target → runlevel 3
  graphical.target  → runlevel 5
  reboot.target     → runlevel 6

CONCEPT: journald — Centralized Logging
-----------------------------------------
  journalctl -u nginx              → logs for nginx unit
  journalctl -f                    → follow (like tail -f)
  journalctl -n 100                → last 100 lines
  journalctl --since "1 hour ago"
  journalctl --since "2024-01-01" --until "2024-01-02"
  journalctl -p err                → only error priority and above
  journalctl -k                    → kernel messages only
  journalctl --disk-usage          → see journal size
  journalctl --vacuum-size=500M    → shrink journal to 500MB
  journalctl -o json-pretty        → JSON output for parsing

CONCEPT: Filesystem & Disk Management
---------------------------------------
Partition tools:
  fdisk /dev/sda     → MBR partitions (legacy)
  gdisk /dev/sda     → GPT partitions (modern)
  parted /dev/sda    → supports both

Filesystems:
  ext4   → default Linux FS; journaled; mature
  xfs    → high-performance; great for large files; default on RHEL
  btrfs  → copy-on-write; snapshots; subvolumes; checksums
  tmpfs  → RAM-backed; used for /tmp, /run

  mkfs.ext4 /dev/sdb1
  mkfs.xfs  /dev/sdb2
  mount /dev/sdb1 /mnt/data
  umount /mnt/data

/etc/fstab — persistent mounts:
  UUID=abc123  /data  ext4  defaults,noatime  0  2
  Fields: device  mountpoint  fstype  options  dump  pass

  blkid              → list block devices with UUIDs
  lsblk -f           → tree view of block devices with FS info
  df -hT             → disk usage with filesystem type
  du -sh /var/log/*  → directory sizes
  iostat -x 1        → disk I/O statistics per device

CONCEPT: Memory Management
----------------------------
  free -h            → RAM + swap usage
  cat /proc/meminfo  → detailed memory stats
  vmstat 1           → virtual memory stats (processes, swap, I/O, CPU)
  slabtop            → kernel slab cache usage

  Memory concepts:
    Page cache   → kernel caches disk reads in RAM (huge performance impact)
    Swap         → disk used as overflow RAM
    OOM Killer   → kernel kills processes when RAM exhausted
                   /proc/<pid>/oom_score shows kill priority
                   /proc/<pid>/oom_score_adj to adjust (-1000 = never kill)

  Swappiness:
    /proc/sys/vm/swappiness (0-100, default 60)
    0  → don't swap unless absolutely necessary
    1  → minimum swapping
    60 → default: swap when RAM 40% free
    For databases and K8s nodes: set to 0 or 1

  echo 'vm.swappiness=1' >> /etc/sysctl.d/99-custom.conf
  sysctl -p /etc/sysctl.d/99-custom.conf

CONCEPT: Networking Fundamentals
----------------------------------
  ip addr            → show interfaces and IP addresses (replaces ifconfig)
  ip link            → show/manage network interfaces
  ip route           → show/manage routing table
  ip neigh           → ARP table
  ss -tulnp          → listening sockets with process (replaces netstat)
  ss -s              → socket summary statistics

  Network config (modern — NetworkManager or systemd-networkd):
    nmcli dev status
    nmcli con show
    nmcli con mod "eth0" ipv4.addresses 192.168.1.10/24
    nmcli con up "eth0"

  /etc/hosts         → static hostname resolution
  /etc/resolv.conf   → DNS servers
  /etc/nsswitch.conf → name resolution order (files → dns)

  Connectivity testing:
    ping -c 4 8.8.8.8
    traceroute / tracepath
    mtr              → real-time traceroute + ping combined
    curl -v / wget
    nc -zv host port → test TCP connectivity
    telnet host port
    dig domain       → DNS lookup
    dig @8.8.8.8 domain +short  → query specific DNS server
    nslookup domain

CONCEPT: Package Management
-----------------------------
Debian/Ubuntu (APT):
  apt update && apt upgrade
  apt install nginx
  apt remove / apt purge (purge removes config too)
  apt autoremove    → remove unused dependencies
  dpkg -l           → list installed packages
  dpkg -L nginx     → list files installed by nginx package
  dpkg -S /bin/ls   → find which package owns a file
  apt-cache show nginx → package details
  /etc/apt/sources.list + /etc/apt/sources.list.d/

RHEL/CentOS/Rocky (DNF/YUM):
  dnf install nginx
  dnf update
  dnf remove
  rpm -qa           → list all installed packages
  rpm -ql nginx     → files installed by nginx
  rpm -qf /bin/ls   → which package owns the file
  /etc/yum.repos.d/ → repo files

HANDS-ON TASKS (Core):
  1. Write and deploy a custom systemd service unit
  2. Use journalctl to investigate a failing service
  3. Partition a disk, create ext4 FS, mount persistently via fstab
  4. Simulate high memory usage, observe OOM killer via dmesg
  5. Set swappiness to 1, persist across reboots
  6. Use ss -tulnp to audit all listening ports
  7. Trace all syscalls of a command with strace -p <pid>
  8. Use ip route to add a static route

================================================================================
3. ADVANCED — Deep Topics
================================================================================

CONCEPT: Advanced Text Processing (awk, sed, grep)
----------------------------------------------------
awk — field processor and mini-programming language:
  awk '{print $1, $3}' file.txt          → print fields 1 and 3
  awk -F: '{print $1}' /etc/passwd       → custom field separator
  awk '$3 > 1000' /etc/passwd            → filter by condition
  awk '{sum += $2} END {print sum}'      → sum a column
  awk '/ERROR/ {count++} END {print count}' app.log → count errors
  awk '{print NR, $0}' file              → print line numbers
  awk 'NR==5, NR==10'                    → print lines 5-10
  awk '!seen[$0]++'                      → deduplicate lines (preserve order)

  Real-world: Parse nginx access logs to count top 10 IPs:
    awk '{print $1}' /var/log/nginx/access.log | sort | uniq -c | sort -rn | head -10

sed — stream editor:
  sed 's/old/new/g' file                 → global replace
  sed -i 's/old/new/g' file             → in-place edit
  sed -i.bak 's/old/new/g' file         → in-place with backup
  sed -n '5,10p' file                   → print lines 5-10
  sed '/^#/d' config.conf               → delete comment lines
  sed '/^$/d' file                       → delete empty lines
  sed -n '/START/,/END/p' file          → print between markers
  sed 's/[[:space:]]*$//'               → strip trailing whitespace

  Real-world: Update version in all config files:
    find . -name "*.yaml" | xargs sed -i 's/version: 1.0/version: 1.1/g'

CONCEPT: Advanced find Usage
------------------------------
  find / -name "*.log" -mtime +30 -delete         → delete logs older than 30 days
  find /etc -type f -perm /o+w                    → world-writable files (security)
  find / -user root -perm -4000                   → setuid root binaries
  find . -size +100M -type f                      → files larger than 100MB
  find . -newer reference_file                    → files newer than reference
  find . -type f -name "*.py" -exec wc -l {} +   → count lines in all Python files
  find /tmp -type f -atime +7 -exec rm {} \;     → delete unaccessed files
  find . -maxdepth 2 -mindepth 1 -type d         → list dirs at specific depth

CONCEPT: Linux Performance Analysis — USE Method
--------------------------------------------------
Brendan Gregg's USE Method: for every resource check Utilization, Saturation, Errors.

CPU:
  top / htop               → per-core CPU usage
  mpstat -P ALL 1          → per-CPU stats from sysstat
  sar -u 1 5               → CPU utilization history
  perf top                 → live kernel/userspace profiling (low overhead)
  perf stat -p <pid>       → hardware performance counters
  vmstat 1                 → CPU steal time (virtualization overhead)
  CPU steal > 5%           → noisy neighbor problem on cloud VMs

Memory:
  free -h                  → quick overview
  /proc/meminfo            → detail (Cached, Buffers, SwapCached, Dirty, etc.)
  vmstat 1                 → si/so = swap in/out (should be 0 in prod)
  slabtop                  → kernel slab cache
  smem -r                  → memory per process including shared memory

Disk I/O:
  iostat -x 1              → %util, await, r/s, w/s per device
    %util > 80%           → disk saturated
    await > 20ms          → high disk latency
  iotop -ao               → per-process I/O (like top for I/O)
  blktrace + blkparse     → extremely detailed block I/O tracing
  lsof +D /var            → open file handles in /var (find who's writing)

Network:
  sar -n DEV 1            → network interface stats (sysstat)
  nethogs                  → per-process bandwidth usage
  iftop                    → real-time bandwidth by connection
  nload                    → interface bandwidth graph
  ss -s                    → socket statistics summary
  /proc/net/dev            → raw interface counters

CONCEPT: Kernel Tuning via sysctl
-----------------------------------
Kernel parameters live in /proc/sys/ and are set via sysctl.

Networking (critical for high-traffic servers):
  net.core.somaxconn=65535         → max connection backlog
  net.ipv4.tcp_max_syn_backlog=65535
  net.core.netdev_max_backlog=5000
  net.ipv4.ip_local_port_range=1024 65535  → ephemeral port range
  net.ipv4.tcp_tw_reuse=1          → reuse TIME_WAIT sockets
  net.ipv4.tcp_fin_timeout=15      → reduce FIN_WAIT2 timeout
  net.core.rmem_max=134217728      → max receive socket buffer (128MB)
  net.core.wmem_max=134217728      → max send socket buffer
  net.ipv4.tcp_keepalive_time=300  → TCP keepalive interval
  net.ipv4.ip_forward=1            → enable IP forwarding (needed for containers)

Virtual memory:
  vm.swappiness=1
  vm.dirty_ratio=15                → % RAM that can be dirty before sync
  vm.dirty_background_ratio=5
  vm.overcommit_memory=1           → allow memory overcommit (needed for Redis)

File descriptors:
  fs.file-max=2097152              → system-wide max open files
  fs.nr_open=1048576               → per-process max open files

Apply immediately: sysctl -w net.core.somaxconn=65535
Apply persistently: echo "net.core.somaxconn=65535" > /etc/sysctl.d/99-network.conf
                    sysctl --system

CONCEPT: ulimit & Resource Limits
-----------------------------------
ulimit controls per-process resource limits.

  ulimit -n 65536         → max open file descriptors (per-process)
  ulimit -u 4096          → max user processes
  ulimit -s unlimited     → stack size
  ulimit -a               → show all current limits

Persistent limits via /etc/security/limits.conf:
  # /etc/security/limits.conf
  *    soft  nofile  65536
  *    hard  nofile  65536
  nginx soft  nofile  65536
  nginx hard  nofile  1048576

systemd services override: in [Service] section:
  LimitNOFILE=1048576
  LimitNPROC=65536

Real-world: "Too many open files" errors in Node.js/Java/nginx = ulimit too low.

CONCEPT: cron & crontab
------------------------
  crontab -e         → edit user crontab
  crontab -l         → list user crontab
  crontab -r         → remove all cron jobs (DANGEROUS)
  cat /etc/crontab   → system crontab (has username field)
  ls /etc/cron.d/    → additional cron job files

Cron format:
  ┌─── minute (0-59)
  │ ┌── hour (0-23)
  │ │ ┌─ day of month (1-31)
  │ │ │ ┌ month (1-12)
  │ │ │ │ ┌ day of week (0-6, Sun=0)
  * * * * * /path/to/command

  0 2 * * *        → daily at 2 AM
  */5 * * * *      → every 5 minutes
  0 9 * * 1-5      → weekdays at 9 AM
  @reboot          → run once at startup
  @daily           → equivalent to 0 0 * * *

Cron directories (no need to edit crontab):
  /etc/cron.hourly/
  /etc/cron.daily/
  /etc/cron.weekly/
  /etc/cron.monthly/

Best practices:
  - Redirect output: * * * * * /script.sh >> /var/log/myjob.log 2>&1
  - Use full paths in scripts
  - Test scripts manually before scheduling
  - For complex scheduling, prefer systemd timers (better logging + dependencies)

CONCEPT: User and Group Management Deep Dive
---------------------------------------------
  useradd -m -s /bin/bash -G docker,sudo username
  usermod -aG docker username   → add to group (note: -a is crucial)
  userdel -r username           → delete user + home dir
  groupadd groupname
  id username                   → show UID, GID, groups
  groups username
  su - username                 → switch user (full login shell)
  sudo -u username command       → run as user without switching

/etc/passwd format:
  username:x:UID:GID:comment:home:shell
  x = password is in /etc/shadow

/etc/shadow format:
  username:$6$salt$hash:lastchange:min:max:warn:inactive:expire
  $6$ = SHA-512 hash

/etc/sudoers — controlled by visudo:
  username ALL=(ALL:ALL) ALL          → full sudo
  %docker ALL=(ALL) NOPASSWD: ALL     → group, no password
  username ALL=(ALL) NOPASSWD: /bin/systemctl restart nginx
  Defaults timestamp_timeout=5        → sudo timeout in minutes

PAM (Pluggable Authentication Modules):
  /etc/pam.d/ → authentication rules per service
  pam_pwquality → password complexity
  pam_tally2 / pam_faillock → account lockout after failed attempts
  pam_limits → enforce ulimits

CONCEPT: SSH Deep Dive
-----------------------
Key-based authentication:
  ssh-keygen -t ed25519 -C "user@host" -f ~/.ssh/id_ed25519
  ssh-copy-id -i ~/.ssh/id_ed25519.pub user@server
  ~/.ssh/authorized_keys → server stores public keys (600 permissions)
  ~/.ssh/config → client connection profiles

~/.ssh/config:
  Host bastion
    HostName 10.0.0.5
    User ec2-user
    IdentityFile ~/.ssh/prod.pem

  Host prod-app
    HostName 10.0.1.10
    User ubuntu
    ProxyJump bastion
    IdentityFile ~/.ssh/prod.pem

/etc/ssh/sshd_config — server hardening:
  PermitRootLogin no
  PasswordAuthentication no
  PubkeyAuthentication yes
  AllowUsers deploy ci-runner
  MaxAuthTries 3
  ClientAliveInterval 300
  ClientAliveCountMax 2
  X11Forwarding no
  AllowTcpForwarding no   (unless needed for tunnels)
  Protocol 2

SSH tunneling:
  ssh -L 8080:localhost:80 user@remote    → local forward (access remote's 80 locally)
  ssh -R 9090:localhost:3000 user@remote  → remote forward (expose local on remote)
  ssh -D 1080 user@remote                → SOCKS5 proxy
  ssh -N -f -L 5432:db.internal:5432 bastion  → background tunnel to DB

SSH agent forwarding:
  ssh-add ~/.ssh/id_ed25519
  ssh -A user@bastion  → forward agent to bastion, then SSH to internal from there

CONCEPT: iptables & nftables — Firewall
-----------------------------------------
iptables chains:
  INPUT   → inbound to local process
  OUTPUT  → outbound from local process
  FORWARD → transit traffic (routed through)

iptables tables:
  filter → default; INPUT/OUTPUT/FORWARD
  nat    → PREROUTING/POSTROUTING/OUTPUT
  mangle → packet modification
  raw    → connection tracking bypass

iptables commands:
  iptables -L -n -v --line-numbers    → list rules with packet counts
  iptables -A INPUT -p tcp --dport 22 -j ACCEPT
  iptables -A INPUT -p tcp --dport 80 -j ACCEPT
  iptables -A INPUT -m state --state ESTABLISHED,RELATED -j ACCEPT
  iptables -P INPUT DROP              → default deny
  iptables -D INPUT 3                 → delete rule #3
  iptables-save > /etc/iptables/rules.v4
  iptables-restore < /etc/iptables/rules.v4

nftables (modern replacement for iptables):
  nft list ruleset
  nft add rule inet filter input tcp dport 22 accept
  nft add rule inet filter input tcp dport {80, 443} accept
  /etc/nftables.conf → persistent config

ufw (Ubuntu frontend for iptables):
  ufw enable
  ufw allow 22/tcp
  ufw allow from 10.0.0.0/8 to any port 5432
  ufw deny 23
  ufw status verbose

firewalld (RHEL frontend):
  firewall-cmd --permanent --add-service=https
  firewall-cmd --permanent --add-port=8080/tcp
  firewall-cmd --reload
  firewall-cmd --list-all

CONCEPT: LVM — Logical Volume Management
------------------------------------------
LVM provides flexible disk management with resizing, snapshots.

Hierarchy: Physical Volumes (PV) → Volume Group (VG) → Logical Volumes (LV)

  pvcreate /dev/sdb /dev/sdc              → initialize physical volumes
  vgcreate data_vg /dev/sdb /dev/sdc     → create volume group
  lvcreate -L 100G -n app_lv data_vg     → create logical volume
  mkfs.ext4 /dev/data_vg/app_lv          → format
  mount /dev/data_vg/app_lv /data        → mount

Resize (online):
  lvextend -L +50G /dev/data_vg/app_lv
  resize2fs /dev/data_vg/app_lv          → resize ext4 online
  xfs_growfs /data                       → resize xfs online

LVM Snapshot (for backups):
  lvcreate -s -L 10G -n app_snap /dev/data_vg/app_lv
  mount -o ro /dev/data_vg/app_snap /mnt/snap   → mount snapshot
  lvremove /dev/data_vg/app_snap                 → remove snapshot

Thin provisioning:
  lvcreate --type thin-pool -L 500G -n thin_pool data_vg
  lvcreate --type thin -V 1T --thinpool thin_pool -n app_lv data_vg
  Allocate more virtual space than physical (overprovisioning).

HANDS-ON TASKS (Advanced):
  1. Parse nginx access logs with awk to find top 10 slowest endpoints
  2. Profile a process with perf stat; interpret output
  3. Tune sysctl for high-connection-count (implement TCP tuning)
  4. Create an LVM setup with a snapshot; simulate backup workflow
  5. Harden sshd_config; set up key-only auth
  6. Write a sudoers rule for a deploy user with limited command access
  7. Set up a complete iptables ruleset with default deny
  8. Use strace to trace a failing binary and identify the failing syscall
  9. Configure systemd timer as cron replacement with proper logging

REAL-WORLD SCENARIO:
  "Node.js app returning EMFILE (too many open files) in production."
  Diagnosis:
    1. lsof -p <pid> | wc -l    → count open FDs
    2. cat /proc/<pid>/limits   → see current limits
    3. ulimit -n                 → current shell limit
  Fix:
    1. Edit /etc/security/limits.conf → increase nofile for app user
    2. In systemd unit: LimitNOFILE=1048576
    3. systemctl daemon-reload && systemctl restart app
    4. Verify: cat /proc/<new_pid>/limits | grep "open files"

================================================================================
4. EXPERT LEVEL — Production-Grade Knowledge
================================================================================

CONCEPT: Linux Namespaces — Foundation of Containers
------------------------------------------------------
Namespaces provide isolation for system resources. This is the core technology
behind Docker and Kubernetes.

  Type         | Isolates
  -------------|------------------------------------------
  pid          | Process IDs (container's PID 1 ≠ host PID 1)
  net          | Network interfaces, routes, iptables rules
  mnt          | Mount points (filesystem views)
  uts          | Hostname and domain name
  ipc          | System V IPC, POSIX message queues
  user         | User and group IDs (UID mapping)
  cgroup       | cgroup root (nested cgroup hierarchies)
  time         | System clocks (Linux 5.6+)

  lsns                               → list all namespaces
  ls -la /proc/1/ns/                → namespace links for PID 1
  nsenter -t <pid> -n ip addr       → enter network namespace of process
  unshare --pid --mount-proc bash   → create new PID namespace

CONCEPT: cgroups (Control Groups)
-----------------------------------
cgroups limit and account for resource usage of process groups.
Docker/Kubernetes use cgroups to enforce CPU/memory limits.

cgroups v1 (legacy):
  /sys/fs/cgroup/memory/
  /sys/fs/cgroup/cpu/
  /sys/fs/cgroup/blkio/

cgroups v2 (unified hierarchy, default on modern systems):
  /sys/fs/cgroup/<group_name>/
    cgroup.procs     → list of PIDs in this cgroup
    memory.max       → memory hard limit
    memory.current   → current memory usage
    cpu.max          → CPU quota/period
    cpu.stat         → CPU usage statistics

  # Create cgroup and limit memory
  mkdir /sys/fs/cgroup/myapp
  echo 512M > /sys/fs/cgroup/myapp/memory.max
  echo $$ > /sys/fs/cgroup/myapp/cgroup.procs

  systemd unit equivalents:
    MemoryMax=512M
    CPUQuota=200%    → 2 full CPUs
    BlockIOWeight=100

CONCEPT: seccomp & capabilities
---------------------------------
Linux capabilities: root's privileges are split into ~40 capabilities.

Key capabilities:
  CAP_NET_ADMIN     → configure network interfaces, routes, iptables
  CAP_NET_BIND_SERVICE → bind to ports < 1024
  CAP_SYS_ADMIN     → broad admin (nearly root; avoid in containers)
  CAP_CHOWN         → change file ownership
  CAP_KILL          → send signals to processes owned by others
  CAP_SYS_PTRACE    → ptrace, strace (needed for debuggers)
  CAP_IPC_LOCK      → lock memory (Redis, Elasticsearch need this)

  getcap /usr/bin/ping           → see file capabilities
  setcap cap_net_bind_service+ep /usr/bin/node → grant capability to binary
  capsh --print                  → show current process capabilities

seccomp (Secure Computing Mode):
  Filters syscalls a process can make. If a filtered syscall is attempted,
  the process is killed with SIGSYS.
  Docker applies a default seccomp profile that blocks ~44 syscalls.
  Kubernetes pods can use seccomp profiles via securityContext.

CONCEPT: Advanced Networking — Bonding, VLANs, Bridges
---------------------------------------------------------
Network Bonding (NIC teaming):
  Combines multiple NICs for redundancy or bandwidth.
  Modes:
    mode=0 (balance-rr)  → round-robin; no redundancy from single switch
    mode=1 (active-backup) → only one NIC active; failover
    mode=4 (802.3ad LACP) → standard link aggregation; requires switch config
    mode=6 (balance-alb)  → adaptive load balancing; no switch changes needed

  /etc/netplan/ (Ubuntu) or /etc/systemd/network/ (systemd-networkd)

VLANs:
  Virtual LANs segment network at layer 2.
  ip link add link eth0 name eth0.100 type vlan id 100
  ip addr add 192.168.100.1/24 dev eth0.100
  ip link set eth0.100 up

Network Bridges (used by VMs and containers):
  ip link add br0 type bridge
  ip link set eth0 master br0
  ip addr add 192.168.1.1/24 dev br0
  ip link set br0 up
  Used by KVM, Docker (docker0), and Kubernetes (cbr0).

CONCEPT: Advanced Storage — NFS, iSCSI
----------------------------------------
NFS (Network Filesystem):
  Server:
    apt install nfs-kernel-server
    echo "/data 10.0.0.0/8(rw,sync,no_subtree_check,no_root_squash)" >> /etc/exports
    exportfs -ra
    systemctl restart nfs-server

  Client:
    mount -t nfs server:/data /mnt/data
    # fstab: server:/data  /mnt/data  nfs  defaults,_netdev  0 0

  Performance options:
    rsize=65536,wsize=65536   → read/write block size
    hard,intr                 → hard mount with interrupt on signal
    noatime                   → don't update access time

iSCSI (block-level storage over TCP):
  Target (server): targetcli
  Initiator (client): open-iscsi
    iscsiadm -m discovery -t sendtargets -p 10.0.0.10
    iscsiadm -m node -l
  After login, device appears as /dev/sdX — format and mount normally.

CONCEPT: System Boot Process Deep Dive
----------------------------------------
  1. BIOS/UEFI
      ├── Power-On Self Test (POST)
      ├── Locate bootable device
      └── Load bootloader (GRUB2) from MBR or ESP partition

  2. GRUB2
      ├── /boot/grub2/grub.cfg → menu entries
      ├── Load kernel + initramfs into memory
      └── Pass kernel parameters (e.g., quiet splash ro root=/dev/sda1)

  3. Kernel Initialization
      ├── Decompress kernel
      ├── Initialize CPU, memory, devices
      ├── Mount initramfs (temporary root FS with drivers)
      ├── Mount real root filesystem
      └── Execute /sbin/init (which is systemd in modern systems)

  4. systemd (PID 1)
      ├── Read default.target (usually multi-user.target)
      ├── Resolve unit dependencies
      ├── Activate units in parallel (respecting After/Requires/Wants)
      └── Boot completes when target is reached

  Kernel parameters (via GRUB or /etc/default/grub):
    systemd.unit=rescue.target    → boot into rescue mode
    rd.break                      → break into initramfs shell (password reset)
    init=/bin/bash                → boot directly to bash (break-glass)

  systemctl get-default           → show default target
  systemctl set-default multi-user.target

CONCEPT: SELinux & AppArmor — Mandatory Access Control
--------------------------------------------------------
DAC (Discretionary Access Control) = traditional Unix permissions.
MAC (Mandatory Access Control) = policy enforced by kernel, overrides DAC.

SELinux (RHEL/CentOS/Fedora):
  Modes: Enforcing (policies enforced), Permissive (logs only), Disabled
  getenforce
  setenforce 0        → set Permissive (temporary)
  /etc/selinux/config → persistent mode setting (SELINUX=enforcing)

  Every file/process has a LABEL (context): user:role:type:level
  ls -Z file          → show SELinux context
  ps -eZ | grep nginx → process context
  restorecon -Rv /var/www/html → restore default context after moving files

  Troubleshooting denials:
    ausearch -c 'nginx' --raw | audit2allow -M nginx_custom
    semodule -i nginx_custom.pp    → install policy module
    sealert -a /var/log/audit/audit.log → human-readable denial messages

AppArmor (Ubuntu/Debian):
  Profiles in /etc/apparmor.d/
  aa-status              → show profiles and status
  aa-complain /path/bin  → set to complain mode (log, don't enforce)
  aa-enforce /path/bin   → enforce mode
  apparmor_parser -r /etc/apparmor.d/nginx → reload profile

CONCEPT: Observability Tooling — Linux Performance Deep Dive
--------------------------------------------------------------
60-second Linux Analysis (Brendan Gregg):
  uptime               → load averages (1, 5, 15 min)
  dmesg -T | tail -20  → kernel errors
  vmstat 1             → virtual memory stats
  mpstat -P ALL 1      → CPU per-core
  pidstat 1            → per-process CPU
  iostat -xz 1        → disk I/O
  free -m              → memory
  sar -n DEV 1         → network
  sar -n TCP,ETCP 1    → TCP stats
  top                  → overview

Advanced tools:
  perf record -g -p <pid> sleep 10
  perf report → flame graph source data
  flamegraph.pl out.folded > flame.svg  → CPU flame graph

  bpftrace -e 'tracepoint:syscalls:sys_enter_read { @[comm] = count(); }'
  bcc tools: execsnoop, opensnoop, biolatency, tcpconnect, runqlat

  strace -c -p <pid>   → syscall frequency summary
  ltrace -p <pid>      → library call trace
  lsof -p <pid>        → open files, sockets
  /proc/<pid>/fd/      → actual FD symlinks
  /proc/<pid>/maps     → memory map
  /proc/<pid>/status   → process status including memory

CONCEPT: Disaster Recovery & System Rescue
-------------------------------------------
Entering rescue/emergency mode:
  systemctl rescue          → drops to rescue.target (minimal services)
  systemctl emergency       → emergency.target (root FS only, read-only)

Password reset (single user):
  1. Reboot → GRUB → press 'e' on boot entry
  2. Find linux line, add: rd.break enforcing=0
  3. Ctrl+X to boot
  4. mount -o remount,rw /sysroot
  5. chroot /sysroot
  6. passwd root
  7. touch /.autorelabel (SELinux relabel on next boot)
  8. exit && reboot

File recovery:
  - Deleted file still open: ls -la /proc/<pid>/fd/ → recover via /proc
  - ext4: extundelete --restore-all /dev/sdb1
  - testdisk → partition/file recovery tool
  - Photorec → file carving by signature

Filesystem check and repair:
  umount /dev/sdb1
  fsck.ext4 -y /dev/sdb1     → auto-repair
  xfs_repair /dev/sdc1
  Never run fsck on a mounted filesystem!

CONCEPT: Automation — cloud-init & User Data
---------------------------------------------
cloud-init runs at first boot on cloud VMs to configure the instance.
  /var/lib/cloud/instance/user-data.txt → stored user data
  /var/log/cloud-init.log               → execution log

#cloud-config example:
  #cloud-config
  users:
    - name: deploy
      groups: docker, sudo
      shell: /bin/bash
      ssh_authorized_keys:
        - ssh-ed25519 AAAA... user@laptop
  packages:
    - nginx
    - docker.io
  runcmd:
    - systemctl enable --now nginx
  write_files:
    - path: /etc/nginx/conf.d/app.conf
      content: |
        server { listen 80; ... }

HANDS-ON TASKS (Expert):
  1. Inspect namespaces and cgroups of a running Docker container
  2. Apply a custom seccomp profile to a containerized app
  3. Create an LVM thin pool with overprovisioning
  4. Troubleshoot an SELinux denial and write a custom policy module
  5. Do a full password recovery in a VM (break into initramfs)
  6. Tune sysctl for production: network, VM, file descriptors
  7. Use bpftrace to trace all open() calls system-wide
  8. Profile a slow process with perf + generate a CPU flame graph
  9. Set up NFS with proper performance tuning options
  10. Write a cloud-init user-data that fully provisions a production server

REAL-WORLD SCENARIOS:
  SCENARIO A — Production server unresponsive, high load:
    1. ssh in (if possible) or use cloud console
    2. uptime → load average (if > # CPUs: saturated)
    3. dmesg -T | tail → OOM kills? kernel errors?
    4. ps aux --sort=-%cpu | head -20 → CPU hogs
    5. ps aux --sort=-%mem | head -20 → memory hogs
    6. iostat -x 1 → if %util 100%: disk I/O bottleneck
    7. ss -s → thousands of CLOSE_WAIT/TIME_WAIT? → app bug or TCP misconfiguration
    8. If OOM: check /var/log/syslog for "oom-kill" → increase memory or fix leak
    Action: kill -9 offending process, document, schedule postmortem.

  SCENARIO B — Disk Full at 3 AM:
    1. df -h → identify full partition
    2. du -sh /var/log/* | sort -rh | head -10 → largest log dirs
    3. lsof +L1 → deleted files still held open (taking space)
    4. kill -HUP <app_pid>  → close and reopen log files
    5. find /var/log -name "*.log" -mtime +7 -exec gzip {} \;
    6. Long-term: logrotate + set log retention policy
    7. Add disk space alert to monitoring (> 80% → alert)

  SCENARIO C — Kubernetes node NotReady (Linux perspective):
    1. SSH into node
    2. systemctl status kubelet → is kubelet running?
    3. journalctl -u kubelet -n 100 → what errors?
    4. df -h → is /var/lib/kubelet or /var/lib/docker full?
    5. free -h → OOM?
    6. dmesg | grep -i oom
    7. iptables -L → are CNI rules intact?
    8. systemctl status containerd
    Fix: restart kubelet/containerd, free disk, address OOM.

################################################################################
#                  PHASE 3: BASH SCRIPTING (MASTER LEVEL)                     #
################################################################################

================================================================================
1. FOUNDATION — Basic Concepts
================================================================================

CONCEPT: Shell Types & Script Execution
-----------------------------------------
Login shell    → reads /etc/profile, ~/.bash_profile, ~/.bashrc
Interactive    → reads ~/.bashrc
Non-interactive → scripts; reads BASH_ENV if set
sh vs bash     → sh is POSIX; bash is superset; #!/bin/bash for bash features

Script execution modes:
  chmod +x script.sh && ./script.sh  → subshell; inherits environment
  bash script.sh                     → explicit interpreter; no need for shebang
  source script.sh / . script.sh     → runs in CURRENT shell (variables persist)
  bash -x script.sh                  → trace execution (print each command)
  bash -n script.sh                  → syntax check only (no execution)
  bash -e script.sh                  → exit on first error
  bash -u script.sh                  → exit on undefined variable
  bash -o pipefail                   → pipe fails if any command in pipe fails

Shebang line:
  #!/usr/bin/env bash    → portable; finds bash in PATH (preferred)
  #!/bin/bash            → hardcoded path; faster but less portable
  #!/usr/bin/env -S bash -euo pipefail  → pass options via env (GNU env only)

CONCEPT: Variables & Data Types
---------------------------------
Bash is untyped by default. Everything is a string unless declared otherwise.

  name="John"            → assignment (NO spaces around =)
  echo $name             → use variable
  echo "${name}"         → use variable (always prefer braces)
  readonly PI=3.14159    → immutable variable
  unset name             → delete variable
  local var="inside"     → local to function (only inside functions)

  declare -i count=0     → integer
  declare -r CONST="val" → readonly
  declare -a arr=()      → indexed array
  declare -A map=()      → associative array (hash map)
  declare -x VAR="val"   → export (same as export VAR=val)
  declare -l str="HI"    → lowercase conversion
  declare -u str="hi"    → uppercase conversion

Environment variables:
  export VAR=value       → visible to child processes
  printenv               → print all environment variables
  env                    → same + can set env for a single command
  env -i bash            → clean environment (no inherited vars)

Special variables:
  $0     → script name
  $1-$9  → positional parameters
  $@     → all arguments (array-like, each quoted separately)
  $*     → all arguments (single string when quoted)
  $#     → number of arguments
  $?     → exit code of last command
  $$     → PID of current shell
  $!     → PID of last background process
  $-     → current shell options (himBH etc.)
  $_     → last argument of previous command
  $LINENO → current line number
  $FUNCNAME → current function name

CONCEPT: Quoting Rules (Critical)
-----------------------------------
Single quotes: '...'   → literal; NO expansion, NO escape
Double quotes: "..."   → variable/command expansion; preserves whitespace
Backticks: `...`       → command substitution (legacy; use $(...))
$(...) → command substitution (modern; nestable)
$((...)) → arithmetic expansion

  name="world"
  echo 'Hello $name'      → Hello $name
  echo "Hello $name"      → Hello world
  echo "Today: $(date)"   → Today: Thu Jan 1 ...
  echo $((2 + 3))          → 5

Word splitting: unquoted variables are split on IFS (default: space/tab/newline)
  files=$(ls /tmp)
  for f in $files    → WRONG: breaks on spaces in filenames
  for f in "$files"  → WRONG: treats entire output as one item
  while IFS= read -r f; do ...; done < <(ls /tmp)  → CORRECT

Always quote variables: "$var", "$@", "${array[@]}"

CONCEPT: Conditionals
----------------------
  if [ condition ]; then ... fi        → POSIX test
  if [[ condition ]]; then ... fi      → bash extended test (preferred)
  if (( arithmetic )); then ... fi     → arithmetic condition

[ ] vs [[ ]]:
  [[ ]] supports: && || =~ < > (no need to escape)
  [[ ]] prevents word splitting and globbing on variables
  [[ ]] supports regex: [[ "$str" =~ ^[0-9]+$ ]]

Test operators:
  File tests:
    -e file   → exists
    -f file   → regular file
    -d dir    → directory
    -r/-w/-x  → readable/writable/executable
    -s file   → exists and not empty
    -L file   → symbolic link
    -nt/-ot   → newer/older than

  String tests:
    -z str    → zero length (empty)
    -n str    → non-zero length
    str1 = str2  → equal (in [ ])
    str1 == str2 → equal (in [[ ]])
    str1 != str2 → not equal
    str1 < str2  → lexicographic less than (in [[ ]])
    [[ str =~ regex ]]  → regex match

  Integer tests:
    -eq -ne -lt -le -gt -ge
    Or use (( n1 > n2 ))

  Logical:
    [ c1 ] && [ c2 ]  → AND
    [[ c1 && c2 ]]    → AND (bash)
    [ c1 ] || [ c2 ]  → OR
    ! condition        → NOT

case statement:
  case "$var" in
    start|begin)  echo "starting" ;;
    stop|end)     echo "stopping" ;;
    [0-9]*)       echo "starts with digit" ;;
    *)            echo "default" ;;
  esac

CONCEPT: Loops
---------------
  for i in 1 2 3; do echo $i; done
  for i in {1..10}; do echo $i; done
  for i in {0..100..5}; do echo $i; done      → step by 5
  for (( i=0; i<10; i++ )); do echo $i; done  → C-style

  for file in /etc/*.conf; do
    echo "Processing $file"
  done

  while [ condition ]; do ... done
  until [ condition ]; do ... done   → loop until condition is true

  while IFS= read -r line; do
    echo "$line"
  done < file.txt

  while IFS=: read -r user _ uid gid _ home shell; do
    echo "$user → $home"
  done < /etc/passwd

  break [n]     → exit n levels of loop
  continue [n]  → skip to next iteration of n-th enclosing loop

HANDS-ON TASKS (Foundation):
  1. Write a script that takes a filename argument, checks it exists, and prints line count
  2. Use declare -A to create a hashmap of server → IP
  3. Practice all quoting modes; observe word splitting behavior
  4. Rewrite a for loop using while + read from a file
  5. Write a case statement that acts as a mini CLI dispatcher

================================================================================
2. CORE CONCEPTS — Intermediate
================================================================================

CONCEPT: Functions
-------------------
  myfunc() {
    local arg1="$1"
    local arg2="$2"
    echo "Args: $arg1, $arg2"
    return 0   → exit code (0-255 only; not for returning data)
  }
  myfunc "hello" "world"

  # Return data via stdout (command substitution captures it):
  get_timestamp() {
    date +%Y%m%d_%H%M%S
  }
  ts=$(get_timestamp)

  # Return data via nameref (bash 4.3+):
  get_ip() {
    local -n result=$1   → nameref: result is an alias for the variable named by $1
    result="192.168.1.1"
  }
  get_ip my_ip
  echo "$my_ip"

Function best practices:
  - Always declare variables with local to prevent global scope pollution
  - Return 0 for success, non-zero for failure (mimics command exit codes)
  - Use $? to check return value
  - Put all functions at top of script (or in a sourced library file)

CONCEPT: Arrays
----------------
Indexed arrays:
  arr=("apple" "banana" "cherry")
  arr[0]="apple"
  arr+=("date")                  → append
  echo "${arr[0]}"               → first element
  echo "${arr[-1]}"              → last element (bash 4.2+)
  echo "${arr[@]}"               → all elements
  echo "${#arr[@]}"              → array length
  echo "${!arr[@]}"              → all indices
  unset arr[1]                   → delete element (leaves gap)
  arr=("${arr[@]:1:3}")          → slice: elements 1,2,3

  # Iterate:
  for item in "${arr[@]}"; do echo "$item"; done

Associative arrays (dictionaries):
  declare -A config
  config[host]="db.internal"
  config[port]="5432"
  config[name]="mydb"

  for key in "${!config[@]}"; do
    echo "$key = ${config[$key]}"
  done

  [[ -v config[host] ]]  → check if key exists

CONCEPT: String Manipulation
------------------------------
Parameter expansion — no external commands needed:

  str="Hello, World!"

  ${str,,}              → lowercase: hello, world!
  ${str^^}              → uppercase: HELLO, WORLD!
  ${#str}               → length: 13
  ${str:7:5}            → substring: World
  ${str:(-6)}           → from end: orld!
  ${str/World/Bash}     → replace first: Hello, Bash!
  ${str//l/L}           → replace all: HeLLo, WorLd!
  ${str#Hello, }        → remove prefix (shortest match)
  ${str##*,}            → remove prefix (longest match)
  ${str%!}              → remove suffix (shortest match)
  ${str%%,*}            → remove suffix (longest match)

  # Default values:
  ${var:-default}       → use default if unset or empty
  ${var:=default}       → assign default if unset or empty
  ${var:+alternate}     → use alternate if set
  ${var:?error message} → error and exit if unset or empty

  # Filename manipulation:
  path="/home/user/file.txt"
  ${path##*/}           → file.txt (basename)
  ${path%/*}            → /home/user (dirname)
  ${path##*.}           → txt (extension)
  ${path%.*}            → /home/user/file (no extension)

  # Indirect expansion:
  varname="PATH"
  echo "${!varname}"    → expands to value of PATH

CONCEPT: Input / Output Redirection
--------------------------------------
  command > file          → stdout to file (truncate)
  command >> file         → stdout to file (append)
  command 2> file         → stderr to file
  command 2>&1            → redirect stderr to stdout
  command > file 2>&1     → both stdout and stderr to file
  command &> file         → shorthand for above (bash only)
  command > /dev/null 2>&1 → discard all output
  command < file          → stdin from file
  command << EOF ... EOF  → here-document (inline stdin)
  command <<< "string"    → here-string (single string as stdin)

  File descriptor manipulation:
  exec 3> output.log      → open FD 3 for writing
  echo "msg" >&3          → write to FD 3
  exec 3>&-               → close FD 3
  exec 4< input.txt       → open FD 4 for reading
  read line <&4           → read from FD 4

Process substitution:
  diff <(sort file1) <(sort file2)   → treat command output as file
  cat <(echo "header") file.txt      → prepend header
  tee >(gzip > out.gz) >(wc -l)     → tee to multiple processes

Pipes:
  cmd1 | cmd2 | cmd3
  Exit code is from last command. Use pipefail:
    set -o pipefail
  Named pipes (FIFO):
    mkfifo /tmp/mypipe
    cmd1 > /tmp/mypipe &
    cmd2 < /tmp/mypipe

CONCEPT: Error Handling — Production Patterns
----------------------------------------------
The strict mode header for all production scripts:
  #!/usr/bin/env bash
  set -euo pipefail
  IFS=$'\n\t'

  set -e     → exit on error
  set -u     → treat unset variables as errors
  set -o pipefail → pipe fails if any command fails
  IFS=$'\n\t' → safer word splitting (no space splitting)

trap for cleanup and error handling:
  cleanup() {
    echo "Cleaning up..."
    rm -f "$tmpfile"
    # always runs, even on error
  }
  trap cleanup EXIT

  on_error() {
    echo "ERROR on line $LINENO" >&2
    echo "Failed command: $BASH_COMMAND" >&2
  }
  trap on_error ERR

  # Handle specific signals:
  trap 'echo "Interrupted"; exit 1' INT TERM

  # Temporarily disable errexit for a command that may fail:
  set +e
  command_that_might_fail
  exit_code=$?
  set -e
  # Or use: command_that_might_fail || true

Error logging pattern:
  readonly LOG_FILE="/var/log/myscript.log"
  readonly SCRIPT_NAME=$(basename "$0")

  log() {
    local level="$1"; shift
    echo "[$(date +'%Y-%m-%dT%H:%M:%S%z')] [$SCRIPT_NAME] [$level] $*" | tee -a "$LOG_FILE"
  }
  log_info()  { log "INFO"  "$@"; }
  log_warn()  { log "WARN"  "$@" >&2; }
  log_error() { log "ERROR" "$@" >&2; }
  die()       { log_error "$@"; exit 1; }

  [[ -f "$config" ]] || die "Config file not found: $config"

CONCEPT: Temporary Files & Secure Practices
---------------------------------------------
  tmpfile=$(mktemp)               → /tmp/tmp.XXXXXXXXXX
  tmpdir=$(mktemp -d)             → temp directory
  tmpfile=$(mktemp /tmp/myapp.XXXXXX)
  trap 'rm -f "$tmpfile"' EXIT   → always clean up

  NEVER: tmpfile="/tmp/$$"       → predictable; race condition (symlink attack)
  ALWAYS: mktemp                 → random suffix; atomic creation

Sensitive data:
  # Don't use: echo "password=$pass" | cmd  → visible in ps aux
  # Use: cmd <<< "$pass"         → heredoc doesn't appear in process list
  # Or: printf '%s\n' "$pass" | cmd
  # Or: set env var: export PGPASSWORD="$pass"

  # Don't store secrets in scripts → use Vault, AWS SSM, env vars from CI

CONCEPT: Script Arguments — getopts
--------------------------------------
  usage() {
    cat <<EOF
  Usage: $(basename "$0") [-h] [-v] [-f FILE] [-n NAME] ARGUMENT
    -h         show this help
    -v         verbose mode
    -f FILE    input file
    -n NAME    name to use
  EOF
    exit 1
  }

  verbose=false
  file=""
  name=""

  while getopts ":hvf:n:" opt; do
    case "$opt" in
      h) usage ;;
      v) verbose=true ;;
      f) file="$OPTARG" ;;
      n) name="$OPTARG" ;;
      :) die "Option -$OPTARG requires an argument" ;;
      \?) die "Unknown option: -$OPTARG" ;;
    esac
  done
  shift $((OPTIND - 1))  → remaining non-option arguments

  [[ $# -lt 1 ]] && usage

For long options: use getopt (external) or manual parsing:
  for arg in "$@"; do
    case "$arg" in
      --file=*) file="${arg#*=}" ;;
      --verbose) verbose=true ;;
    esac
  done

HANDS-ON TASKS (Core):
  1. Build a logging library with log levels; source it in multiple scripts
  2. Write a script using all string manipulation operators (no sed/awk)
  3. Implement getopts for a deployment script with -e (env), -v (verbose), -d (dry-run)
  4. Use trap to ensure cleanup on EXIT, ERR, INT, TERM
  5. Manipulate arrays: filter, transform, deduplicate without external tools
  6. Use process substitution to diff outputs of two commands
