################################################################################
#          PHASE 5: CONTAINERS — DOCKER, DOCKER COMPOSE, DOCKER SWARM         #
################################################################################

================================================================================
1. FOUNDATION — Basic Concepts
================================================================================

CONCEPT: Container vs Virtual Machine
---------------------------------------
Virtual Machine:
  Full OS, hypervisor, hardware virtualization
  Boot time: minutes; size: GBs; strong isolation

Container:
  Shares host kernel; isolated via namespaces + cgroups
  Boot time: milliseconds; size: MBs; process-level isolation
  NOT a VM — it's a process with an isolated view of the OS

Container runtime stack:
  containerd → runc → Linux kernel (namespaces + cgroups)
  Docker → containerd → runc (Docker is a layer on top of containerd)

CONCEPT: Docker Architecture
------------------------------
  Docker CLI  →  Docker Daemon (dockerd)  →  containerd  →  runc
                       ↓
             Docker Hub / Registry

  dockerd     → background daemon; manages images, containers, networks, volumes
  containerd  → industry-standard container runtime (CNCF project)
  runc        → OCI runtime; creates/starts containers (low-level)
  Docker CLI  → client talking to dockerd via Unix socket (/var/run/docker.sock)
  REST API    → dockerd exposes API; CLI wraps it

CONCEPT: Images & Layers
--------------------------
A Docker image is a read-only stack of layers.
Each instruction in a Dockerfile creates a new layer (or uses cache).
Layers are content-addressed (SHA256) and shared across images.

  Base image layer    (FROM ubuntu:22.04)
  ↓
  Package layer       (RUN apt-get install...)
  ↓
  App code layer      (COPY . .)
  ↓
  Config layer        (ENV, EXPOSE)
  ↓
  Container writable layer  (added at runtime; lost when container removed)

  docker image inspect myapp:v1    → see all layers, config, env
  docker history myapp:v1          → layer history with sizes
  docker image ls                  → list images
  docker image rm myapp:v1         → remove image
  docker image prune -a            → remove all unused images

CONCEPT: Essential Docker Commands
------------------------------------
  Lifecycle:
    docker run -d -p 8080:80 --name webapp nginx   → detached, port-mapped
    docker start / stop / restart / rm <name>
    docker rm -f <name>            → force remove running container
    docker ps -a                   → all containers (running + stopped)
    docker ps --format "table {{.Names}}\t{{.Status}}\t{{.Ports}}"

  Interaction:
    docker exec -it webapp bash    → exec into running container
    docker exec webapp cat /etc/nginx/nginx.conf
    docker attach webapp           → attach to STDIN/STDOUT (ctrl+p+q detach)
    docker logs webapp             → stdout/stderr logs
    docker logs -f --tail 100 webapp → follow last 100 lines
    docker top webapp              → processes inside container

  Inspection:
    docker inspect webapp          → full JSON config
    docker inspect -f '{{.NetworkSettings.IPAddress}}' webapp  → filter with Go template
    docker stats                   → real-time CPU/memory/network/disk

  Cleanup:
    docker system prune            → remove stopped containers, dangling images
    docker system prune -a         → remove all unused resources
    docker system df               → show disk usage

HANDS-ON TASKS (Foundation):
  1. Run nginx, override the default page with a bind mount
  2. Inspect image layers with docker history and docker image inspect
  3. Exec into a running container; install tools; commit to new image
  4. Use docker stats to observe CPU/memory under load
  5. Clean the entire Docker environment with docker system prune -a

================================================================================
2. CORE CONCEPTS — Intermediate
================================================================================

CONCEPT: Dockerfile — Complete Reference
------------------------------------------
  FROM ubuntu:22.04                       → base image (always pin version)
  FROM ubuntu:22.04 AS builder            → named stage for multi-stage builds

  ARG BUILD_VERSION=1.0                   → build-time variable (not in final image)
  ENV APP_PORT=8080                       → runtime environment variable
  ENV GO_VERSION=1.21 \
      CGO_ENABLED=0

  WORKDIR /app                            → set working directory (creates if absent)

  COPY --chown=appuser:appgroup src/ ./   → copy with ownership
  COPY --from=builder /build/bin /usr/local/bin/  → copy from build stage

  ADD https://example.com/file.tar.gz /tmp/  → ADD can fetch URLs and auto-extract tars
                                              → Prefer COPY for local files (more explicit)

  RUN apt-get update && apt-get install -y \
        curl \
        ca-certificates \
      && rm -rf /var/lib/apt/lists/*      → clean cache in same layer (critical for size)

  USER appuser                            → run as non-root (security requirement)
  USER 1001:1001                         → numeric UID:GID (for k8s runAsUser)

  EXPOSE 8080                             → documentation only; doesn't publish port
  VOLUME ["/data"]                        → creates mount point; marks as external data

  ENTRYPOINT ["./app"]                   → executable; NOT overridden by docker run args
  CMD ["--port", "8080"]                 → default args to ENTRYPOINT; overridden by docker run

  ENTRYPOINT vs CMD:
    ENTRYPOINT ["./app"] + CMD ["--port","8080"]
      docker run myapp                  → ./app --port 8080
      docker run myapp --port 9090      → ./app --port 9090
      docker run --entrypoint bash myapp → bash (override entrypoint)

  HEALTHCHECK --interval=30s --timeout=10s --retries=3 \
    CMD curl -f http://localhost:8080/health || exit 1

  LABEL org.opencontainers.image.source="https://github.com/org/repo"
  LABEL org.opencontainers.image.version="1.2.3"
  LABEL org.opencontainers.image.revision="abc1234"

CONCEPT: Multi-Stage Builds
-----------------------------
Build a small production image by using a full builder image and copying
only the final binary into a minimal runtime image.

  # ── Stage 1: Build ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
  FROM golang:1.21-alpine AS builder

  WORKDIR /build
  COPY go.mod go.sum ./
  RUN go mod download                     → separate layer: cache deps independently

  COPY . .
  RUN CGO_ENABLED=0 GOOS=linux go build -ldflags="-s -w" -o server ./cmd/server

  # ── Stage 2: Test (optional) ─────────────────────────────────────────────────
  FROM builder AS tester
  RUN go test ./...

  # ── Stage 3: Production ──────────────────────────────────────────────────────
  FROM gcr.io/distroless/static-debian12  → no shell, no package manager (~2MB)

  COPY --from=builder /build/server /server
  USER nonroot:nonroot
  EXPOSE 8080
  ENTRYPOINT ["/server"]

  Result: builder ~400MB → production image ~10MB
  Use: scratch (empty), alpine (~5MB), distroless (no shell; hardened)

  Target a specific stage:
  docker build --target tester -t myapp:test .
  docker build --target builder -t myapp:dev .

CONCEPT: .dockerignore
-----------------------
  .git/
  .gitignore
  Dockerfile*
  docker-compose*
  node_modules/
  __pycache__/
  *.pyc
  .env*
  *.log
  .DS_Store
  tests/
  docs/
  README.md
  coverage/

  Critical: without .dockerignore, COPY . . sends entire build context (including
  .git, node_modules) to Docker daemon — massively increases build time and image size.

CONCEPT: Docker Networking
---------------------------
Network drivers:
  bridge    → default; isolated network on single host; containers communicate by name
  host      → container shares host network stack (no NAT; better performance)
  none      → no network (fully isolated)
  overlay   → multi-host networking (used by Docker Swarm)
  macvlan   → container gets its own MAC/IP on the LAN (appears as physical device)

  docker network create mynet                     → user-defined bridge
  docker network create --driver overlay swarmnet → overlay for Swarm
  docker network ls
  docker network inspect mynet
  docker network connect mynet container1

User-defined bridge vs default bridge:
  Default bridge: containers communicate by IP only
  User-defined bridge: automatic DNS resolution by container name
    container1 can reach container2 at http://container2:8080

  docker run -d --network mynet --name db postgres
  docker run -d --network mynet --name api myapp  → api reaches db at db:5432

Port publishing:
  -p 8080:80            → host:container (all interfaces)
  -p 127.0.0.1:8080:80  → bind to localhost only (more secure)
  -p 8080:80/udp        → UDP port
  -P                    → publish all EXPOSED ports to random host ports

CONCEPT: Docker Volumes
------------------------
Volume types:
  Named volume  → docker volume create; managed by Docker; persists after container removal
  Bind mount    → maps host path into container; use for dev, config injection
  tmpfs         → in-memory; not persisted; sensitive data

  docker volume create pgdata
  docker run -v pgdata:/var/lib/postgresql/data postgres    → named volume
  docker run -v /host/path:/container/path:ro nginx        → bind mount (read-only)
  docker run --tmpfs /tmp:rw,noexec,size=128m app          → tmpfs

  docker volume ls
  docker volume inspect pgdata
  docker volume prune               → remove all unused volumes (data loss risk!)

Volume drivers (plugins):
  local       → default; on host filesystem
  nfs         → NFS share
  rexray      → cloud storage (EBS, EFS, S3)
  portworx    → distributed storage

Backup a volume:
  docker run --rm \
    -v pgdata:/source:ro \
    -v $(pwd):/backup \
    alpine tar czf /backup/pgdata.tar.gz -C /source .

CONCEPT: Container Security Basics
------------------------------------
  Never run as root:
    Dockerfile: USER 1001:1001
    docker run --user 1001:1001 myimage

  Read-only filesystem:
    docker run --read-only myimage
    docker run --read-only --tmpfs /tmp myimage  → writable /tmp only

  Drop capabilities:
    docker run --cap-drop ALL --cap-add NET_BIND_SERVICE myimage

  No privilege escalation:
    docker run --security-opt no-new-privileges myimage

  Seccomp profile:
    docker run --security-opt seccomp=profile.json myimage

  AppArmor:
    docker run --security-opt apparmor=docker-default myimage

  Resource limits:
    docker run --memory 512m --cpus 1.5 --pids-limit 100 myimage

HANDS-ON TASKS (Core):
  1. Write a multi-stage Dockerfile for a Go app (builder + distroless runtime)
  2. Build with --no-cache vs cached; measure size difference
  3. Create user-defined bridge network; verify DNS resolution between containers
  4. Use named volume for PostgreSQL; verify data persists across container removal
  5. Run a container with --read-only + --cap-drop ALL
  6. Write a thorough .dockerignore; measure build context size before and after

================================================================================
3. ADVANCED — Deep Topics
================================================================================

CONCEPT: Docker BuildKit
--------------------------
BuildKit is the modern Docker build backend (default since Docker 23).

Enable (older Docker):
  DOCKER_BUILDKIT=1 docker build .
  export DOCKER_BUILDKIT=1

Key features:
  Parallel stage execution (multi-stage builds run stages concurrently)
  Better caching (cache invalidation only when inputs change)
  Build secrets (not stored in image layers)
  SSH forwarding (access private repos during build)
  Cache mounts (persistent cache across builds)

Build secrets (not baked into image):
  # In Dockerfile:
  RUN --mount=type=secret,id=npmrc,target=/root/.npmrc \
      npm install

  # At build time:
  docker build --secret id=npmrc,src=~/.npmrc .

Cache mounts (persist across builds; not in image):
  RUN --mount=type=cache,target=/go/pkg/mod \
      --mount=type=cache,target=/root/.cache/go-build \
      go build -o /app/server .

  # Python pip cache:
  RUN --mount=type=cache,target=/root/.cache/pip \
      pip install -r requirements.txt

SSH mounts (private git repos):
  RUN --mount=type=ssh \
      git clone git@github.com:org/private-repo.git

  docker build --ssh default=$SSH_AUTH_SOCK .

Inline cache (embed cache metadata in image):
  docker build \
    --cache-from type=registry,ref=myapp:buildcache \
    --cache-to   type=registry,ref=myapp:buildcache,mode=max \
    -t myapp:latest .

CONCEPT: Image Optimization Techniques
----------------------------------------
  1. Use minimal base images: scratch, distroless, alpine
  2. Multi-stage builds (don't ship build tools)
  3. Combine RUN commands (fewer layers)
  4. Clean up in the same RUN (package caches, temp files)
  5. .dockerignore (reduce build context)
  6. Order layers: infrequent changes first (dependencies before source code)
  7. Use --no-install-recommends for apt
  8. Strip debug symbols: -ldflags="-s -w" for Go
  9. Use UPX to compress binaries (trade CPU for size)
  10. Avoid COPY . . early — copy only what's needed

  Layer analysis:
    dive myapp:v1       → interactive layer-by-layer size inspection
    docker image inspect --format='{{.Size}}' myapp:v1

CONCEPT: Container Resource Management
-----------------------------------------
  Memory:
    --memory 512m            → hard limit; OOM kill if exceeded
    --memory-swap 512m       → equal to memory = no swap
    --memory-reservation 256m → soft limit (scheduler hint)
    --oom-kill-disable        → don't OOM kill (use with care)

  CPU:
    --cpus 1.5               → max 1.5 CPUs (modern; preferred)
    --cpu-shares 512         → relative weight (1024 = default)
    --cpuset-cpus 0,1        → pin to CPU cores 0 and 1

  Block I/O:
    --device-read-bps /dev/sda:10mb   → throttle read rate
    --device-write-iops /dev/sda:100  → throttle write IOPS

  PIDs:
    --pids-limit 100         → prevent fork bombs

  View limits:
    docker inspect -f '{{.HostConfig.Memory}}' container
    cat /sys/fs/cgroup/memory/docker/<id>/memory.limit_in_bytes

CONCEPT: Docker Registry Operations
--------------------------------------
  Tagging:
    docker tag myapp:latest myregistry.com/org/myapp:1.2.3
    docker tag myapp:latest myregistry.com/org/myapp:latest
    docker tag myapp:latest myregistry.com/org/myapp:$(git rev-parse --short HEAD)

  Push/pull:
    docker push myregistry.com/org/myapp:1.2.3
    docker pull myregistry.com/org/myapp:1.2.3
    docker pull --platform linux/arm64 myapp:latest  → multi-arch

  Login:
    docker login myregistry.com
    docker login -u AWS -p $(aws ecr get-login-password) 123.dkr.ecr.us-east-1.amazonaws.com
    cat ~/.docker/config.json  → stored credentials (base64, not secure — use credential helpers)

  Credential helpers:
    docker-credential-ecr-login  → AWS ECR (auto-refreshes token)
    docker-credential-gcr        → GCR
    docker-credential-pass       → Linux (uses pass password manager)

  Registry API (v2):
    GET /v2/<name>/tags/list    → list tags
    GET /v2/<name>/manifests/<tag>  → get manifest
    DELETE /v2/<name>/manifests/<digest>  → delete image

  Running a private registry:
    docker run -d -p 5000:5000 \
      -v registry-data:/var/lib/registry \
      --name registry registry:2

CONCEPT: Multi-Architecture Builds
-------------------------------------
Build images for multiple CPU architectures (amd64, arm64, arm/v7).
Critical for: M1/M2 Macs (arm64), Raspberry Pi, AWS Graviton.

  # Setup buildx builder:
  docker buildx create --name multi-arch --use
  docker buildx inspect --bootstrap

  # Build for multiple platforms:
  docker buildx build \
    --platform linux/amd64,linux/arm64,linux/arm/v7 \
    -t myapp:latest \
    --push .          → must push; can't load multi-platform to local daemon

  # Build and load single arch (for local testing):
  docker buildx build --platform linux/arm64 -t myapp:arm64 --load .

  Manifest list (fat manifest):
    docker manifest inspect nginx:latest    → see all platform entries
    Each platform entry points to a platform-specific image

  Cross-compilation (Go):
    FROM --platform=$BUILDPLATFORM golang:1.21 AS builder
    ARG TARGETOS TARGETARCH
    RUN GOOS=$TARGETOS GOARCH=$TARGETARCH go build -o server .

CONCEPT: Docker Content Trust & Image Signing
-----------------------------------------------
  Docker Content Trust (DCT):
    DOCKER_CONTENT_TRUST=1 docker push myapp:v1   → sign on push
    DOCKER_CONTENT_TRUST=1 docker pull myapp:v1   → verify on pull
    Uses Notary (TUF framework) for key management

  Cosign (modern, CNCF):
    cosign sign --key cosign.key myregistry.com/myapp:v1@sha256:abc...
    cosign verify --key cosign.pub myregistry.com/myapp:v1
    OIDC signing (keyless):
      cosign sign myregistry.com/myapp:v1   → uses GitHub/Google OIDC
      cosign verify --certificate-identity-regexp='github.com/org/repo' \
                    --certificate-oidc-issuer='https://token.actions.githubusercontent.com' \
                    myregistry.com/myapp:v1

HANDS-ON TASKS (Advanced):
  1. Use BuildKit cache mounts to eliminate repeated go mod download
  2. Build a multi-arch image (amd64 + arm64); push to registry
  3. Use --secret to pass npm token without baking into layer
  4. Use dive to audit a real image's layer composition
  5. Set up a local registry with TLS; push and pull images
  6. Sign an image with cosign; verify the signature

================================================================================
SECTION B: DOCKER COMPOSE
================================================================================

CONCEPT: Docker Compose Overview
----------------------------------
Docker Compose defines and runs multi-container applications via YAML.
File: docker-compose.yml (or compose.yml — newer standard)
Compose V2: docker compose (plugin, built into Docker CLI, replaces docker-compose)

Use cases:
  - Local development environments
  - Integration testing (spin up app + dependencies)
  - Single-host small deployments (not production at scale)

CONCEPT: docker-compose.yml — Complete Reference
--------------------------------------------------

  # compose.yml
  name: myapp                               → project name (default: dir name)

  services:

    api:
      build:
        context: ./api
        dockerfile: Dockerfile
        target: development                  → build a specific stage
        args:
          BUILD_VERSION: ${VERSION:-dev}
        cache_from:
          - myapp-api:cache
      image: myapp-api:dev                  → tag the built image

      container_name: myapp-api             → fixed name (avoid in prod)
      hostname: api

      ports:
        - "8080:8080"
        - "127.0.0.1:9229:9229"             → debug port, localhost only

      environment:
        APP_ENV: development
        DATABASE_URL: postgresql://postgres:secret@db:5432/appdb
        REDIS_URL: redis://redis:6379
      env_file:
        - .env                              → load from .env file
        - .env.local                        → override (if exists)

      volumes:
        - ./api:/app                        → bind mount (hot reload in dev)
        - /app/node_modules                 → exclude node_modules from bind mount
        - api_uploads:/app/uploads          → named volume

      depends_on:
        db:
          condition: service_healthy        → wait for health check
        redis:
          condition: service_started

      healthcheck:
        test: ["CMD", "curl", "-f", "http://localhost:8080/health"]
        interval: 30s
        timeout: 10s
        retries: 3
        start_period: 10s

      restart: unless-stopped              → always/no/on-failure/unless-stopped

      networks:
        - frontend
        - backend

      deploy:
        resources:
          limits:
            cpus: '1.0'
            memory: 512M
          reservations:
            memory: 256M

      logging:
        driver: json-file
        options:
          max-size: "10m"
          max-file: "3"

      command: ["npm", "run", "dev"]        → override CMD
      entrypoint: ["/docker-entrypoint.sh"] → override ENTRYPOINT

      user: "1001:1001"
      read_only: true
      security_opt:
        - no-new-privileges:true
      cap_drop:
        - ALL
      tmpfs:
        - /tmp

    db:
      image: postgres:15-alpine
      volumes:
        - pgdata:/var/lib/postgresql/data
        - ./db/init:/docker-entrypoint-initdb.d:ro
      environment:
        POSTGRES_DB: appdb
        POSTGRES_USER: postgres
        POSTGRES_PASSWORD: secret
      healthcheck:
        test: ["CMD-SHELL", "pg_isready -U postgres -d appdb"]
        interval: 10s
        timeout: 5s
        retries: 5

    redis:
      image: redis:7-alpine
      command: redis-server --appendonly yes --requirepass secret
      volumes:
        - redis_data:/data

    nginx:
      image: nginx:alpine
      ports:
        - "80:80"
        - "443:443"
      volumes:
        - ./nginx/nginx.conf:/etc/nginx/nginx.conf:ro
        - ./nginx/certs:/etc/nginx/certs:ro
      depends_on:
        - api
      networks:
        - frontend

  volumes:
    pgdata:
      driver: local
    redis_data:
    api_uploads:
      external: true                        → must exist before compose up

  networks:
    frontend:
      driver: bridge
    backend:
      driver: bridge
      internal: true                        → no external access (security)

CONCEPT: Compose Commands
--------------------------
  docker compose up -d                     → start all services detached
  docker compose up --build                → rebuild images before starting
  docker compose up -d --scale api=3       → scale api to 3 replicas

  docker compose down                      → stop + remove containers + networks
  docker compose down -v                   → also remove volumes (DATA LOSS)
  docker compose down --remove-orphans

  docker compose ps                        → list services
  docker compose logs -f api               → follow logs for api service
  docker compose logs --tail=50 api db     → last 50 lines for api and db

  docker compose exec api bash             → exec into running service
  docker compose run --rm api go test ./... → one-off command in service container

  docker compose pull                      → pull latest images
  docker compose build                     → build all services with build config
  docker compose build --no-cache api      → force rebuild api

  docker compose config                    → validate + print resolved config
  docker compose top                       → running processes per service

CONCEPT: Compose Profiles
--------------------------
Profiles let you selectively start services:

  services:
    api:
      # no profile → always started
    db:
      # no profile → always started
    adminer:
      profiles: [tools]                    → only with --profile tools
    prometheus:
      profiles: [monitoring]
    grafana:
      profiles: [monitoring]

  docker compose up -d                    → starts api, db only
  docker compose --profile tools up -d    → adds adminer
  docker compose --profile monitoring up -d → adds prometheus, grafana
  docker compose --profile tools --profile monitoring up -d

CONCEPT: Compose Override Files
---------------------------------
Layer multiple compose files — later files override earlier ones:

  docker compose up                        → uses docker-compose.yml
  docker compose -f compose.yml -f compose.override.yml up  → explicit
  COMPOSE_FILE=compose.yml:compose.prod.yml docker compose up

  Typical pattern:
    compose.yml            → base service definitions
    compose.override.yml   → dev overrides (auto-loaded)
    compose.prod.yml        → production overrides (explicit)
    compose.test.yml        → test overrides (for CI)

  # compose.override.yml (development)
  services:
    api:
      build:
        target: development
      volumes:
        - ./api:/app             → live code reload
      command: npm run dev
      environment:
        DEBUG: "true"

  # compose.prod.yml
  services:
    api:
      image: myregistry.com/myapp:${TAG}   → use pre-built image in prod
      volumes: []                          → no bind mounts in prod
      restart: always

CONCEPT: Compose for Integration Testing (CI)
----------------------------------------------
  # In CI pipeline:
  docker compose -f compose.yml -f compose.test.yml up -d
  docker compose run --rm test-runner go test ./... -v
  docker compose down -v --remove-orphans

  # compose.test.yml
  services:
    api:
      environment:
        DATABASE_URL: postgresql://test:test@db:5432/testdb
    test-runner:
      build:
        context: .
        target: tester
      depends_on:
        db:
          condition: service_healthy
        api:
          condition: service_healthy
      command: ["go", "test", "./..."]
      profiles: [test]

HANDS-ON TASKS (Docker Compose):
  1. Build full-stack app (API + DB + Redis + nginx reverse proxy)
  2. Use profiles for optional services (admin tools, monitoring)
  3. Create compose.override.yml for dev + compose.prod.yml for prod
  4. Test depends_on with health checks; observe startup ordering
  5. Use compose in CI: spin up, run tests, tear down

================================================================================
SECTION C: DOCKER SWARM
================================================================================

CONCEPT: Docker Swarm Architecture
------------------------------------
Docker Swarm is Docker's native clustering and orchestration.
Built into Docker; no separate installation needed.

Components:
  Manager nodes → Raft consensus; orchestrate services; accept API requests
  Worker nodes  → execute tasks (container instances)

  Raft consensus: minimum 3 managers for HA (tolerates 1 failure)
                  5 managers tolerate 2 failures
                  Always use odd number of managers

  Swarm concepts:
    Service → desired state declaration (image, replicas, ports, etc.)
    Task    → a single container instance of a service
    Stack   → group of services from a compose file (deployed together)

CONCEPT: Swarm Setup & Management
-----------------------------------
  # Initialize Swarm:
  docker swarm init --advertise-addr 192.168.1.10   → on first manager

  # Get join tokens:
  docker swarm join-token manager    → to add manager nodes
  docker swarm join-token worker     → to add worker nodes

  # Join as worker:
  docker swarm join --token <token> 192.168.1.10:2377

  # Promote/demote:
  docker node promote worker1        → worker → manager
  docker node demote manager2        → manager → worker

  # Node management:
  docker node ls                     → list all nodes
  docker node inspect self           → inspect current node
  docker node update --availability drain manager1  → prevent tasks on node
  docker node update --availability active manager1

CONCEPT: Services & Deployment
--------------------------------
  # Deploy replicated service:
  docker service create \
    --name webapp \
    --replicas 3 \
    --publish published=80,target=8080 \
    --network myoverlay \
    --update-delay 10s \
    --update-parallelism 1 \
    --rollback-parallelism 1 \
    myapp:1.2.3

  # Global service (one task per node):
  docker service create --mode global --name node-exporter prom/node-exporter

  # Service management:
  docker service ls                   → list services
  docker service ps webapp            → list tasks for service
  docker service inspect webapp       → full config
  docker service logs -f webapp       → logs from all tasks
  docker service update --image myapp:1.2.4 webapp  → rolling update
  docker service scale webapp=5       → scale up/down
  docker service rollback webapp      → rollback to previous spec
  docker service rm webapp

CONCEPT: Swarm Stacks (Compose-based deployment)
--------------------------------------------------
  # Deploy a stack from compose file:
  docker stack deploy -c compose.yml mystack

  # Stack management:
  docker stack ls
  docker stack ps mystack             → all tasks in stack
  docker stack services mystack       → services in stack
  docker stack rm mystack

  # Stack compose file (Swarm-specific additions):
  services:
    api:
      image: myapp:1.2.3             → stacks require pre-built images
      deploy:
        replicas: 3
        placement:
          constraints:
            - node.role == worker
            - node.labels.region == us-east
          preferences:
            - spread: node.labels.zone   → spread across availability zones
        update_config:
          parallelism: 1               → update 1 at a time
          delay: 10s
          order: start-first           → start new before stopping old
          failure_action: rollback
          monitor: 30s
        rollback_config:
          parallelism: 0               → rollback all at once
          failure_action: pause
        resources:
          limits:
            cpus: '0.5'
            memory: 256M
          reservations:
            memory: 128M
        restart_policy:
          condition: on-failure
          delay: 5s
          max_attempts: 3

CONCEPT: Swarm Networking & Overlay Networks
----------------------------------------------
  # Create overlay network:
  docker network create -d overlay --attachable mynet

  # Services on same overlay network communicate by service name (DNS)
  # Swarm's internal DNS: service-name → VIP → load balances to replicas

  Routing mesh:
    Swarm publishes ports on ALL nodes (ingress network).
    Request to any node's port is routed to a healthy task, regardless of
    where the task is running. No need to know which node runs what.

  Internal LB (VIP mode):
    Default: Virtual IP per service; requests load balanced to replicas
    dnsrr mode: DNS round-robin (returns all task IPs, client does LB)

CONCEPT: Swarm Secrets & Configs
----------------------------------
  Secrets (encrypted at rest and in transit; in-memory in containers):
    docker secret create db_password db_pass.txt
    echo "mysecretpassword" | docker secret create db_password -

    In service:
    docker service create \
      --name db \
      --secret db_password \
      postgres

    In compose:
    secrets:
      db_password:
        external: true                     → must exist in Swarm
    services:
      db:
        secrets:
          - db_password

    Inside container: /run/secrets/db_password → file with secret value

  Configs (non-sensitive; config files distributed to containers):
    docker config create nginx_conf ./nginx.conf
    docker service create --config nginx_conf nginx

CONCEPT: Swarm vs Kubernetes (context only — not comparison)
--------------------------------------------------------------
Docker Swarm is simpler to operate and is appropriate for:
  - Smaller teams / simpler workloads
  - Shops already using Docker Compose (easy migration)
  - When operational complexity of Kubernetes is not justified
  - Edge deployments, bare metal with limited resources

In most modern production environments, Kubernetes has replaced Swarm.
Understanding Swarm is valuable for: maintaining legacy systems, and for
deeply understanding how container orchestration works before Kubernetes.

HANDS-ON TASKS (Docker Swarm):
  1. Initialize a 3-node Swarm (3 VMs); verify Raft consensus
  2. Deploy a service with 5 replicas; observe task distribution
  3. Perform a rolling update with --update-delay; observe no downtime
  4. Use placement constraints to pin a service to specific nodes
  5. Create a Swarm secret; deploy a service consuming it
  6. Deploy a full stack from compose.yml; scale and roll back

================================================================================
4. EXPERT LEVEL — Production-Grade Knowledge
================================================================================

CONCEPT: Container Image Security — Full Pipeline
---------------------------------------------------
Defense in depth for container images:

  1. Base image hygiene:
       - Use minimal images (distroless, alpine, scratch)
       - Pin to digest: FROM ubuntu@sha256:abc123...
       - Regularly rebuild to get OS patches
       - Use official images (Docker Hub "Official Image" badge)

  2. Build-time scanning:
       trivy image myapp:latest
       grype myapp:latest
       snyk container test myapp:latest

  3. SBOM (Software Bill of Materials):
       syft myapp:latest -o spdx-json > sbom.json
       grype sbom:sbom.json                         → scan SBOM for vulns

  4. Image signing:
       cosign sign myapp:latest                     → sign after push
       cosign verify myapp:latest                   → verify before deploy

  5. Admission control (Kubernetes):
       OPA Gatekeeper / Kyverno → enforce image signing, no :latest tags,
       require labels, deny privileged containers

  6. Runtime scanning:
       Falco → detects anomalous behavior at runtime
       Sysdig Secure → commercial runtime security

CONCEPT: Docker Daemon Security Hardening
-------------------------------------------
  /etc/docker/daemon.json:
  {
    "icc": false,                          → disable inter-container communication by default
    "no-new-privileges": true,             → prevent privilege escalation
    "userns-remap": "default",             → user namespace remapping (root in container = non-root on host)
    "log-driver": "json-file",
    "log-opts": {
      "max-size": "10m",
      "max-file": "3"
    },
    "storage-driver": "overlay2",
    "default-address-pools": [
      {"base":"172.17.0.0/12","size":24}
    ],
    "live-restore": true,                  → keep containers running on daemon restart
    "default-ulimits": {
      "nofile": { "Name": "nofile", "Hard": 65536, "Soft": 65536 }
    }
  }

  Never expose Docker socket to containers (privilege escalation):
    DANGEROUS: -v /var/run/docker.sock:/var/run/docker.sock
    Alternatives: Docker-out-of-Docker (DOOD) with careful RBAC, Kaniko, Buildah

  Docker daemon TLS (remote access):
    docker --tlsverify --tlscacert=ca.pem --tlscert=cert.pem --tlskey=key.pem \
      -H tcp://host:2376 ps
    DOCKER_HOST=tcp://host:2376 DOCKER_TLS_VERIFY=1 docker ps

CONCEPT: Production Logging Patterns
---------------------------------------
  Log drivers:
    json-file  → default; rotatable; local only
    syslog     → sends to syslog/rsyslog
    journald   → systemd journal
    fluentd    → structured forwarding to Fluentd
    awslogs    → CloudWatch Logs
    gelf       → Graylog Extended Log Format

  Production pattern (EFK/ELK stack):
    Container → json-file → Fluentd DaemonSet → Elasticsearch → Kibana
    Or: Container → stdout → Promtail → Loki → Grafana

  Structured logging (JSON):
    Containers should log JSON to stdout/stderr (no file logging).
    Log aggregator parses JSON fields for filtering/alerting.

    {"level":"error","timestamp":"2024-01-15T10:30:00Z","service":"api",
     "trace_id":"abc123","user_id":456,"error":"db connection timeout"}

CONCEPT: Docker in Production — Operational Patterns
------------------------------------------------------
  Health checks in production:
    HEALTHCHECK in Dockerfile → Swarm/Compose use this
    Kubernetes uses liveness/readiness probes (overrides Docker healthcheck)
    Probe types: HTTP GET, TCP socket, exec command

  Init containers and entrypoint patterns:
    # docker-entrypoint.sh
    #!/bin/sh
    set -e

    # Wait for dependencies
    until nc -z db 5432; do sleep 1; done

    # Run migrations
    ./migrate up

    # Exec the actual command (replaces shell with app process)
    exec "$@"          → CRITICAL: exec replaces shell; PID 1 receives signals

    ENTRYPOINT ["./docker-entrypoint.sh"]
    CMD ["./server"]

  Graceful shutdown:
    - App must handle SIGTERM (Swarm/Kubernetes sends this to stop)
    - Clean up: close DB connections, finish in-flight requests, flush buffers
    - Container stop grace period: docker stop -t 30 (30s before SIGKILL)
    - Compose: stop_grace_period: 30s

  Zombie process reaping:
    PID 1 must reap zombie processes.
    Shell scripts don't do this. Solutions:
      - Use tini as PID 1: docker run --init myapp
      - Add tini to Dockerfile: RUN apk add tini; ENTRYPOINT ["/sbin/tini","--"]
      - Go/Node/Python apps with proper signal handling typically don't need it

CONCEPT: Troubleshooting Containers
--------------------------------------
  Container won't start:
    docker logs container_name          → check error output
    docker inspect container_name       → check exit code, error
    docker run -it --entrypoint sh image  → override entrypoint to debug

  Container exits immediately:
    docker logs container_name          → stdout/stderr capture
    Likely: entrypoint exits; app crashes; missing env vars

  Can't reach container:
    docker inspect -f '{{.NetworkSettings}}' container  → check network
    docker exec container curl localhost:8080           → test from inside
    iptables -L -n | grep <port>                       → check host firewall

  Disk space issues:
    docker system df -v                 → detailed breakdown
    docker container ls -s             → container disk usage
    docker system prune -a --volumes   → nuclear cleanup (carefully!)

  High CPU in container:
    docker stats container              → live metrics
    docker exec container top          → processes inside

REAL-WORLD SCENARIOS:
  SCENARIO A — Container image keeps getting rejected by security scanner:
    1. trivy image myapp:latest --format table → identify CVEs
    2. Identify layer with vulnerable packages: docker history + trivy
    3. Update base image: FROM ubuntu:22.04 → FROM ubuntu:24.04
    4. Rebuild → rescan → verify CVEs resolved
    5. Add trivy to CI pipeline with --exit-code 1 for CRITICAL
    6. Set up automated base image updates (Renovate/Dependabot)

  SCENARIO B — Multi-environment Docker Compose workflow:
    Developer flow:
      docker compose up                 → starts with compose.override.yml (dev)
      Bind mounts for live reload, debug ports exposed, verbose logging
    CI flow:
      docker compose -f compose.yml -f compose.test.yml up -d
      docker compose run --rm test-runner
      docker compose down -v
    Production:
      Never use Compose for production at scale → migrate to Kubernetes
      If using Compose: compose.prod.yml with pre-built images, restart policies

  SCENARIO C — Docker Swarm rolling update with zero downtime:
    1. docker service update --image myapp:v2
         --update-parallelism 1
         --update-delay 15s
         --update-order start-first
         --rollback-on-failure
         webapp
    2. Docker starts 1 new v2 task, health-checks it
    3. On healthy: stops 1 old v1 task
    4. Repeats until all replicas updated
    5. If any new task fails health check: auto-rollback
    6. Monitor: docker service ps webapp (watch CURRENT STATE column)

================================================================================
PHASE 5: CONTAINERS — SUMMARY CHECKLIST
================================================================================
DOCKER CORE:
  [ ] Docker architecture (CLI → dockerd → containerd → runc)
  [ ] Image layers, content addressing, layer sharing
  [ ] All essential docker commands
  [ ] Dockerfile: all instructions (FROM, RUN, COPY, ADD, CMD, ENTRYPOINT, etc.)
  [ ] ENTRYPOINT vs CMD interaction
  [ ] Multi-stage builds (builder + minimal runtime)
  [ ] .dockerignore
  [ ] Docker networking: bridge, host, none, overlay, macvlan
  [ ] User-defined bridge DNS resolution
  [ ] Docker volumes: named, bind mounts, tmpfs
  [ ] Container security: non-root, read-only, cap-drop, no-new-privileges
  [ ] BuildKit: cache mounts, build secrets, SSH mounts
  [ ] Image optimization: size reduction techniques
  [ ] Resource limits: memory, CPU, pids
  [ ] Multi-architecture builds (buildx)
  [ ] Image signing (cosign, DCT)
  [ ] Registry operations + credential helpers

DOCKER COMPOSE:
  [ ] Complete compose.yml syntax (all directives)
  [ ] depends_on with health check conditions
  [ ] Compose profiles
  [ ] Override files (compose.override.yml, compose.prod.yml)
  [ ] Compose for CI/CD integration testing
  [ ] docker compose commands

DOCKER SWARM:
  [ ] Swarm architecture (managers, workers, Raft)
  [ ] Swarm init, join, node management
  [ ] Service creation, scaling, rolling updates, rollback
  [ ] Stack deployment from compose files
  [ ] Overlay networking and routing mesh
  [ ] Swarm secrets and configs
  [ ] Placement constraints and preferences

EXPERT:
  [ ] Container image security pipeline (SBOM, signing, admission control)
  [ ] Docker daemon hardening (daemon.json)
  [ ] Production logging drivers + structured JSON logging
  [ ] PID 1 / tini / signal handling / graceful shutdown
  [ ] Zombie reaping
  [ ] Container troubleshooting methodology
  [ ] exec entrypoint pattern
