################################################################################
#                   PHASE 6: KUBERNETES (EXTREMELY DEEP)                      #
################################################################################

================================================================================
1. FOUNDATION — Architecture & Core Objects
================================================================================

CONCEPT: Kubernetes Architecture
----------------------------------
Kubernetes is a container orchestration platform. It manages containerized
workloads across a cluster of machines, providing scheduling, self-healing,
scaling, service discovery, and configuration management.

CONTROL PLANE (manages cluster state):
  kube-apiserver
    - The ONLY entry point to the cluster state
    - RESTful API server; validates + persists to etcd
    - Authentication → Authorization (RBAC) → Admission Control → etcd
    - Stateless; horizontally scalable; all components talk to it

  etcd
    - Distributed key-value store; source of truth for ALL cluster state
    - Uses Raft consensus algorithm (need 3, 5, 7 nodes for HA)
    - Stores: pods, secrets, configmaps, deployments, RBAC, etc.
    - Only kube-apiserver talks to etcd directly

  kube-scheduler
    - Watches for unscheduled pods; assigns them to nodes
    - Considers: resource requests/limits, taints/tolerations, affinity,
      node selectors, topology spread, priority classes
    - Two phases: Filtering (feasible nodes) → Scoring (best node)

  kube-controller-manager
    - Runs control loops (controllers) that reconcile desired vs actual state
    - Node Controller: detects unreachable nodes
    - ReplicaSet Controller: ensures correct pod count
    - Deployment Controller: manages rolling updates
    - Endpoints Controller: populates Endpoints/EndpointSlice objects
    - ServiceAccount Controller: creates default SAs
    - Job Controller, CronJob Controller, etc.

  cloud-controller-manager (cloud environments only)
    - Interfaces with cloud provider APIs
    - Node lifecycle, route management, load balancer provisioning

DATA PLANE (runs workloads):
  kubelet
    - Node agent; runs on every node
    - Watches kube-apiserver for pods assigned to its node
    - Creates/deletes containers via CRI (container runtime interface)
    - Reports node/pod status back to apiserver
    - Runs health probes (liveness, readiness, startup)

  kube-proxy
    - Network proxy on every node
    - Implements Service abstraction via iptables/IPVS rules
    - Routes Service VIPs to backend pod IPs
    - Modes: iptables (default), IPVS (better performance at scale)

  Container Runtime
    - Implements CRI; actually creates containers
    - containerd (most common), CRI-O, Docker (deprecated)

  CNI Plugin (network)
    - Provisions pod networking (assigns IPs, sets up routes)
    - Examples: Calico, Cilium, Flannel, Weave

CONCEPT: The Reconciliation Loop (Core Mental Model)
------------------------------------------------------
Every Kubernetes controller follows this pattern:

  DESIRED STATE (stored in etcd via API objects)
        ↕  reconcile
  ACTUAL STATE (what's really running)

  Loop: Watch → Compare → Act
    1. Watch apiserver for object changes
    2. Compare desired spec with actual status
    3. Take action to make actual match desired
    4. Update status subresource

  This is declarative: you describe WHAT you want, not HOW to achieve it.
  If a pod dies → controller creates a new one → eventually consistent.

CONCEPT: Core Kubernetes Objects
----------------------------------
Pod:
  Smallest deployable unit. One or more containers sharing:
  - Network namespace (same IP, communicate via localhost)
  - Storage volumes
  - PID namespace (optionally)

  Pods are ephemeral. Never manage pods directly in production.
  Use higher-level controllers (Deployment, StatefulSet, etc.)

  apiVersion: v1
  kind: Pod
  metadata:
    name: myapp
    labels:
      app: myapp
      version: v1
  spec:
    containers:
    - name: app
      image: myapp:1.0.0
      ports:
      - containerPort: 8080
      resources:
        requests:
          cpu: 100m
          memory: 128Mi
        limits:
          cpu: 500m
          memory: 256Mi
      env:
      - name: APP_ENV
        value: production
      livenessProbe:
        httpGet:
          path: /health
          port: 8080
        initialDelaySeconds: 10
        periodSeconds: 30
      readinessProbe:
        httpGet:
          path: /ready
          port: 8080
        initialDelaySeconds: 5
        periodSeconds: 10

Deployment:
  Manages ReplicaSets → manages Pods. Provides rolling updates + rollback.

  apiVersion: apps/v1
  kind: Deployment
  metadata:
    name: myapp
    namespace: production
  spec:
    replicas: 3
    selector:
      matchLabels:
        app: myapp
    strategy:
      type: RollingUpdate
      rollingUpdate:
        maxSurge: 1          → can have 4 pods during update (3+1)
        maxUnavailable: 0    → never go below 3 pods
    template:
      metadata:
        labels:
          app: myapp
          version: v2
      spec:
        containers:
        - name: app
          image: myapp:2.0.0

  kubectl rollout history deployment/myapp
  kubectl rollout undo deployment/myapp
  kubectl rollout undo deployment/myapp --to-revision=3
  kubectl rollout status deployment/myapp --timeout=5m
  kubectl rollout pause/resume deployment/myapp

StatefulSet:
  For stateful apps (databases, message queues).
  Provides: stable network identity, ordered deployment/scaling,
  stable persistent storage.

  Pod names: mydb-0, mydb-1, mydb-2 (not random)
  DNS: mydb-0.mydb.namespace.svc.cluster.local
  Ordered startup: 0 → 1 → 2 (each waits for previous to be Running+Ready)
  Ordered termination: 2 → 1 → 0

DaemonSet:
  Runs one pod per node (or per subset of nodes with nodeSelector).
  Use cases: log collectors (Fluentd/Promtail), monitoring agents (node-exporter),
  CNI plugins, storage daemons (Longhorn, Rook), kube-proxy.

  Updates: RollingUpdate (default) or OnDelete

Job & CronJob:
  Job: runs pod(s) to completion; retries on failure
    completions: 5          → run 5 successful completions
    parallelism: 2          → 2 at a time
    backoffLimit: 4         → retry 4 times before marking failed
    activeDeadlineSeconds   → kill job if exceeds time

  CronJob: creates Jobs on a schedule
    schedule: "0 2 * * *"
    concurrencyPolicy: Allow/Forbid/Replace
    startingDeadlineSeconds: 100
    successfulJobsHistoryLimit: 3
    failedJobsHistoryLimit: 1

Service:
  Stable network endpoint for a set of pods. Implements load balancing.

  Types:
    ClusterIP:   default; cluster-internal VIP; no external access
    NodePort:    exposes on static port (30000-32767) on every node
    LoadBalancer: provisions cloud LB; external IP
    ExternalName: DNS alias to external service (no proxying)
    Headless:    clusterIP: None; no VIP; DNS returns pod IPs directly
                 Used by StatefulSets and service mesh

  selector: app: myapp    → routes to pods matching this label
  Port mapping: port → targetPort (container port)
  sessionAffinity: ClientIP → sticky sessions

ConfigMap & Secret:
  ConfigMap: non-sensitive key-value config; injected as env vars or volumes
  Secret: base64-encoded (NOT encrypted by default!) sensitive data

  Secret types:
    Opaque              → generic key-value
    kubernetes.io/tls   → TLS certificate
    kubernetes.io/dockerconfigjson → registry credentials

  Consume as env var:
    env:
    - name: DB_PASS
      valueFrom:
        secretKeyRef:
          name: db-secret
          key: password

  Consume as volume (recommended for files/certs):
    volumes:
    - name: secrets-vol
      secret:
        secretName: db-secret
    volumeMounts:
    - name: secrets-vol
      mountPath: /etc/secrets
      readOnly: true

HANDS-ON TASKS (Foundation):
  1. Deploy a 3-replica Deployment; trigger rolling update; rollback
  2. Create a StatefulSet with persistent storage; verify pod ordering
  3. Create a DaemonSet; verify one pod per node
  4. Expose a Deployment with ClusterIP, NodePort, and LoadBalancer Services
  5. Inject ConfigMap as env vars AND as a volume; observe differences
  6. kubectl explain pod.spec.containers --recursive → explore API docs

================================================================================
2. CORE CONCEPTS — Intermediate
================================================================================

CONCEPT: Namespaces
--------------------
Namespaces provide soft multi-tenancy; logical isolation within a cluster.

  kubectl get namespaces
  kubectl create namespace production
  kubectl config set-context --current --namespace=production  → set default NS

  Default namespaces:
    default         → catch-all if no namespace specified
    kube-system     → system components (coredns, kube-proxy, etc.)
    kube-public     → publicly readable; cluster-info ConfigMap
    kube-node-lease → node heartbeat leases

  Resource scoping:
    Namespaced: pods, deployments, services, secrets, configmaps, RBAC roles
    Cluster-scoped: nodes, PVs, StorageClasses, ClusterRoles, Namespaces

  ResourceQuota: limit total resources per namespace
  LimitRange: set default + min/max per pod/container in namespace

CONCEPT: Labels, Selectors & Annotations
------------------------------------------
Labels:
  Key-value pairs for identifying/grouping objects.
  Used by selectors (Services, Deployments, NetworkPolicies).

  kubectl label pod mypod env=production
  kubectl get pods -l app=myapp,env=production
  kubectl get pods -l 'app in (myapp,otherapp)'
  kubectl get pods -l 'env notin (dev,staging)'
  kubectl get pods -l '!deprecated'           → label not present

  Recommended labels:
    app.kubernetes.io/name: myapp
    app.kubernetes.io/version: "1.2.3"
    app.kubernetes.io/component: frontend
    app.kubernetes.io/part-of: myplatform
    app.kubernetes.io/managed-by: helm

Annotations:
  Non-identifying metadata; not used by selectors.
  Used for: tool configuration, deployment notes, monitoring scrape configs,
  ingress rules, cert-manager, etc.

  kubectl annotate pod mypod deployment-tool/deployed-by=jenkins
  kubectl get pod mypod -o jsonpath='{.metadata.annotations}'

CONCEPT: Resource Requests & Limits
--------------------------------------
Request: minimum guaranteed resources; used by scheduler for placement.
Limit: maximum allowed; enforced by cgroups.

  resources:
    requests:
      cpu: "100m"       → 0.1 CPU core (100 millicores)
      memory: "128Mi"   → 128 mebibytes
    limits:
      cpu: "500m"
      memory: "256Mi"

  CPU behavior:
    CPU is compressible. If over limit: throttled (not killed).
    cpu requests → cgroup cpu.shares (relative weight)
    cpu limits   → cgroup cpu.quota/cpu.period (enforced)

  Memory behavior:
    Memory is incompressible. If over limit: OOM killed (SIGKILL, exit 137).
    Always set memory request = limit for critical apps (Guaranteed QoS).

  QoS Classes (scheduler + kubelet eviction order):
    Guaranteed:  requests == limits (both set); highest priority; evicted last
    Burstable:   requests < limits OR only one set; middle priority
    BestEffort:  no requests or limits set; lowest priority; evicted first

  PRODUCTION RULE: Always set requests. Set limits ≥ requests.
  For critical services: requests = limits (Guaranteed QoS).

CONCEPT: Health Probes
------------------------
  Liveness probe:   Is the container alive? If fails → restart container.
                    Use for: detecting deadlocks, hung processes.

  Readiness probe:  Is the container ready for traffic? If fails → remove from
                    Service endpoints (no traffic). Container NOT restarted.
                    Use for: warming up, dependency checks.

  Startup probe:    Is the container done starting? Disables liveness during
                    startup. If fails → restart. Use for: slow-starting apps.
                    Use with failureThreshold * periodSeconds >= max startup time.

  Probe types:
    httpGet:    GET request; 2xx/3xx = success
    tcpSocket:  TCP connection succeeds = alive
    exec:       Command exit code 0 = alive
    grpc:       gRPC health protocol (K8s 1.24+)

  Parameters:
    initialDelaySeconds: 10    → wait before first probe (deprecated for startupProbe)
    periodSeconds: 10          → interval between probes
    timeoutSeconds: 5          → probe timeout
    failureThreshold: 3        → failures before action taken
    successThreshold: 1        → successes before marked ready (readiness only)

CONCEPT: Node Selection & Scheduling
--------------------------------------
nodeSelector (simple key-value match):
  nodeSelector:
    disktype: ssd
    kubernetes.io/arch: amd64

nodeAffinity (richer rules):
  affinity:
    nodeAffinity:
      requiredDuringSchedulingIgnoredDuringExecution:   → hard requirement
        nodeSelectorTerms:
        - matchExpressions:
          - key: topology.kubernetes.io/zone
            operator: In
            values: [us-east-1a, us-east-1b]
      preferredDuringSchedulingIgnoredDuringExecution:  → soft preference
      - weight: 100
        preference:
          matchExpressions:
          - key: disktype
            operator: In
            values: [ssd]

podAffinity / podAntiAffinity:
  Co-locate or spread pods relative to other pods.

  # Spread replicas across different nodes (anti-affinity):
  affinity:
    podAntiAffinity:
      requiredDuringSchedulingIgnoredDuringExecution:
      - labelSelector:
          matchLabels:
            app: myapp
        topologyKey: kubernetes.io/hostname    → spread across different nodes

  # Co-locate with cache pods:
  affinity:
    podAffinity:
      preferredDuringSchedulingIgnoredDuringExecution:
      - weight: 50
        podAffinityTerm:
          labelSelector:
            matchLabels:
              app: redis-cache
          topologyKey: kubernetes.io/hostname

CONCEPT: Taints & Tolerations
-------------------------------
Taints: applied to NODES; repel pods that don't tolerate them.
Tolerations: applied to PODS; allow scheduling on tainted nodes.

  # Taint a node:
  kubectl taint nodes node1 key=value:NoSchedule
  kubectl taint nodes node1 key=value:PreferNoSchedule
  kubectl taint nodes node1 key=value:NoExecute    → evicts existing pods too

  # Remove taint:
  kubectl taint nodes node1 key=value:NoSchedule-

  # Tolerate in pod spec:
  tolerations:
  - key: "key"
    operator: "Equal"
    value: "value"
    effect: "NoSchedule"

  - key: "node.kubernetes.io/not-ready"
    operator: "Exists"
    effect: "NoExecute"
    tolerationSeconds: 300    → tolerate for 300s before eviction

  Common built-in taints:
    node.kubernetes.io/not-ready          → node not ready
    node.kubernetes.io/unreachable        → node unreachable
    node.kubernetes.io/memory-pressure    → node memory low
    node.kubernetes.io/disk-pressure      → node disk low
    node.kubernetes.io/unschedulable      → node cordoned
    node.kubernetes.io/network-unavailable

  Use case: dedicated nodes
    kubectl taint nodes gpu-node nvidia.com/gpu=present:NoSchedule
    Only pods with the toleration will schedule there.

CONCEPT: Topology Spread Constraints
--------------------------------------
Spread pods evenly across failure domains (zones, nodes).

  topologySpreadConstraints:
  - maxSkew: 1                              → max difference between zones
    topologyKey: topology.kubernetes.io/zone
    whenUnsatisfiable: DoNotSchedule       → hard: fail if can't spread
    labelSelector:
      matchLabels:
        app: myapp
  - maxSkew: 1
    topologyKey: kubernetes.io/hostname    → also spread across nodes
    whenUnsatisfiable: ScheduleAnyway     → soft: prefer but allow
    labelSelector:
      matchLabels:
        app: myapp

  Production: combine zone spread + node spread for HA deployments.

CONCEPT: Horizontal Pod Autoscaler (HPA)
------------------------------------------
  apiVersion: autoscaling/v2
  kind: HorizontalPodAutoscaler
  metadata:
    name: myapp-hpa
  spec:
    scaleTargetRef:
      apiVersion: apps/v1
      kind: Deployment
      name: myapp
    minReplicas: 2
    maxReplicas: 20
    metrics:
    - type: Resource
      resource:
        name: cpu
        target:
          type: Utilization
          averageUtilization: 70       → scale when avg CPU > 70%
    - type: Resource
      resource:
        name: memory
        target:
          type: AverageValue
          averageValue: 200Mi
    - type: Pods
      pods:
        metric:
          name: http_requests_per_second    → custom metric from Prometheus adapter
        target:
          type: AverageValue
          averageValue: "1000"
    behavior:
      scaleUp:
        stabilizationWindowSeconds: 0      → scale up immediately
        policies:
        - type: Pods
          value: 4
          periodSeconds: 60              → add max 4 pods per 60s
      scaleDown:
        stabilizationWindowSeconds: 300   → wait 5 min before scale down
        policies:
        - type: Percent
          value: 25
          periodSeconds: 60

  Requires: metrics-server (resource metrics) or custom metrics adapter

Vertical Pod Autoscaler (VPA):
  Recommends / auto-adjusts CPU+memory requests+limits.
  Mode: Off (recommend only), Initial (set on create), Auto (update running)
  Not compatible with HPA on same metric; use VPA for requests, HPA for replicas.

Cluster Autoscaler (CA):
  Adds/removes nodes based on pending pods and underutilized nodes.
  Integrates with cloud provider node groups (ASGs, MIGs, VMSS).

HANDS-ON TASKS (Core):
  1. Create a Deployment with liveness + readiness + startup probes; simulate failures
  2. Implement node affinity + pod anti-affinity for HA spread
  3. Taint a node; observe pod eviction; add toleration
  4. Set topology spread constraints; verify even zone distribution
  5. Configure HPA with CPU + custom metrics; load test and watch scaling
  6. Set ResourceQuota on a namespace; hit limits; observe behavior

================================================================================
3. ADVANCED — Storage, Networking, Security
================================================================================

CONCEPT: Persistent Volumes (PV), PVCs & StorageClasses
---------------------------------------------------------
PersistentVolume (PV):
  Cluster-scoped storage resource; created by admin or dynamically.
  Independent of pod lifecycle.

  accessModes:
    ReadWriteOnce (RWO)    → single node read-write (most block storage)
    ReadOnlyMany (ROX)     → multiple nodes read-only
    ReadWriteMany (RWX)    → multiple nodes read-write (NFS, CephFS, EFS)
    ReadWriteOncePod (RWOP) → single pod read-write (K8s 1.22+)

  reclaimPolicy:
    Retain    → keep PV data after PVC deleted; manual cleanup
    Delete    → delete underlying storage when PVC deleted
    Recycle   → deprecated

PersistentVolumeClaim (PVC):
  Namespace-scoped request for storage; binds to a matching PV.

  apiVersion: v1
  kind: PersistentVolumeClaim
  metadata:
    name: data-pvc
  spec:
    accessModes: [ReadWriteOnce]
    storageClassName: gp3
    resources:
      requests:
        storage: 50Gi

  Binding: Kubernetes finds a PV matching access mode, size, and storageClass.
  Static provisioning:  admin creates PV manually → PVC binds to it
  Dynamic provisioning: StorageClass creates PV automatically on PVC creation

StorageClass:
  Defines a "class" of storage with parameters.

  apiVersion: storage.k8s.io/v1
  kind: StorageClass
  metadata:
    name: gp3
    annotations:
      storageclass.kubernetes.io/is-default-class: "true"
  provisioner: ebs.csi.aws.com
  parameters:
    type: gp3
    iops: "3000"
    throughput: "125"
    encrypted: "true"
  reclaimPolicy: Delete
  volumeBindingMode: WaitForFirstConsumer   → delay binding until pod scheduled
  allowVolumeExpansion: true

  volumeBindingMode:
    Immediate:             → bind as soon as PVC created (wrong zone risk)
    WaitForFirstConsumer:  → bind when pod scheduled (same zone as pod)

CSI (Container Storage Interface):
  Standard interface for storage plugins. Replaced in-tree drivers.
  Key CSI drivers: aws-ebs-csi-driver, gce-pd-csi-driver, azuredisk-csi-driver,
                   nfs-subdir-external-provisioner, rook-ceph, longhorn

  Volume snapshots (CSI feature):
  apiVersion: snapshot.storage.k8s.io/v1
  kind: VolumeSnapshot
  metadata:
    name: mydata-snapshot
  spec:
    volumeSnapshotClassName: csi-aws-vsc
    source:
      persistentVolumeClaimName: data-pvc

CONCEPT: Kubernetes Networking Deep Dive
------------------------------------------
The Kubernetes networking model requires:
  1. Every pod gets its own unique IP address
  2. Pods on different nodes can communicate without NAT
  3. Agents on nodes can communicate with all pods

CNI Plugin responsibilities:
  - Assign IP addresses to pods
  - Set up routes between pods on different nodes
  - Implement NetworkPolicy enforcement (if supported)

  CNI plugins:
    Flannel:  simple; VXLAN overlay; no NetworkPolicy support
    Calico:   BGP or VXLAN; NetworkPolicy; eBPF mode available
    Cilium:   eBPF-based; L7 NetworkPolicy; Hubble observability; no kube-proxy
    Weave:    encrypted overlay; NetworkPolicy
    Canal:    Flannel + Calico NetworkPolicy
    Antrea:   Open vSwitch; good for vSphere

Service Networking:
  Service VIP is a virtual IP (ClusterIP) that doesn't exist on any interface.
  kube-proxy programs iptables/IPVS rules on every node to intercept traffic
  to the VIP and redirect to a backend pod (DNAT).

  IPVS mode (better for large clusters):
    kubectl edit configmap -n kube-system kube-proxy
    mode: "ipvs"
    ipvs.scheduler: rr/lc/dh/sh/sed/nq
    Benefits: O(1) lookups vs O(n) iptables; more LB algorithms

DNS (CoreDNS):
  Every pod gets DNS configured pointing to CoreDNS service.
  CoreDNS serves:
    <svc-name>.<namespace>.svc.cluster.local → ClusterIP
    <pod-ip-dashes>.<namespace>.pod.cluster.local → pod IP

  Search domains (in pod /etc/resolv.conf):
    search <namespace>.svc.cluster.local svc.cluster.local cluster.local
  This means from within a pod:
    curl http://myservice            → resolves if same namespace
    curl http://myservice.other-ns  → cross-namespace
    curl http://myservice.other-ns.svc.cluster.local → fully qualified

  Headless service DNS (StatefulSet):
    mydb-0.mydb.production.svc.cluster.local → direct pod IP (no VIP)

  CoreDNS customization:
  kubectl edit configmap coredns -n kube-system
  # Add custom DNS entries, forward to different nameservers for specific domains

CONCEPT: NetworkPolicy
------------------------
NetworkPolicy is the Kubernetes firewall. Default: allow all (no NetworkPolicy).
Requires a CNI plugin that enforces policies (Calico, Cilium, Weave, Antrea).

  apiVersion: networking.k8s.io/v1
  kind: NetworkPolicy
  metadata:
    name: api-netpol
    namespace: production
  spec:
    podSelector:
      matchLabels:
        app: api
    policyTypes:
    - Ingress
    - Egress
    ingress:
    - from:
      - namespaceSelector:
          matchLabels:
            name: production
        podSelector:
          matchLabels:
            app: nginx         → AND condition (same element)
      - podSelector:
          matchLabels:
            app: monitoring    → OR condition (different element)
      ports:
      - protocol: TCP
        port: 8080
    egress:
    - to:
      - podSelector:
          matchLabels:
            app: postgres
      ports:
      - protocol: TCP
        port: 5432
    - to: []                   → allow DNS
      ports:
      - protocol: UDP
        port: 53
      - protocol: TCP
        port: 53

  Default deny all (baseline security):
  apiVersion: networking.k8s.io/v1
  kind: NetworkPolicy
  metadata:
    name: default-deny-all
  spec:
    podSelector: {}            → matches ALL pods in namespace
    policyTypes: [Ingress, Egress]
    # No ingress/egress rules = deny all

CONCEPT: Ingress
-----------------
Ingress exposes HTTP/HTTPS routes from outside the cluster to internal services.
Requires an Ingress Controller (nginx-ingress, Traefik, HAProxy, AWS ALB, etc.)

  apiVersion: networking.k8s.io/v1
  kind: Ingress
  metadata:
    name: myapp-ingress
    namespace: production
    annotations:
      nginx.ingress.kubernetes.io/rewrite-target: /
      nginx.ingress.kubernetes.io/ssl-redirect: "true"
      nginx.ingress.kubernetes.io/proxy-body-size: "50m"
      cert-manager.io/cluster-issuer: letsencrypt-prod
  spec:
    ingressClassName: nginx
    tls:
    - hosts:
      - myapp.example.com
      secretName: myapp-tls
    rules:
    - host: myapp.example.com
      http:
        paths:
        - path: /api
          pathType: Prefix
          backend:
            service:
              name: api-service
              port:
                number: 8080
        - path: /
          pathType: Prefix
          backend:
            service:
              name: frontend-service
              port:
                number: 80

  pathType: Exact / Prefix / ImplementationSpecific

CONCEPT: Gateway API (Next-Gen Ingress)
-----------------------------------------
Gateway API is the successor to Ingress. More expressive, extensible, role-based.
Separates concerns: infrastructure (GatewayClass, Gateway) vs routing (HTTPRoute).

  GatewayClass → defines controller (nginx, istio, traefik, aws, etc.)
  Gateway      → L4/L7 listener (like an Ingress Controller instance)
  HTTPRoute    → routing rules for HTTP traffic
  TCPRoute     → TCP routing (non-HTTP)
  TLSRoute     → TLS routing with SNI

  # GatewayClass (cluster-scoped; created by platform team)
  apiVersion: gateway.networking.k8s.io/v1
  kind: GatewayClass
  metadata:
    name: nginx
  spec:
    controllerName: k8s.nginx.org/nginx-gateway-controller

  # Gateway (cluster or namespace scoped; created by infra team)
  apiVersion: gateway.networking.k8s.io/v1
  kind: Gateway
  metadata:
    name: prod-gateway
    namespace: infra
  spec:
    gatewayClassName: nginx
    listeners:
    - name: https
      port: 443
      protocol: HTTPS
      tls:
        mode: Terminate
        certificateRefs:
        - name: prod-tls
      allowedRoutes:
        namespaces:
          from: All

  # HTTPRoute (namespace-scoped; created by app team)
  apiVersion: gateway.networking.k8s.io/v1
  kind: HTTPRoute
  metadata:
    name: myapp-route
    namespace: production
  spec:
    parentRefs:
    - name: prod-gateway
      namespace: infra
    hostnames: ["myapp.example.com"]
    rules:
    - matches:
      - path:
          type: PathPrefix
          value: /api
      backendRefs:
      - name: api-service
        port: 8080
        weight: 100
    - matches:
      - headers:
        - name: X-Canary
          value: "true"
      backendRefs:
      - name: api-service-v2
        port: 8080

CONCEPT: RBAC (Role-Based Access Control)
-------------------------------------------
RBAC controls who can do what in the cluster.

  Resources:
    Role            → namespace-scoped; rules for namespaced resources
    ClusterRole     → cluster-scoped; rules for any resource
    RoleBinding     → binds Role/ClusterRole to subjects IN a namespace
    ClusterRoleBinding → binds ClusterRole to subjects cluster-wide

  Subjects: User, Group, ServiceAccount

  # Role (namespace-scoped)
  apiVersion: rbac.authorization.k8s.io/v1
  kind: Role
  metadata:
    name: pod-reader
    namespace: production
  rules:
  - apiGroups: [""]            → "" = core API group
    resources: ["pods", "pods/log"]
    verbs: ["get", "list", "watch"]
  - apiGroups: ["apps"]
    resources: ["deployments"]
    verbs: ["get", "list", "watch", "update", "patch"]
  - apiGroups: [""]
    resources: ["secrets"]
    resourceNames: ["myapp-secret"]   → restrict to specific named resources
    verbs: ["get"]

  # RoleBinding
  apiVersion: rbac.authorization.k8s.io/v1
  kind: RoleBinding
  metadata:
    name: alice-pod-reader
    namespace: production
  subjects:
  - kind: User
    name: alice
    apiGroup: rbac.authorization.k8s.io
  - kind: Group
    name: dev-team
    apiGroup: rbac.authorization.k8s.io
  - kind: ServiceAccount
    name: ci-runner
    namespace: ci
  roleRef:
    kind: Role
    name: pod-reader
    apiGroup: rbac.authorization.k8s.io

  Least privilege patterns:
    Developers: get/list/watch pods/logs; no secrets; no exec
    CI/CD SA:   create/update deployments; push to registry
    Monitoring: get/list/watch all resources; no write
    Admin:      full access (human only; break-glass)

  Check permissions:
    kubectl auth can-i create pods --as=alice
    kubectl auth can-i create pods --as=system:serviceaccount:ci:ci-runner
    kubectl auth can-i '*' '*' --all-namespaces   → check all

CONCEPT: ServiceAccounts
--------------------------
ServiceAccounts are identities for pods (not humans).
Every pod runs as a SA. Default SA has minimal permissions.

  # Create SA with no automounted token (more secure):
  apiVersion: v1
  kind: ServiceAccount
  metadata:
    name: api-sa
    namespace: production
    annotations:
      eks.amazonaws.com/role-arn: arn:aws:iam::123:role/api-role  → IRSA (AWS)
  automountServiceAccountToken: false

  # Mount SA token only when needed:
  spec:
    serviceAccountName: api-sa
    automountServiceAccountToken: true   → override SA-level setting

  Token projection (short-lived, audience-bound — K8s 1.22+):
  volumes:
  - name: token
    projected:
      sources:
      - serviceAccountToken:
          path: token
          expirationSeconds: 3600
          audience: vault

  IRSA (IAM Roles for Service Accounts) on EKS:
    Annotate SA with IAM role ARN + OIDC → pod assumes AWS IAM role
    No long-lived credentials; token-based; per-pod IAM identity

CONCEPT: Secrets Management Deep Dive
----------------------------------------
  Problem: Base64 is NOT encryption. Secrets in etcd are base64 by default.

  Solution 1: Encryption at Rest (etcd encryption):
    # /etc/kubernetes/encryption-config.yaml
    apiVersion: apiserver.config.k8s.io/v1
    kind: EncryptionConfiguration
    resources:
    - resources: [secrets]
      providers:
      - aescbc:
          keys:
          - name: key1
            secret: <base64-encoded-32-byte-key>
      - identity: {}   → fallback for existing unencrypted secrets

    kube-apiserver flag: --encryption-provider-config=...

  Solution 2: External Secrets Operator (ESO):
    Syncs secrets from external sources (AWS SSM, Vault, GCP Secret Manager)
    into Kubernetes Secrets.

    apiVersion: external-secrets.io/v1beta1
    kind: ExternalSecret
    metadata:
      name: db-credentials
    spec:
      refreshInterval: 1h
      secretStoreRef:
        name: aws-secretsmanager
        kind: ClusterSecretStore
      target:
        name: db-secret           → name of K8s Secret to create
      data:
      - secretKey: password       → key in K8s Secret
        remoteRef:
          key: /prod/db/password  → path in AWS Secrets Manager

  Solution 3: HashiCorp Vault Agent Injector:
    Vault agent sidecar injects secrets as files at runtime.
    Secrets never stored in Kubernetes. Vault handles rotation.

    Annotations on pod:
      vault.hashicorp.com/agent-inject: "true"
      vault.hashicorp.com/agent-inject-secret-config: "secret/data/myapp/config"
      vault.hashicorp.com/role: "myapp"

  Solution 4: Sealed Secrets (Bitnami):
    Encrypt secrets BEFORE committing to git.
    Only the controller in the cluster can decrypt.
    kubeseal --cert pub-cert.pem -o yaml < secret.yaml > sealed-secret.yaml
    Git-safe: sealed secrets can be committed to version control.

CONCEPT: Pod Security
----------------------
Pod Security Admission (PSA) replaced PodSecurityPolicy (PSP deprecated K8s 1.21):

  Three policy levels:
    privileged → no restrictions (for system namespaces)
    baseline   → prevents known privilege escalations
    restricted → hardened (follows security best practices)

  Three modes:
    enforce → reject violating pods
    audit   → allow but log
    warn    → allow but warn user

  Apply via namespace labels:
    kubectl label namespace production \
      pod-security.kubernetes.io/enforce=restricted \
      pod-security.kubernetes.io/audit=restricted \
      pod-security.kubernetes.io/warn=restricted

  Restricted profile requirements:
    - No privileged containers
    - No hostPID, hostIPC, hostNetwork
    - No hostPath volumes
    - Run as non-root
    - Drop ALL capabilities
    - No privilege escalation
    - Seccomp profile set (RuntimeDefault or Localhost)

  Restricted-compliant pod spec:
    spec:
      securityContext:
        runAsNonRoot: true
        runAsUser: 1001
        runAsGroup: 1001
        fsGroup: 1001
        seccompProfile:
          type: RuntimeDefault
      containers:
      - name: app
        securityContext:
          allowPrivilegeEscalation: false
          readOnlyRootFilesystem: true
          capabilities:
            drop: [ALL]
            add: [NET_BIND_SERVICE]  → only if needed

HANDS-ON TASKS (Advanced):
  1. Implement full NetworkPolicy: default-deny + allow specific flows
  2. Configure RBAC for: admin, developer, read-only, CI service account
  3. Deploy app with IRSA on EKS (no AWS keys in pods)
  4. Set up External Secrets Operator with AWS Secrets Manager
  5. Apply Pod Security Admission restricted mode to a namespace
  6. Implement HPA with custom Prometheus metrics
  7. Deploy a StatefulSet with volumeClaimTemplates (per-pod PVC)
  8. Configure Gateway API HTTPRoute with header-based routing

================================================================================
4. EXPERT LEVEL — etcd, HA, Operators, Advanced Patterns
================================================================================

CONCEPT: etcd — Deep Dive & Operations
-----------------------------------------
etcd is the backbone of Kubernetes. Everything depends on it.

Architecture:
  Raft consensus: leader-follower; leader handles all writes
  Quorum: (N/2)+1 nodes needed for cluster health
    3 nodes → tolerate 1 failure (quorum=2)
    5 nodes → tolerate 2 failures (quorum=3)
    7 nodes → tolerate 3 failures (quorum=4)

  etcd performance:
    - Latency-sensitive: use SSD/NVMe disks (never HDD or network storage)
    - Target: <10ms p99 disk fsync latency
    - Isolate etcd disks from other workloads
    - Benchmark: etcd-bench or fio

etcd Operations:
  # CLI (etcdctl):
  export ETCDCTL_API=3
  export ETCDCTL_ENDPOINTS=https://127.0.0.1:2379
  export ETCDCTL_CACERT=/etc/kubernetes/pki/etcd/ca.crt
  export ETCDCTL_CERT=/etc/kubernetes/pki/etcd/peer.crt
  export ETCDCTL_KEY=/etc/kubernetes/pki/etcd/peer.key

  etcdctl member list                    → cluster members
  etcdctl endpoint health                → health check
  etcdctl endpoint status --write-out=table → DB size, revision, leader
  etcdctl get /registry/pods/ --prefix --keys-only  → all pod keys
  etcdctl get /registry/secrets/default/mysecret   → raw secret

  Backup:
  etcdctl snapshot save /backup/etcd-$(date +%Y%m%d_%H%M%S).db
  etcdctl snapshot status /backup/etcd-latest.db  → verify backup

  Restore:
  etcdctl snapshot restore /backup/etcd-latest.db \
    --data-dir=/var/lib/etcd-restore \
    --initial-cluster="master1=https://192.168.1.10:2380" \
    --initial-cluster-token=etcd-cluster-1 \
    --initial-advertise-peer-urls=https://192.168.1.10:2380 \
    --name=master1

  Compaction (reduce etcd size):
  etcdctl compact $(etcdctl endpoint status --write-out=json | jq -r '.[0].Status.revision')
  etcdctl defrag --cluster              → free disk space after compaction

  Monitor: DB size should stay < 8GB (default quota); set --quota-backend-bytes=8589934592

  Encrypt etcd secrets (see encryption-config.yaml above)
  Backup frequency: every 30 min in production; retain 7 days

CONCEPT: Kubernetes HA Architecture
--------------------------------------
  Stacked etcd:        etcd on same machines as control plane (simpler; fewer nodes)
  External etcd:       dedicated etcd cluster (more resilient; more nodes)

  HA Control Plane (3+ masters):
    - Multiple kube-apiserver instances behind a load balancer (HAProxy/ALB/NGINX)
    - kube-controller-manager + kube-scheduler: active-standby (leader election via lease)
    - etcd: quorum-based (3 or 5 nodes)

  kubeadm HA setup:
    kubeadm init --control-plane-endpoint "k8s.example.com:6443" \
      --upload-certs \
      --pod-network-cidr 10.244.0.0/16

    kubeadm join k8s.example.com:6443 \
      --control-plane \
      --certificate-key <key> \
      --token <token> \
      --discovery-token-ca-cert-hash sha256:<hash>

  Certificate management:
    /etc/kubernetes/pki/ → all cluster certificates
    Expiry: 1 year (kubeadm default)
    Rotate: kubeadm certs renew all
    Check: kubeadm certs check-expiration

CONCEPT: Custom Resource Definitions (CRDs) & Operators
---------------------------------------------------------
CRDs extend the Kubernetes API with custom resource types.

  apiVersion: apiextensions.k8s.io/v1
  kind: CustomResourceDefinition
  metadata:
    name: databases.mycompany.io
  spec:
    group: mycompany.io
    versions:
    - name: v1alpha1
      served: true
      storage: true
      schema:
        openAPIV3Schema:
          type: object
          properties:
            spec:
              type: object
              properties:
                engine:
                  type: string
                  enum: [postgres, mysql]
                version:
                  type: string
                replicas:
                  type: integer
                  minimum: 1
    scope: Namespaced
    names:
      plural: databases
      singular: database
      kind: Database
      shortNames: [db]

  # Instance of the CRD:
  apiVersion: mycompany.io/v1alpha1
  kind: Database
  metadata:
    name: prod-db
  spec:
    engine: postgres
    version: "15"
    replicas: 3

Operators:
  An Operator = CRD + Controller. Encodes operational knowledge.
  The controller watches CRD instances and reconciles desired state.

  Operator Pattern:
    1. User creates CR (e.g., Database)
    2. Operator controller detects new CR
    3. Controller creates underlying K8s resources (Deployment, Service, PVC)
    4. Controller continuously reconciles if anything drifts

  Popular Operators:
    cert-manager         → TLS certificate management (Let's Encrypt + others)
    prometheus-operator  → Manages Prometheus/Alertmanager instances
    velero               → backup/restore
    strimzi              → Kafka on Kubernetes
    rook-ceph            → distributed storage
    external-secrets     → secrets synchronization
    argo-rollouts        → advanced deployment strategies
    keda                 → event-driven autoscaling

  Build Operators:
    Kubebuilder (Go)     → scaffolding + code generation; produces controller-runtime based operator
    Operator SDK (Go)    → built on Kubebuilder + Ansible/Helm operators
    Kopf (Python)        → Kubernetes operator framework in Python

CONCEPT: Admission Controllers & Webhooks
-------------------------------------------
Admission controllers intercept API requests AFTER auth but BEFORE persistence.

  Built-in admission controllers:
    NamespaceLifecycle   → prevent operations on terminating namespaces
    LimitRanger          → enforce LimitRange policies
    ResourceQuota        → enforce ResourceQuota
    PodSecurity          → enforce Pod Security Standards
    DefaultStorageClass  → assign default StorageClass to PVCs
    MutatingAdmissionWebhook  → calls external webhook to mutate resources
    ValidatingAdmissionWebhook → calls external webhook to validate resources

  Mutating Webhook (e.g., inject sidecar, set defaults):
    Called before validation. Can modify the resource.
    Example: Istio injects Envoy sidecar via mutating webhook.

  Validating Webhook (e.g., enforce policies):
    Called after mutation. Can only approve/reject. Cannot modify.
    Example: OPA Gatekeeper, Kyverno

  Kyverno Policy (validate + mutate + generate):
    apiVersion: kyverno.io/v1
    kind: ClusterPolicy
    metadata:
      name: require-labels
    spec:
      validationFailureAction: Enforce
      rules:
      - name: check-team-label
        match:
          resources:
            kinds: [Deployment]
        validate:
          message: "Deployment must have 'team' label"
          pattern:
            metadata:
              labels:
                team: "?*"
      - name: disallow-latest-tag
        match:
          resources:
            kinds: [Pod]
        validate:
          message: "Image tag 'latest' is not allowed"
          deny:
            conditions:
            - key: "{{image}}"
              operator: Equals
              value: "*:latest"

CONCEPT: Resource Management at Scale
----------------------------------------
  LimitRange (per namespace — sets defaults and bounds):
    apiVersion: v1
    kind: LimitRange
    metadata:
      name: default-limits
    spec:
      limits:
      - type: Container
        default:
          cpu: "200m"
          memory: "256Mi"
        defaultRequest:
          cpu: "100m"
          memory: "128Mi"
        max:
          cpu: "4"
          memory: "8Gi"
        min:
          cpu: "10m"
          memory: "32Mi"
      - type: PersistentVolumeClaim
        max:
          storage: 100Gi

  ResourceQuota (per namespace — total limits):
    apiVersion: v1
    kind: ResourceQuota
    metadata:
      name: production-quota
    spec:
      hard:
        pods: "50"
        requests.cpu: "20"
        requests.memory: 40Gi
        limits.cpu: "40"
        limits.memory: 80Gi
        persistentvolumeclaims: "20"
        requests.storage: 500Gi
        count/deployments.apps: "30"
        count/services: "30"
        count/secrets: "50"

  PriorityClass (scheduling priority):
    apiVersion: scheduling.k8s.io/v1
    kind: PriorityClass
    metadata:
      name: critical-apps
    value: 1000000
    globalDefault: false
    preemptionPolicy: PreemptLowerPriority
    description: "Critical production apps; will preempt lower priority pods"

    Lower priority pods are evicted to make room for high-priority pods.
    Built-in: system-cluster-critical (2000001000), system-node-critical (2000000000)

CONCEPT: Pod Disruption Budgets (PDB)
----------------------------------------
  Protects availability during voluntary disruptions (node drain, cluster upgrade).

  apiVersion: policy/v1
  kind: PodDisruptionBudget
  metadata:
    name: myapp-pdb
  spec:
    minAvailable: 2          → always keep 2 pods running (use one or the other)
    # maxUnavailable: 1      → allow at most 1 pod down
    selector:
      matchLabels:
        app: myapp

  kubectl drain node1 --ignore-daemonsets → respects PDBs; waits if PDB violated
  kubectl drain blocks until PDB is satisfied or timeout.

  Production: set PDB for ALL production deployments.
  Without PDB, node drain can terminate all replicas simultaneously.

CONCEPT: Advanced Scheduling — Descheduler
--------------------------------------------
  The scheduler makes decisions at pod creation time. Descheduler re-evaluates
  running pods and evicts them for re-scheduling when conditions change.

  Descheduler strategies:
    RemoveDuplicates          → max one pod per node per RS/Deployment
    LowNodeUtilization        → evict pods from over-utilized nodes
    RemovePodsViolatingNodeAffinity → re-evaluate affinity rules
    RemovePodsViolatingInterPodAntiAffinity
    RemovePodsViolatingTopologySpreadConstraint

  Run as CronJob in the cluster.

CONCEPT: Kubernetes API Server — Deep Dive
-------------------------------------------
  API Groups:
    Core (v1):          pods, services, configmaps, secrets, nodes, PVs
    apps/v1:            deployments, replicasets, statefulsets, daemonsets
    batch/v1:           jobs, cronjobs
    networking.k8s.io:  ingress, networkpolicy
    rbac.authorization.k8s.io: roles, rolebindings
    storage.k8s.io:     storageclasses
    autoscaling/v2:     HPA
    policy/v1:          PDB

  API versioning:
    alpha (v1alpha1) → beta (v1beta1) → stable (v1)
    Beta: off-by-default in some versions; may change
    Stable: backwards compatible

  kubectl explain:
    kubectl explain pod
    kubectl explain pod.spec.containers.resources
    kubectl api-resources           → list all resources + API groups
    kubectl api-versions            → list all API versions

  Direct API access:
    kubectl proxy --port=8001 &
    curl http://localhost:8001/api/v1/namespaces/default/pods
    curl http://localhost:8001/apis/apps/v1/namespaces/default/deployments

  Watch (streaming):
    curl http://localhost:8001/api/v1/pods?watch=true → SSE stream of events

CONCEPT: Kubernetes Observability Built-in
--------------------------------------------
  Events:
    kubectl get events -n production --sort-by='.lastTimestamp'
    kubectl get events --field-selector reason=OOMKilling
    Events are stored in etcd; expire after 1 hour (by default)

  kubectl top (requires metrics-server):
    kubectl top nodes
    kubectl top pods -n production --sort-by=memory
    kubectl top pods --containers

  kubectl debug:
    kubectl debug -it pod/mypod --image=busybox:latest --target=app → ephemeral container
    kubectl debug node/node1 -it --image=ubuntu             → debug node via pod

  Ephemeral containers (K8s 1.23+):
    Added to running pods for debugging WITHOUT restart.
    kubectl debug -it --image=busybox --target=app mypod
    Share PID/network/filesystem of target container.

CONCEPT: Multi-Tenancy Patterns
---------------------------------
  Namespace per team (soft isolation):
    + Simple; native K8s; RBAC per namespace
    - Shared node resources; blast radius risk; no network isolation by default
    Combine with: ResourceQuota, LimitRange, NetworkPolicy, PSA, RBAC

  vCluster (virtual clusters):
    Full K8s API server inside a namespace; hard isolation at API level.
    Tenants get full admin in their vCluster; host cluster is unaffected.

  Cluster per team (hard isolation):
    + Maximum isolation; separate etcd, nodes, blast radius
    - Operational overhead; fleet management needed (Cluster API, Rancher)

  Cluster API (CAPI):
    Declarative cluster lifecycle management.
    Create/update/delete clusters via Kubernetes custom resources.
    Providers: AWS (CAPA), Azure (CAPZ), GCP (CAPG), vSphere (CAPV)

CONCEPT: Upgrade Strategy
---------------------------
  kubeadm upgrade:
    kubeadm upgrade plan          → check available versions
    kubeadm upgrade apply v1.29.0 → upgrade control plane
    # Then upgrade each node:
    kubectl drain node1 --ignore-daemonsets
    apt-get install kubeadm=1.29.0 kubelet=1.29.0 kubectl=1.29.0
    kubeadm upgrade node
    systemctl restart kubelet
    kubectl uncordon node1

  Upgrade order: etcd → apiserver → controller-manager → scheduler → kubelet
  Version skew policy: kubelet can be max 2 minor versions behind apiserver
  Always upgrade one minor version at a time (1.27 → 1.28 → 1.29)

HANDS-ON TASKS (Expert):
  1. Back up and restore etcd; verify cluster state preserved
  2. Set up HA control plane with 3 masters + external load balancer
  3. Write a CRD + Operator (use Kubebuilder); reconcile a custom resource
  4. Deploy cert-manager; issue a Let's Encrypt cert for an Ingress
  5. Implement Kyverno policies: require labels, disallow :latest, require resources
  6. Configure etcd encryption at rest; verify secrets are encrypted in etcd
  7. Implement PDB for all production Deployments; test with node drain
  8. Set up KEDA with SQS queue as trigger for autoscaling
  9. Use kubectl debug with ephemeral container to debug a distroless pod
  10. Implement full multi-tenancy: separate namespaces with quota, RBAC, NetworkPolicy, PSA

REAL-WORLD SCENARIOS:
  SCENARIO A — Pod stuck in Pending:
    kubectl describe pod <pod>   → look at Events section
    Common causes + fixes:
      Insufficient CPU/memory   → check node capacity vs requests; scale nodes
      No matching node          → check nodeSelector, affinity, taints
      PVC not bound             → check StorageClass, PV availability
      Image pull failure        → check imagePullSecret, registry access
      Unschedulable             → kubectl describe node (check conditions)

  SCENARIO B — Service not reachable from another pod:
    1. kubectl exec -it debug-pod -- curl http://svc.namespace:port → timeout?
    2. kubectl get endpoints svc → are endpoints populated? (selector match?)
    3. kubectl describe svc → correct selector? correct port?
    4. kubectl get networkpolicy → is there a policy blocking traffic?
    5. kubectl exec pod -- nslookup svc → DNS resolution working?
    6. Check kube-proxy logs: kubectl logs -n kube-system -l k8s-app=kube-proxy

  SCENARIO C — Node goes NotReady:
    kubectl describe node <node> → check Conditions, Taints, Events
    SSH into node:
      systemctl status kubelet → is it running?
      journalctl -u kubelet -n 100 → errors?
      df -h → disk full? (/var/lib/kubelet? /var/lib/containerd?)
      free -h → OOM?
      systemctl status containerd
    Fix: clear disk, restart kubelet/containerd; if unrecoverable: drain + delete node

  SCENARIO D — Memory leak causing OOM kills:
    kubectl get events --field-selector reason=OOMKilling
    kubectl top pods → watch memory over time
    kubectl describe pod <pod> → check restartCount, lastState
    Fix immediate: increase memory limit
    Fix long-term: profile app, fix leak
    Implement: VPA to auto-tune limits; alert on restart count > N
