################################################################################
#                        PHASE 10: AWS (VERY DEEP)                            #
################################################################################

================================================================================
1. FOUNDATION — AWS Core Concepts
================================================================================

CONCEPT: AWS Global Infrastructure
-------------------------------------
  Region:           Geographic area with multiple AZs (e.g., us-east-1)
  Availability Zone: One or more physically separate data centers within a region
                    (e.g., us-east-1a, us-east-1b, us-east-1c)
  Local Zone:       Extension of a region closer to end users (low latency)
  Wavelength Zone:  5G edge locations
  Edge Location:    CloudFront CDN PoPs (400+ globally)

  Design rule: Distribute across ≥3 AZs for high availability.
  Region selection: data residency, latency to users, service availability, pricing.

CONCEPT: AWS Well-Architected Framework (6 Pillars)
-----------------------------------------------------
  Operational Excellence:  automate, make frequent small changes, anticipate failure
  Security:                defense in depth, least privilege, encryption everywhere
  Reliability:             automatically recover, scale horizontally, stop guessing capacity
  Performance Efficiency:  use managed services, go global in minutes
  Cost Optimization:       pay for what you use, right-size, use reserved/spot
  Sustainability:          maximize utilization, use managed services

CONCEPT: AWS IAM — Deep Dive
------------------------------
IAM is the foundation of ALL AWS security. Everything authenticates via IAM.

Identity types:
  IAM User:         Long-lived human or service identity with credentials
  IAM Group:        Collection of users sharing permissions
  IAM Role:         Assumable identity; no long-lived credentials; short-lived tokens
  IAM Policy:       JSON document defining permissions

Authentication:
  Console:          Username + password + MFA
  CLI/SDK:          Access Key ID + Secret Access Key (or role assumption)
  EC2/Lambda:       Instance profile / execution role → auto-rotated via metadata service
  Cross-account:    Role assumption via STS

Policy types:
  Identity-based:   Attached to users/groups/roles (what CAN this identity do?)
  Resource-based:   Attached to resources (who CAN access this resource?) — S3, KMS, SQS, etc.
  Permission boundary: Max permissions an identity can have (guardrails)
  SCP (Service Control Policy): Org-level ceiling; can't be overridden
  Session policy:   Limit permissions for a specific assumed role session
  ACL:              Legacy; use resource policies instead

Policy evaluation order:
  1. Explicit DENY (anywhere) → always denies
  2. SCP → if no SCP allow, deny
  3. Resource-based policy ALLOW → allows for cross-account
  4. Permission boundary → must allow
  5. Identity-based policy ALLOW → allows
  Default: implicit DENY

IAM Policy anatomy:
  {
    "Version": "2012-10-17",
    "Statement": [
      {
        "Sid": "AllowS3ReadForMyBucket",
        "Effect": "Allow",
        "Principal": { "AWS": "arn:aws:iam::123456789:role/myapp" },  → resource policies only
        "Action": [
          "s3:GetObject",
          "s3:ListBucket"
        ],
        "Resource": [
          "arn:aws:s3:::my-bucket",
          "arn:aws:s3:::my-bucket/*"
        ],
        "Condition": {
          "StringEquals": {
            "s3:prefix": ["production/"],
            "aws:RequestedRegion": "us-east-1"
          },
          "Bool": {
            "aws:MultiFactorAuthPresent": "true"
          },
          "IpAddress": {
            "aws:SourceIp": ["10.0.0.0/8"]
          },
          "StringLike": {
            "aws:PrincipalTag/Environment": "production"
          }
        }
      }
    ]
  }

IAM Best Practices:
  - Root account: MFA enabled; no access keys; use only for billing/org tasks
  - All humans: individual IAM users or federated SSO (never share)
  - Services: IAM roles (never embed access keys)
  - Least privilege: start with no permissions; add as needed
  - Permission boundaries: cap developer permissions
  - IAM Access Analyzer: detect unintended external access
  - CloudTrail: log all API calls (including IAM)

  IRSA (IAM Roles for Service Accounts) — EKS:
    K8s ServiceAccount → OIDC token → AWS STS → assume IAM Role
    Zero long-lived credentials inside pods.
    aws eks create-cluster → enable OIDC provider
    eksctl create iamserviceaccount ...
    Annotation: eks.amazonaws.com/role-arn: arn:aws:iam::123:role/myapp

HANDS-ON TASKS (Foundation):
  1. Create IAM role with least-privilege policy for an EKS workload
  2. Configure MFA for all IAM users; enforce via SCP
  3. Create permission boundary limiting developer to specific services/regions
  4. Use IAM Access Analyzer; review and remediate findings
  5. Set up IRSA for an EKS pod accessing S3

================================================================================
2. CORE CONCEPTS — Compute, Networking, Storage
================================================================================

CONCEPT: EC2 — Deep Dive
--------------------------
Instance families:
  General Purpose:    T (burstable), M (balanced CPU/mem)
  Compute Optimized:  C (high CPU) — web servers, batch processing
  Memory Optimized:   R (high RAM) — databases, in-memory cache
  Storage Optimized:  I (NVMe SSD), D (dense HDD), H (high disk throughput)
  Accelerated:        P/G (GPU), Inf (Inferentia), Trn (Trainium)
  Graviton (ARM):     T4g, M7g, C7g, R7g — 40% better price/performance

Instance sizing convention: family + generation + attributes + size
  Example: m7g.2xlarge → m=general purpose, 7=gen7, g=graviton, 2xlarge=size

Purchasing options:
  On-Demand:      Pay per second/hour; no commitment; baseline
  Reserved (RI):  1 or 3 year commitment; up to 72% discount
  Savings Plans:  Flexible commitment to $/hour spend; up to 66% discount
  Spot:           Spare capacity; up to 90% discount; can be interrupted (2-min notice)
  Dedicated Host: Physical server dedicated to you (compliance/licensing)
  Dedicated Instance: Hardware not shared with other accounts

Spot fleet strategy:
  diversify: spread across instance types + AZs to reduce interruption risk
  lowestPrice: cheapest instances (higher interruption risk)
  capacityOptimized: pools with most available capacity (low interruption)

User Data (bootstrap script at launch):
  #!/bin/bash
  set -ex
  yum update -y
  yum install -y docker
  systemctl enable --now docker
  # cloud-init runs this once on first boot

IMDSv2 (Instance Metadata Service v2):
  Mandatory in secure environments (prevents SSRF → credential theft).
  Token-based: must GET a token first, then use token to query metadata.

  TOKEN=$(curl -X PUT "http://169.254.169.254/latest/api/token" \
    -H "X-aws-ec2-metadata-token-ttl-seconds: 21600")
  curl -H "X-aws-ec2-metadata-token: $TOKEN" \
    http://169.254.169.254/latest/meta-data/iam/security-credentials/

  Enforce IMDSv2 on launch: --metadata-options HttpTokens=required
  Enforce via SCP: Deny ec2:RunInstances if HttpTokens != required

EC2 Auto Scaling Group (ASG):
  Maintains N instances between min/max count.
  Scaling policies:
    Target Tracking:    maintain CPU at 60% (adds/removes instances)
    Step Scaling:       add 2 at 70% CPU, add 4 at 85% CPU
    Simple Scaling:     fixed add/remove count; cooldown period
    Scheduled:         scale at specific times
    Predictive:        ML-based; scales before load arrives

  Launch Template (not Launch Config — use templates):
    Defines: AMI, instance type, security groups, key pair, user data, IAM profile
    Supports multiple instance types + purchase options (mixed fleet)

  Instance refresh: rolling replacement of instances (for AMI/config updates)
    aws autoscaling start-instance-refresh --auto-scaling-group-name myasg \
      --preferences MinHealthyPercentage=90,InstanceWarmup=300

  Lifecycle hooks:
    autoscaling:EC2_INSTANCE_LAUNCHING  → run init scripts before in-service
    autoscaling:EC2_INSTANCE_TERMINATING → run drain scripts before termination

CONCEPT: VPC — Deep Dive
--------------------------
VPC = Virtual Private Cloud = your isolated network in AWS.

  CIDR: typically 10.0.0.0/16 (65,536 IPs)
  Subnets: divide VPC CIDR across AZs
    Public subnet:   has route to Internet Gateway; EC2 has public IP
    Private subnet:  no route to IGW; only outbound via NAT Gateway
    Isolated subnet: no outbound internet (databases, internal APIs)

  VPC Components:
    Internet Gateway (IGW):   enables inbound + outbound internet access
    NAT Gateway:              outbound-only internet for private subnets
                              (per-AZ; one per AZ for HA)
    Route Table:              directs traffic (each subnet has one)
    Security Group (SG):      stateful firewall at instance/ENI level
    Network ACL (NACL):       stateless firewall at subnet level
    VPC Flow Logs:            capture network traffic metadata
    VPC Endpoint:             private connectivity to AWS services (no internet)
    VPC Peering:              connect two VPCs (non-transitive)
    Transit Gateway (TGW):    hub-and-spoke for many VPCs (transitive routing)

  Subnet design (per AZ):
    Public:   10.0.0.0/24, 10.0.1.0/24, 10.0.2.0/24   → LBs, Bastion hosts
    Private:  10.0.10.0/23, 10.0.12.0/23, 10.0.14.0/23 → App servers, EKS nodes
    Isolated: 10.0.20.0/24, 10.0.21.0/24, 10.0.22.0/24 → RDS, ElastiCache

  Security Groups (stateful):
    Return traffic automatically allowed.
    Rules: allow only; no explicit deny.
    Can reference other SGs as source (self-referencing for clusters).

    Inbound rules: what comes IN to the resource
    Outbound rules: what goes OUT from the resource

    Best practice: layered SGs
      ALB SG:    inbound 443 from 0.0.0.0/0
      App SG:    inbound 8080 from ALB SG only
      DB SG:     inbound 5432 from App SG only

  Network ACL (stateless):
    Explicit allow AND deny. Rules evaluated in order (lowest number first).
    Both inbound AND outbound rules needed for each flow.
    Mostly used for broad subnet-level deny (DDoS, compliance).

  VPC Endpoints:
    Gateway endpoint:   S3 + DynamoDB only; no charge; route-table based
    Interface endpoint: All other services; elastic network interface; costs money
      Use to keep traffic within AWS network (no internet exit).

    aws ec2 create-vpc-endpoint \
      --vpc-id vpc-abc123 \
      --service-name com.amazonaws.us-east-1.s3 \
      --route-table-ids rtb-abc123    → Gateway endpoint

  Transit Gateway:
    Central hub routing between VPCs + on-premises (VPN/Direct Connect).
    Supports: route tables, VPN attachments, Direct Connect GW attachments.
    More scalable than VPC Peering (which doesn't scale beyond ~10 VPCs).

CONCEPT: EKS — Deep Dive
--------------------------
EKS = managed Kubernetes control plane.
AWS manages: apiserver, etcd, controller-manager, scheduler HA.
You manage: worker nodes, add-ons, VPC config.

  Cluster creation:
    eksctl create cluster \
      --name prod \
      --region us-east-1 \
      --version 1.29 \
      --nodegroup-name standard \
      --node-type m7g.2xlarge \
      --nodes-min 3 \
      --nodes-max 20 \
      --managed                → managed node group (AWS patches the node OS)

  Node types:
    Managed node group:   AWS manages lifecycle; auto-drain on termination; SSM support
    Self-managed:         full control; custom AMI; more operational burden
    Fargate:              serverless pods; per-pod billing; no node management

  EKS Add-ons (managed by AWS; auto-updated):
    vpc-cni:              AWS VPC CNI (pods get VPC IPs)
    kube-proxy:
    coredns:
    aws-ebs-csi-driver:   EBS persistent volumes
    aws-efs-csi-driver:   EFS persistent volumes (RWX)
    aws-load-balancer-controller: ALB/NLB from K8s Ingress/Service

  AWS VPC CNI:
    Each pod gets a real VPC IP address (from ENI secondary IPs).
    Benefits: VPC routing, SGs per pod, VPC Flow Logs for pods.
    IPAMD: daemon managing IP address allocation from ENIs.
    Max pods per node = (max ENIs × (IPs per ENI - 1)) + 2
    Prefix delegation: /28 CIDR prefix per ENI slot → ~16× more pods per node

  Karpenter (node autoscaler — modern replacement for Cluster Autoscaler):
    Provisions right-sized nodes just-in-time based on pending pod requirements.
    Supports mixed instance types + AZs + purchase options in one NodePool.
    Faster than Cluster Autoscaler (< 1 min vs several minutes).

    # NodePool:
    apiVersion: karpenter.sh/v1beta1
    kind: NodePool
    metadata:
      name: default
    spec:
      template:
        metadata:
          labels:
            karpenter.sh/nodepool: default
        spec:
          requirements:
          - key: karpenter.k8s.aws/instance-category
            operator: In
            values: [c, m, r]
          - key: karpenter.k8s.aws/instance-generation
            operator: Gt
            values: ["5"]
          - key: kubernetes.io/arch
            operator: In
            values: [amd64, arm64]
          - key: karpenter.sh/capacity-type
            operator: In
            values: [on-demand, spot]
          nodeClassRef:
            apiVersion: karpenter.k8s.aws/v1beta1
            kind: EC2NodeClass
            name: default
      disruption:
        consolidationPolicy: WhenUnderutilized    → consolidate nodes
        consolidateAfter: 30s
      limits:
        cpu: "1000"
        memory: 2000Gi

  EKS Security:
    Cluster endpoint: private + public (restrict public to known CIDRs)
    Node SG: only allow traffic from control plane + same SG
    IRSA: never use instance profile for pod permissions
    Envelope encryption: encrypt K8s secrets with KMS
    GuardDuty EKS Protection: detect threats in K8s audit logs
    aws-auth ConfigMap (legacy) → EKS access entries (new API, preferred)

CONCEPT: S3 — Deep Dive
-------------------------
S3 is object storage. Not a filesystem (despite being usable like one).

  Core concepts:
    Bucket:     global namespace container for objects
    Object:     file + metadata; max 5TB; key = full path
    Key:        unique identifier within bucket (e.g., "images/2024/photo.jpg")
    Prefix:     key prefix for pseudo-directory listing
    Multipart:  required for objects > 5GB; recommended > 100MB

  Storage classes (cost vs retrieval tradeoff):
    Standard:               frequent access; millisecond retrieval
    Intelligent-Tiering:    auto-tier based on access patterns; no retrieval fee
    Standard-IA:            infrequent access; cheaper storage; retrieval fee
    One Zone-IA:            single AZ; 20% cheaper than Standard-IA; less durable
    Glacier Instant:        archive; millisecond retrieval; much cheaper
    Glacier Flexible:       archive; 1-5 min (Expedited) to 3-5 hr (Standard) retrieval
    Glacier Deep Archive:   cheapest; 12 hr retrieval; long-term retention

  Lifecycle policies:
    Transition: Standard → Standard-IA after 30 days → Glacier after 90 days
    Expiration: delete objects after 365 days
    Abort incomplete multipart uploads: after 7 days

  Bucket policies vs IAM:
    Public access: bucket policy with Principal: "*"
    Cross-account: bucket policy grants + IAM allows in source account
    Same account: IAM policy alone sufficient (bucket policy optional)

  S3 Security:
    Block Public Access: enable all 4 settings on all buckets (default now)
    Bucket policy: deny HTTP (require HTTPS):
      Condition: "aws:SecureTransport": "false" → Effect: Deny
    Server-side encryption:
      SSE-S3:    AWS-managed keys (free)
      SSE-KMS:   AWS KMS keys (audit trail; per-request cost)
      SSE-C:     customer-provided keys
      DSSE-KMS:  dual-layer encryption
    In-transit: HTTPS (TLS 1.2+)
    VPC Endpoint + bucket policy:
      Condition: aws:SourceVpc = vpc-abc123 → restrict to VPC only

  Object Lock (WORM — Write Once Read Many):
    Governance mode: can be removed by privileged users
    Compliance mode: cannot be removed by anyone (including root)
    Legal hold: indefinite retention (overrides retention dates)
    Use for: audit logs, compliance data that must not be tampered with

  Versioning:
    Once enabled, cannot be disabled (only suspended).
    MFA delete: require MFA to permanently delete versions.

  Replication:
    CRR: Cross-Region Replication (DR, data residency)
    SRR: Same-Region Replication (log aggregation, multiple accounts)
    Requires versioning on both source and destination.

  S3 Event Notifications:
    Triggers: s3:ObjectCreated:*, s3:ObjectRemoved:*, s3:ObjectRestore:*
    Destinations: Lambda, SQS, SNS, EventBridge

  Performance:
    Prefix-based partitioning (no longer an issue; AWS fixed in 2018)
    Multi-part upload for large files: 8-25 MB per part recommended
    Transfer Acceleration: CloudFront edge → S3 (global upload optimization)
    S3 Select: SQL query on CSV/JSON/Parquet data in place (no download)

CONCEPT: RDS — Deep Dive
--------------------------
RDS = managed relational database (MySQL, PostgreSQL, MariaDB, Oracle, SQL Server, Db2).
Aurora = AWS cloud-native database (MySQL/PostgreSQL compatible; 5× MySQL performance).

  RDS vs Aurora:
    RDS: traditional engine; storage tightly coupled to compute
    Aurora: storage auto-scales 10GB → 128TB; 6-way replication across 3 AZs
            Aurora Global: replication to 5 regions in <1s (RPO~1s, RTO<1min)
            Aurora Serverless v2: auto-scales compute from 0.5 ACU to 256 ACU

  Multi-AZ:
    Synchronous standby in different AZ.
    Automatic failover in 60-120s (RDS) or <30s (Aurora).
    Standby is NOT readable (use read replicas for read scaling).

  Read Replicas:
    Asynchronous replication; readable; can be in different regions.
    Up to 15 Aurora replicas (5 for RDS).
    Promote to standalone DB for disaster recovery.

  Subnet Group: RDS must be in ≥2 AZs; subnets define which AZs.
  Parameter Group: DB engine configuration (e.g., max_connections, shared_buffers)
  Option Group: additional features (e.g., Oracle options, MySQL audit plugin)

  Security:
    Always in private subnet (no public IP)
    Security Group: allow only from app SG
    Encryption at rest: KMS (enable at creation; cannot add later to unencrypted)
    Encryption in transit: enforce SSL via parameter group
    IAM authentication: token-based login (no password); rotate automatically
    Secrets Manager: rotate DB password automatically (built-in integration)

  Backup:
    Automated: 1-35 days; point-in-time recovery (PITR) to any second
    Manual snapshot: user-initiated; retained until deleted
    Aurora Backtrack: rewind DB in-place (without restore) up to 72 hours

  Enhanced Monitoring + Performance Insights:
    OS metrics: CPU, memory, disk, network (1-second granularity)
    Performance Insights: DB load analysis; top SQL queries; wait events

CONCEPT: ElastiCache
----------------------
Managed Redis and Memcached.

  Redis Cluster mode disabled (single shard):
    One primary + up to 5 read replicas
    Multi-AZ failover available
    Use for: session store, leaderboards, Pub/Sub

  Redis Cluster mode enabled (multiple shards):
    Horizontally partitioned data across 1-500 shards
    Each shard has primary + replicas
    Online resharding supported

  ElastiCache Security:
    VPC only; private subnets
    Encryption at rest + in transit (TLS)
    AUTH token (Redis password)
    RBAC (Redis 6+): user-level ACLs

HANDS-ON TASKS (Core):
  1. Create a production-grade VPC (public/private/isolated subnets, NAT GW, VPC endpoints)
  2. Create EKS cluster with managed node groups + Karpenter
  3. Configure IRSA for a pod that reads from S3
  4. Set up S3 bucket with lifecycle, versioning, replication, Object Lock
  5. Deploy RDS Aurora PostgreSQL Multi-AZ with Secrets Manager rotation
  6. Set up Transit Gateway connecting 3 VPCs

================================================================================
3. ADVANCED — Load Balancing, CDN, Messaging, Security Services
================================================================================

CONCEPT: Elastic Load Balancing
---------------------------------
  ALB (Application Load Balancer):
    Layer 7 (HTTP/HTTPS/gRPC/WebSocket).
    Target types: instances, IPs, Lambda functions.
    Path-based routing: /api/* → api-target-group
    Host-based routing: api.example.com → api-tg
    Header/query-based routing: any HTTP attributes
    Weighted target groups: blue/green + canary
    Authentication: OIDC + Cognito (built-in)
    WAF integration
    Access logs → S3

  NLB (Network Load Balancer):
    Layer 4 (TCP/UDP/TLS).
    Ultra-low latency, millions of requests/s.
    Static IP per AZ (or Elastic IP).
    Preserves client IP.
    Use for: non-HTTP, gaming, financial services, VPC Link (API Gateway).

  GWLB (Gateway Load Balancer):
    Layer 3 + 4. Routes traffic through virtual appliances (firewalls, IDS/IPS).
    Uses GENEVE protocol. Transparent to source/destination.

  Target Groups:
    Health check: protocol, path, interval, threshold
    Deregistration delay: 300s (wait for in-flight requests before removing target)
    Stickiness: load balancer or application cookie

  AWS Load Balancer Controller (for Kubernetes):
    Ingress → ALB (per-Ingress or shared ALB via IngressGroup)
    Service (LoadBalancer) → NLB
    Annotations control ALB/NLB behavior:
      alb.ingress.kubernetes.io/scheme: internet-facing
      alb.ingress.kubernetes.io/target-type: ip       → direct pod IPs
      alb.ingress.kubernetes.io/certificate-arn: arn:aws:acm:...
      alb.ingress.kubernetes.io/group.name: myapp     → shared ALB

CONCEPT: CloudFront
---------------------
CDN with 400+ edge locations. Not just for static content.

  Origins: S3, ALB, EC2, API Gateway, custom HTTP (any IP)
  Behaviors: cache policies + origin request policies per path pattern

  Cache Policy:
    CachingOptimized: default; S3/static content
    CachingDisabled: dynamic API responses
    Custom: TTL range + which headers/cookies/query strings vary cache key

  Origin Request Policy:
    What headers/cookies/query strings to forward to origin.
    AllViewer: forward everything (no caching benefits)

  Security:
    OAC (Origin Access Control): restrict S3 to CloudFront only (replaces OAI)
    SSL/TLS: ACM certificate (must be in us-east-1 for CloudFront)
    WAF v2: attach to CloudFront for global edge protection
    Geo restriction: block/allow by country
    Field-level encryption: encrypt specific form fields at edge
    Signed URLs/Cookies: time-limited private content access

  Lambda@Edge + CloudFront Functions:
    Lambda@Edge: full Lambda at 4 lifecycle points (viewer/origin request/response)
                 Use for: auth, URL rewrites, A/B testing (50ms timeout)
    CloudFront Functions: lightweight JS at viewer request/response
                          Use for: URL normalization, header manipulation (<1ms)

CONCEPT: Route 53
------------------
AWS DNS service + domain registration.

  Record types:
    A:      IPv4 address
    AAAA:   IPv6 address
    CNAME:  canonical name (alias; only for non-apex)
    ALIAS:  AWS-specific; like CNAME but works at apex; no extra DNS lookup
            Use for: apex domain (example.com) → ALB/CloudFront/S3
    MX:     mail exchange
    TXT:    arbitrary text (SPF, DKIM, domain verification)
    SRV:    service record

  Routing policies:
    Simple:         single record; basic
    Weighted:       split traffic by percentage (canary, A/B)
    Latency:        route to lowest latency region
    Failover:       primary + secondary; health check driven
    Geolocation:    route based on user's country/continent
    Geoproximity:   route based on distance + bias (Traffic Flow required)
    Multi-value:    return up to 8 healthy records; client-side LB
    IP-based:       route based on CIDR ranges

  Health checks:
    HTTP/HTTPS/TCP endpoint or CloudWatch alarm.
    String matching (look for keyword in response).
    Route 53 checks from multiple locations globally.
    Required for failover routing.

  Private Hosted Zone:
    Internal DNS resolution within VPC(s).
    Associate with multiple VPCs (same or different regions).
    Custom domain names for internal services.

CONCEPT: SQS, SNS, EventBridge
---------------------------------
SQS (Simple Queue Service):
  Decouples producers from consumers. Managed message queue.

  Standard Queue:  at-least-once delivery; best-effort ordering; near-unlimited throughput
  FIFO Queue:      exactly-once; strict order; 3,000 msg/s (with batching)

  Key settings:
    Visibility Timeout:     time a consumer has to process + delete message (default 30s)
    Message Retention:      1 min to 14 days (default 4 days)
    Dead Letter Queue (DLQ): unprocessable messages after N retries
    Long Polling:           wait up to 20s for message (reduces empty receives)
    Delay Queue:            postpone delivery of new messages

  SQS + Lambda: Lambda polls SQS; scales to match queue depth
  SQS + ECS/EKS + KEDA: KEDA scales pods based on queue depth

SNS (Simple Notification Service):
  Pub/Sub messaging. One message → multiple subscribers.
  Subscriptions: SQS, Lambda, HTTP/S, email, SMS, Kinesis Firehose
  Fan-out pattern: SNS topic → multiple SQS queues
  Message filtering: subscription filter policies (only receive relevant messages)

EventBridge:
  Serverless event bus. Route events between AWS services + custom apps.
  Event Pattern matching: route to different targets based on event content.
  Scheduled Rules: cron-based event generation (replacement for CloudWatch Events).
  Pipes: connect source (SQS, DynamoDB Streams) → optional transform → target.
  Schema Registry: auto-discovers event schemas; generates code bindings.

CONCEPT: Lambda — Deep Dive
-----------------------------
Function-as-a-service. Event-driven compute.

  Execution model:
    Init phase:  cold start; download code; init runtime; run init code
    Invoke phase: run handler function
    Shutdown:    freeze execution environment (may be reused for next invoke)

  Cold start mitigation:
    Provisioned Concurrency: keep N environments warm (cost)
    Snap Start (Java): restore from snapshot instead of full init
    Use lighter runtimes (Python/Node faster than Java/Go/C#)
    Minimize init code (move heavy imports inside handler if rarely needed)
    Keep function package small

  Triggers:
    API Gateway, ALB, CloudFront, S3, DynamoDB Streams, Kinesis, SQS, SNS,
    EventBridge, Cognito, IoT, Step Functions, etc.

  Limits:
    Timeout: max 15 minutes (default 3 seconds — always increase!)
    Memory: 128MB to 10GB (CPU scales proportionally)
    Concurrent executions: 1,000 per region (soft limit; request increase)
    Deployment package: 50MB zipped / 250MB unzipped / 10GB container image
    /tmp storage: 512MB to 10GB

  VPC Lambda:
    Lambda can run inside a VPC (access RDS, ElastiCache).
    Creates ENI in your subnet — ensure enough IPs.
    Adds cold start latency (pre-allocated ENI pool mitigates since 2020).

  Lambda Layers:
    Shared code/libraries across multiple functions.
    Up to 5 layers per function.
    Max unzipped size including function: 250MB.

  Lambda Container Images:
    Package function as Docker image (up to 10GB).
    Custom runtimes, large ML models, binary dependencies.
    Cached at edge; warm starts benefit from layer caching.

  Lambda Power Tuning:
    AWS Step Functions state machine that tests function with different memory sizes.
    Finds optimal cost/performance tradeoff.

CONCEPT: API Gateway
----------------------
  REST API:     full-featured; caching; usage plans; API keys; request/response transformation
  HTTP API:     simpler; cheaper (~70% less); JWT auth; no request transformation; faster
  WebSocket:    bidirectional; connection management

  Integration types:
    Lambda:         sync or async invocation
    HTTP:           proxy to external HTTP endpoint
    AWS Service:    directly call AWS APIs (SQS, Step Functions, etc.)
    Mock:           return hardcoded response (testing)

  Authentication:
    Cognito User Pools: JWT validation
    Lambda Authorizer:  custom auth logic in Lambda (token or request-based)
    IAM:               SigV4 signing

  Throttling:
    Account level: 10,000 req/s per region (soft)
    Stage level: per-method throttling
    Usage Plans + API Keys: per-customer rate limiting

HANDS-ON TASKS (Advanced):
  1. Create ALB with path-based routing; deploy EKS Ingress with ALB controller
  2. Set up CloudFront distribution in front of S3 + ALB with WAF
  3. Implement SNS fan-out → SQS → Lambda pipeline
  4. Create Lambda in VPC reading from RDS; handle cold starts
  5. Set up API Gateway with Lambda authorizer + Cognito
  6. Configure Route 53 failover routing with health checks

================================================================================
4. EXPERT LEVEL — Security Services, Monitoring, Cost, Advanced Patterns
================================================================================

CONCEPT: AWS KMS — Deep Dive
------------------------------
KMS manages cryptographic keys. Used by almost all AWS services.

  Key types:
    AWS Managed Key:    created by AWS for a service (e.g., aws/s3); free; can't control
    Customer Managed Key (CMK): you create/control; $1/month + API call charges
    Customer Provided Key Material: import your own key material
    Multi-Region Key:   same key ID in multiple regions (no re-encryption for DR)

  Key policies (resource-based policy on the key):
    {
      "Sid": "AllowKeyUsage",
      "Effect": "Allow",
      "Principal": { "AWS": "arn:aws:iam::123:role/myapp" },
      "Action": ["kms:GenerateDataKey", "kms:Decrypt"],
      "Resource": "*",
      "Condition": {
        "StringEquals": { "kms:ViaService": "s3.us-east-1.amazonaws.com" }
      }
    }

  Envelope encryption:
    KMS does NOT encrypt your data directly (expensive API calls, 4KB limit).
    Instead:
    1. Generate Data Key (GenerateDataKey) → plaintext key + encrypted key
    2. Encrypt data locally with plaintext key
    3. Discard plaintext key; store encrypted key with data
    4. To decrypt: call KMS Decrypt on encrypted key → get plaintext → decrypt data
    Only 2 KMS API calls per encryption/decryption cycle.

  Key rotation:
    AWS Managed: auto-rotated every year (cannot disable)
    CMK: optional auto-rotation every year (key material changes; old versions kept for decrypt)

  KMS Grants: temporary delegated access to a key (for AWS services to use on your behalf)
  KMS Aliases: human-readable key names (alias/my-key → key ARN)

  CloudTrail logs every KMS API call (who used what key, when, from where).

CONCEPT: AWS Secrets Manager
------------------------------
Manages, rotates, and retrieves secrets.

  Store: API keys, DB passwords, OAuth tokens, TLS certs.
  Automatic rotation: built-in Lambda for RDS, Redshift, DocumentDB.
                      Custom Lambda for any secret type.
  Cross-account access: resource-based policy on secret.
  Versioning: AWSCURRENT / AWSPREVIOUS / AWSPENDING (during rotation).
  Caching SDK: cache secrets in memory; reduces API calls + cost.

  vs Systems Manager Parameter Store:
    SecretsMgr: designed for secrets; automatic rotation; $0.40/secret/month
    SSM Param Store Standard: free; 10KB limit; no auto-rotation
    SSM Param Store Advanced: $0.05/param/month; 8KB limit; manual rotation
    Use SSM for config; Secrets Manager for secrets requiring rotation.

CONCEPT: CloudTrail
---------------------
Records all API calls to AWS (management plane + data plane optionally).

  Trail types:
    Management events: control plane (CreateBucket, RunInstances, etc.) — default
    Data events:      S3 object-level (GetObject, PutObject), Lambda invocations, DynamoDB
    Insights events:  unusual API activity detection

  S3 → CloudTrail Logs → Athena for SQL queries:
    SELECT eventSource, eventName, userIdentity.arn, sourceIPAddress
    FROM cloudtrail_logs
    WHERE errorCode IS NOT NULL
    AND eventTime > '2024-01-01'
    ORDER BY eventTime DESC

  CloudTrail + EventBridge: real-time alerts on specific API calls.
    Pattern: { "source": ["aws.iam"], "detail-type": ["AWS API Call via CloudTrail"],
               "detail": { "eventName": ["CreateUser", "AttachUserPolicy"] } }
    Target: SNS → alert on unauthorized IAM changes.

  CloudTrail Lake: SQL-queryable event data store (retain 7 years; no S3 needed).

CONCEPT: AWS CloudWatch — Deep Dive
-------------------------------------
CloudWatch = metrics + logs + alarms + dashboards + events.

  Metrics:
    Namespace: AWS/EC2, AWS/RDS, custom (CWAgent, app metrics)
    Dimensions: filter/group (InstanceId, AutoScalingGroupName, etc.)
    Resolution: 1-second (high resolution) or 60-second (standard)
    Retention: 15 months (high res: 3 hours); aggregated over time

  Useful EC2 metrics:
    CPUUtilization, NetworkIn/Out, DiskReadOps/WriteOps
    NOT: memory, disk usage (need CloudWatch Agent)

  CloudWatch Agent:
    Collects OS-level metrics: memory, disk, swap, processes.
    Collects logs from files and journald.
    Config via SSM Parameter Store (centralized agent config).

  CloudWatch Logs:
    Log Group → Log Stream → Log Events
    Retention: 1 day to indefinitely (set retention; default = never expire)
    Metric Filters: extract metrics from log patterns
    Insights: SQL-like query language for log analysis

    # CloudWatch Logs Insights query:
    fields @timestamp, @message, @logStream
    | filter @message like /ERROR/
    | stats count(*) as errorCount by bin(5m)
    | sort errorCount desc

  CloudWatch Alarms:
    Threshold:  metric > value for N evaluation periods
    Anomaly Detection: ML-based; alert on unusual patterns
    Composite: combine multiple alarms (AND/OR logic)
    Actions: SNS → PagerDuty/Slack/email; Auto Scaling; EC2 recover/reboot

  Embedded Metrics Format (EMF):
    Log JSON with specific fields → CloudWatch auto-extracts as metrics.
    No PutMetricData API calls; metrics embedded in logs.
    { "_aws": { "Timestamp": 1234, "Metrics": [{"Name":"Latency","Unit":"ms"}] },
      "Latency": 42, "Service": "api", "Environment": "prod" }

CONCEPT: AWS GuardDuty
------------------------
Continuous threat detection using ML + threat intelligence.

  Data sources:
    CloudTrail management events
    VPC Flow Logs
    DNS logs
    S3 data events
    EKS audit logs (EKS Protection)
    RDS login activity (RDS Protection)
    Lambda network activity (Lambda Protection)
    Malware scanning (EC2 + S3)

  Finding types:
    Backdoor: EC2 communicating with known C2 servers
    CryptoCurrency: mining activity detected
    Recon: port scanning, credential brute force
    Stealth: CloudTrail logging disabled, password policy weakened
    Trojan: data exfiltration patterns
    UnauthorizedAccess: unusual API calls, root credential use

  Suppression Rules: suppress false positives (known pentest IPs, etc.)
  Remediation: EventBridge → Lambda → auto-isolate (SG change) compromised instance

CONCEPT: AWS Security Hub
--------------------------
Aggregates findings from GuardDuty, Inspector, Macie, IAM Access Analyzer,
Firewall Manager, 3rd party tools. Provides security posture score.

  Security Standards: CIS AWS Benchmarks, AWS Foundational Best Practices, PCI DSS, NIST.
  Cross-account aggregation: all accounts → management account findings.
  Automated remediation: Security Hub + EventBridge + Lambda.

CONCEPT: AWS Inspector
------------------------
Automated vulnerability assessment.
  EC2: OS CVEs (via SSM Agent)
  Container images: ECR scanning (on push + continuous)
  Lambda: code + package vulnerability scanning

  Findings: CVE details, CVSS score, affected packages, remediation.
  Integration: Security Hub for aggregation.

CONCEPT: ECR — Elastic Container Registry
-------------------------------------------
  Create repository:
    aws ecr create-repository \
      --repository-name myapp \
      --image-scanning-configuration scanOnPush=true \
      --encryption-configuration encryptionType=KMS \
      --region us-east-1

  Authentication:
    aws ecr get-login-password --region us-east-1 | \
      docker login --username AWS --password-stdin 123.dkr.ecr.us-east-1.amazonaws.com

  Cross-account access (registry policy):
    aws ecr set-repository-policy \
      --repository-name myapp \
      --policy-text file://ecr-policy.json

  Lifecycle policies (auto-cleanup):
    {
      "rules": [
        {
          "rulePriority": 1,
          "description": "Keep last 10 tagged images",
          "selection": {
            "tagStatus": "tagged",
            "tagPrefixList": ["v"],
            "countType": "imageCountMoreThan",
            "countNumber": 10
          },
          "action": { "type": "expire" }
        },
        {
          "rulePriority": 2,
          "description": "Delete untagged images older than 1 day",
          "selection": {
            "tagStatus": "untagged",
            "countType": "sinceImagePushed",
            "countUnit": "days",
            "countNumber": 1
          },
          "action": { "type": "expire" }
        }
      ]
    }

  Pull-through cache: cache Docker Hub / Quay / GitHub Container Registry in ECR.
    Reduces external dependencies; avoids rate limits.
    aws ecr create-pull-through-cache-rule \
      --ecr-repository-prefix dockerhub \
      --upstream-registry-url registry-1.docker.io

CONCEPT: ECS — Elastic Container Service
------------------------------------------
  Task Definition: blueprint (like Pod spec): containers, CPU/memory, networking, IAM role.
  Task:            running instance of a task definition.
  Service:         desired count + load balancer integration + rolling updates.
  Cluster:         logical grouping (EC2 or Fargate).

  Launch types:
    EC2:    you manage nodes; more control; bin packing
    Fargate: serverless; per-task billing; no node management; AWS manages infra

  ECS Service Discovery: Route 53 Cloud Map for service-to-service communication.

  ECS vs EKS: ECS is simpler; EKS is more powerful + portable.
  Use ECS for: simple container workloads, Fargate-first, tight AWS integration.
  Use EKS for: Kubernetes ecosystem, complex scheduling, operators, service mesh.

CONCEPT: Infrastructure as Code Deployment Patterns
------------------------------------------------------
  CloudFormation:
    Native AWS IaC. JSON/YAML templates.
    Stacks: unit of deployment.
    StackSets: deploy across multiple accounts + regions.
    Change Sets: preview changes before applying.
    Drift Detection: identify manual changes.
    No dependency on external tools; free.

  CDK (Cloud Development Kit):
    Define AWS infrastructure in TypeScript/Python/Java/Go.
    Synthesizes to CloudFormation.
    L1: CloudFormation constructs (1:1 mapping)
    L2: opinionated constructs with sensible defaults
    L3: patterns (e.g., ApplicationLoadBalancedFargateService)

  Terraform (Phase 11) + Terragrunt: see next phase.

CONCEPT: AWS Cost Optimization
--------------------------------
  Tagging strategy (foundation for cost allocation):
    Required tags: Environment, Team, Project, CostCenter
    Enforce via SCP + AWS Config
    aws resourcegroupstaggingapi get-resources --tag-filters Key=Environment

  Cost analysis tools:
    Cost Explorer:    visualize spend by service, account, tag, region
    AWS Budgets:      alert when spend exceeds threshold
    Compute Optimizer: right-sizing recommendations (EC2, Lambda, ECS, EBS)
    Trusted Advisor:  cost + security + performance recommendations

  EC2 Savings Plans vs Reserved Instances:
    Savings Plans more flexible: commit to $/hour; applies to EC2 + Lambda + Fargate
    EC2 RI: specific instance family; less flexible; higher discount

  Spot Instance strategies:
    Use for: batch jobs, CI/CD agents, stateless web tier, ML training
    diversify across instance types + AZs
    Use Spot interruption handler (SIGTERM → drain + checkpoint)
    eks/karpenter: mix spot + on-demand per NodePool

  Right-sizing:
    Compute Optimizer → oversized/undersized resource recommendations
    EC2: check CPUUtilization < 20% for weeks → right-size down
    Lambda: AWS Lambda Power Tuning
    RDS: check CPU + memory; scale instance class

REAL-WORLD SCENARIOS:
  SCENARIO A — EKS cluster security hardening:
    1. Enable control plane logging (api, audit, authenticator, controllerManager, scheduler)
    2. Restrict endpoint public access to VPN CIDR only
    3. Enable GuardDuty EKS Protection + Inspector for ECR
    4. Enforce IMDSv2 on all nodes (LaunchTemplate: MetadataOptions.HttpTokens=required)
    5. Enable envelope encryption for K8s secrets (KMS)
    6. Use IRSA for all workloads (no instance profile for pod permissions)
    7. Enable VPC Flow Logs + CloudTrail data events for EKS cluster
    8. Security Hub: review CIS Kubernetes Benchmark findings

  SCENARIO B — Production incident: S3 bucket publicly exposed:
    1. GuardDuty / Security Hub alert fires
    2. Immediate: aws s3api put-public-access-block → block all public access
    3. Check bucket policy + ACL for unintended grants
    4. CloudTrail: who changed bucket policy? When?
       SELECT * FROM cloudtrail WHERE eventName = 'PutBucketPolicy'
         AND requestParameters LIKE '%mybucket%'
    5. Review S3 access logs: what was accessed while public?
    6. Incident report: timeline, data accessed, remediation, prevention
    7. Prevention: SCP to deny s3:PutBucketPublicAccessBlock + mandatory tagging

  SCENARIO C — Cost spike investigation:
    1. Cost Explorer: which service increased? Which account? Which region?
    2. EC2: check for unexpected instances (rogue, forgotten, over-sized)
    3. S3: check data transfer costs (CloudFront vs direct S3; inter-region)
    4. RDS: check for dev/test instances running 24/7 (stop with schedule)
    5. EKS: check for Karpenter nodes that didn't scale down (consolidation)
    6. Data transfer: biggest cost; use VPC endpoints, keep traffic in-region
    7. Set Budget alert at 110% of previous month's spend

################################################################################
#                   PHASE 11: TERRAFORM + TERRAGRUNT                          #

================================================================================
1. FOUNDATION — Terraform Core Concepts
================================================================================

CONCEPT: What Terraform Does
------------------------------
Terraform is an Infrastructure as Code (IaC) tool that:
  - Declares desired infrastructure state in HCL (HashiCorp Configuration Language)
  - Plans the diff between current and desired state
  - Applies changes to match desired state
  - Tracks deployed resources in a state file

Core workflow:
  Write (HCL) → terraform init → terraform plan → terraform apply → terraform destroy

Why Terraform over cloud-native (CloudFormation, ARM):
  - Cloud-agnostic: AWS + GCP + Azure + 1,000+ providers in one codebase
  - Provider ecosystem (Terraform Registry)
  - Elegant dependency graph
  - Mature open source community
  - Terragrunt + Atlantis + Spacelift ecosystem

CONCEPT: HCL Syntax Fundamentals
-----------------------------------
  # Blocks:
  resource "aws_instance" "web" {           → resource <type> <local_name>
    ami           = "ami-0c55b159cbfafe1f0"
    instance_type = "t3.medium"

    tags = {
      Name = "web-server"
    }
  }

  # Variable declaration:
  variable "instance_type" {
    type        = string
    description = "EC2 instance type"
    default     = "t3.medium"
    validation {
      condition     = contains(["t3.medium","t3.large","m5.xlarge"], var.instance_type)
      error_message = "Must be a valid instance type."
    }
    sensitive = false                        → true = masked in logs
  }

  # Data source:
  data "aws_ami" "ubuntu" {
    most_recent = true
    owners      = ["099720109477"]           → Canonical
    filter {
      name   = "name"
      values = ["ubuntu/images/hvm-ssd/ubuntu-*-22.04-amd64-server-*"]
    }
  }

  # Output:
  output "instance_id" {
    value       = aws_instance.web.id
    description = "EC2 instance ID"
    sensitive   = false
  }

  # Locals:
  locals {
    common_tags = {
      Environment = var.environment
      Team        = var.team
      ManagedBy   = "terraform"
    }
    name_prefix = "${var.environment}-${var.project}"
  }

  # References:
  resource "aws_instance" "web" {
    ami           = data.aws_ami.ubuntu.id    → data source ref
    instance_type = var.instance_type         → variable ref
    tags          = local.common_tags         → local ref
  }
  # Cross-resource: aws_vpc.main.id
  # Other module: module.vpc.vpc_id

CONCEPT: Providers
-------------------
  terraform {
    required_version = ">= 1.6.0"
    required_providers {
      aws = {
        source  = "hashicorp/aws"
        version = "~> 5.0"              → pessimistic constraint: >=5.0, <6.0
      }
      kubernetes = {
        source  = "hashicorp/kubernetes"
        version = ">= 2.23.0"
      }
      helm = {
        source  = "hashicorp/helm"
        version = "~> 2.12"
      }
    }
  }

  provider "aws" {
    region  = var.aws_region
    profile = "production"              → AWS CLI profile
    # Or:
    assume_role {
      role_arn     = "arn:aws:iam::123:role/terraform-role"
      session_name = "terraform-prod"
      external_id  = var.external_id
    }
    default_tags {
      tags = local.common_tags          → applied to ALL resources automatically
    }
  }

  # Multiple provider instances (alias):
  provider "aws" {
    alias  = "us_east"
    region = "us-east-1"
  }
  provider "aws" {
    alias  = "eu_west"
    region = "eu-west-1"
  }
  resource "aws_s3_bucket" "eu_bucket" {
    provider = aws.eu_west
    bucket   = "my-eu-bucket"
  }

  Version constraints:
    = 1.2.3    → exact version
    != 1.2.0   → exclude version
    >= 1.2.0   → at least
    ~> 1.2     → >=1.2.0, <2.0.0 (minor compatibility)
    ~> 1.2.3   → >=1.2.3, <1.3.0 (patch compatibility)

CONCEPT: Terraform State
--------------------------
State is Terraform's record of managed resources.
Maps config (resource "aws_instance" "web") → real resource (i-0abc123).

  terraform show                    → human-readable state
  terraform show -json              → JSON state
  terraform state list              → list all resources in state
  terraform state show aws_s3_bucket.main  → inspect specific resource
  terraform state mv resource.old resource.new  → rename resource in state
  terraform state rm resource.name  → remove resource from state (unmanage)
  terraform state pull > state.json → download remote state
  terraform state push state.json   → upload state (dangerous; last resort)
  terraform import aws_s3_bucket.main my-bucket  → import existing resource

  State file contains: resource IDs, metadata, sensitive values (encrypted at rest).
  State file is THE most sensitive Terraform artifact. Treat like a secret.

  terraform.tfstate → local state (default; not for teams)
  Remote state: S3 + DynamoDB (for AWS teams)

CONCEPT: Remote State (S3 Backend)
-------------------------------------
  terraform {
    backend "s3" {
      bucket         = "my-terraform-state"
      key            = "production/vpc/terraform.tfstate"
      region         = "us-east-1"
      encrypt        = true                → SSE-S3 encryption
      kms_key_id     = "arn:aws:kms:..."   → use KMS instead of SSE-S3
      dynamodb_table = "terraform-locks"   → state locking
      profile        = "terraform-admin"
    }
  }

  State locking (DynamoDB):
    Creates a lock record before apply; removes after.
    Prevents concurrent applies from corrupting state.
    DynamoDB table: partition key = LockID (string)

  Bootstrap (create S3 + DynamoDB before they're in state):
    # s3_backend_bootstrap/main.tf
    resource "aws_s3_bucket" "tfstate" {
      bucket = "my-terraform-state"
      lifecycle { prevent_destroy = true }
    }
    resource "aws_s3_bucket_versioning" "tfstate" {
      bucket = aws_s3_bucket.tfstate.id
      versioning_configuration { status = "Enabled" }
    }
    resource "aws_dynamodb_table" "tflock" {
      name         = "terraform-locks"
      billing_mode = "PAY_PER_REQUEST"
      hash_key     = "LockID"
      attribute { name = "LockID"; type = "S" }
    }
    # Apply locally (local backend) → then migrate backend

HANDS-ON TASKS (Foundation):
  1. Write and apply a simple Terraform config (VPC + subnet + EC2)
  2. Run plan, apply, and destroy cycles; observe state changes
  3. Configure S3 backend with DynamoDB locking
  4. Use terraform import to bring existing resources under management
  5. Use terraform state mv to refactor resources without destroying them

================================================================================
2. CORE CONCEPTS — Variables, Expressions, Modules
================================================================================

CONCEPT: Variable Types & Complex Expressions
-----------------------------------------------
  # Type system:
  variable "name"         { type = string }
  variable "count"        { type = number }
  variable "enabled"      { type = bool }
  variable "tags"         { type = map(string) }
  variable "az_list"      { type = list(string) }
  variable "subnets"      { type = list(object({ cidr = string, az = string })) }
  variable "any_value"    { type = any }            → no type enforcement

  # Supplying values (precedence: highest → lowest):
  1. -var flag:           terraform apply -var="region=us-east-1"
  2. -var-file flag:      terraform apply -var-file="prod.tfvars"
  3. TF_VAR_* env vars:   export TF_VAR_region=us-east-1
  4. terraform.tfvars:    auto-loaded
  5. terraform.tfvars.json
  6. *.auto.tfvars:       auto-loaded (alphabetical)
  7. Default value

  # String interpolation:
  "${var.environment}-${var.project}-vpc"

  # Conditional expression:
  instance_type = var.environment == "production" ? "m5.xlarge" : "t3.medium"

  # For expression (transform collections):
  output "instance_ips" {
    value = [for i in aws_instance.web : i.private_ip]
  }
  output "sg_map" {
    value = { for k, v in var.services : k => v.port }
  }

  # Splat operator:
  output "all_arns" {
    value = aws_iam_role.workers[*].arn   → list of all ARNs
  }

  # Dynamic blocks:
  resource "aws_security_group" "app" {
    name = "app-sg"
    dynamic "ingress" {
      for_each = var.allowed_ports
      content {
        from_port   = ingress.value
        to_port     = ingress.value
        protocol    = "tcp"
        cidr_blocks = ["10.0.0.0/8"]
      }
    }
  }

  # Count vs for_each:
  # count: indexed (count.index); order-sensitive; bad for maps
  resource "aws_subnet" "public" {
    count  = length(var.public_cidrs)
    cidr_block = var.public_cidrs[count.index]
  }

  # for_each: key-based; stable identity; better for maps/sets
  resource "aws_subnet" "public" {
    for_each   = { for idx, cidr in var.public_cidrs : idx => cidr }
    cidr_block = each.value
    tags = { Name = "public-${each.key}" }
  }

  # toset() for for_each:
  resource "aws_iam_role_policy_attachment" "policies" {
    for_each   = toset(var.policy_arns)
    role       = aws_iam_role.app.name
    policy_arn = each.value
  }

CONCEPT: Modules — Writing & Using
------------------------------------
  Module = reusable, encapsulated Terraform configuration.

  Module structure:
  modules/vpc/
  ├── main.tf          → resources
  ├── variables.tf     → input variables
  ├── outputs.tf       → output values
  ├── versions.tf      → required_providers (no backend block in modules)
  └── README.md

  # modules/vpc/main.tf
  resource "aws_vpc" "main" {
    cidr_block           = var.vpc_cidr
    enable_dns_hostnames = true
    enable_dns_support   = true
    tags = merge(var.tags, { Name = "${var.name}-vpc" })
  }

  resource "aws_subnet" "private" {
    for_each          = var.private_subnets
    vpc_id            = aws_vpc.main.id
    cidr_block        = each.value.cidr
    availability_zone = each.value.az
    tags = { Name = "${var.name}-private-${each.key}" }
  }

  # modules/vpc/outputs.tf
  output "vpc_id"             { value = aws_vpc.main.id }
  output "private_subnet_ids" { value = [for s in aws_subnet.private : s.id] }

  # Using a module:
  module "vpc" {
    source  = "./modules/vpc"          → local path
    # OR:
    source  = "terraform-aws-modules/vpc/aws"   → Terraform Registry
    version = "~> 5.0"
    # OR:
    source  = "git::https://github.com/org/modules.git//vpc?ref=v1.2.3"

    name    = "production"
    vpc_cidr = "10.0.0.0/16"
    private_subnets = {
      "us-east-1a" = { cidr = "10.0.10.0/24", az = "us-east-1a" }
      "us-east-1b" = { cidr = "10.0.11.0/24", az = "us-east-1b" }
    }
    tags = local.common_tags
  }

  # Accessing module outputs:
  resource "aws_eks_cluster" "main" {
    vpc_config {
      subnet_ids = module.vpc.private_subnet_ids
    }
  }

CONCEPT: Module Design Principles
------------------------------------
  1. Single responsibility: one module = one concern (vpc, eks, rds, iam-role)
  2. Opinionated but flexible: sensible defaults; allow overrides via variables
  3. No backend configuration in modules (only root modules)
  4. No provider configuration in modules (pass via required_providers + root config)
  5. Document all variables and outputs
  6. Version modules (Git tags; Registry versions)
  7. Test modules (Terratest or terraform test)

  Module composition pattern:
    Root module (environment)
      └── module "vpc"
      └── module "eks"  (depends on vpc outputs)
      └── module "rds"  (depends on vpc outputs)
      └── module "apps" (depends on eks + rds outputs)

  Avoid module nesting > 2 levels deep (hard to debug).

CONCEPT: Terraform Lifecycle
------------------------------
  lifecycle {
    create_before_destroy = true    → create new resource before destroying old
                                      useful for zero-downtime replacement
    prevent_destroy       = true    → block terraform destroy on this resource
                                      use on stateful resources (RDS, S3)
    ignore_changes        = [       → don't update resource if these change
      tags,
      ami,
      user_data,
    ]
    replace_triggered_by  = [       → force replacement when these change
      aws_launch_template.app.latest_version
    ]
    precondition {
      condition     = data.aws_ami.ubuntu.architecture == "x86_64"
      error_message = "AMI must be x86_64"
    }
    postcondition {
      condition     = self.arn != ""
      error_message = "ARN must not be empty after creation"
    }
  }

  Most critical:
    create_before_destroy: stateless resources (instances, SGs) — no downtime
    prevent_destroy:       stateful resources (databases, state buckets)
    ignore_changes:        ASG managed fields (desired_capacity), Helm values

CONCEPT: Data Sources
-----------------------
  # Get current AWS account info:
  data "aws_caller_identity" "current" {}
  data "aws_region" "current" {}
  data "aws_partition" "current" {}    → aws, aws-us-gov, aws-cn

  locals {
    account_id = data.aws_caller_identity.current.account_id
    region     = data.aws_region.current.name
    partition  = data.aws_partition.current.partition
  }

  # Look up existing VPC by tag:
  data "aws_vpc" "existing" {
    tags = { Environment = "shared" }
  }

  # Get SSM parameter (for AMI IDs, config):
  data "aws_ssm_parameter" "eks_ami" {
    name = "/aws/service/eks/optimized-ami/1.29/amazon-linux-2/recommended/image_id"
  }

  # Remote state (read another Terraform workspace's outputs):
  data "terraform_remote_state" "vpc" {
    backend = "s3"
    config = {
      bucket = "my-terraform-state"
      key    = "production/vpc/terraform.tfstate"
      region = "us-east-1"
    }
  }
  resource "aws_eks_cluster" "main" {
    vpc_config {
      subnet_ids = data.terraform_remote_state.vpc.outputs.private_subnet_ids
    }
  }

HANDS-ON TASKS (Core):
  1. Write a reusable VPC module with public/private/isolated subnets
  2. Write an EKS module using the VPC module's outputs
  3. Use for_each to create multiple IAM users from a map variable
  4. Write dynamic blocks for security group rules
  5. Use data.terraform_remote_state to share outputs between workspaces
  6. Test lifecycle prevent_destroy by attempting to destroy an RDS resource

================================================================================
3. ADVANCED — Workspaces, Testing, Backends, Sensitive Data
================================================================================

CONCEPT: Workspaces
---------------------
  Workspaces allow multiple state files from the same config.
  Each workspace = isolated state file.

  terraform workspace new staging
  terraform workspace new production
  terraform workspace list
  terraform workspace select production
  terraform workspace show

  Use workspace name in config:
  locals {
    env_config = {
      staging    = { instance_type = "t3.medium",  min_nodes = 2  }
      production = { instance_type = "m5.xlarge",  min_nodes = 5  }
    }
    config = local.env_config[terraform.workspace]
  }

  Workspace state path in S3:
    Default: key = "terraform.tfstate"
    With workspaces: key = "env:/production/terraform.tfstate"

  Workspace limitations:
    - Single config directory for all envs (harder isolation)
    - Easy to accidentally apply to wrong workspace
    - Preferred alternative: Terragrunt (separate state per directory)

  Workspaces good for:
    - Ephemeral feature branch environments
    - Running module tests in isolation

CONCEPT: Sensitive Values
--------------------------
  variable "db_password" {
    type      = string
    sensitive = true         → masked in plan + apply output; still in state
  }

  output "connection_string" {
    value     = "postgresql://user:${var.db_password}@${aws_db_instance.main.endpoint}"
    sensitive = true         → mask in output
  }

  # Sensitive in locals:
  locals {
    db_url = sensitive("postgres://${var.db_password}@...")
  }

  IMPORTANT: sensitive = true only masks in terminal output.
  The value is STILL stored in plain text in the state file.
  Protect state file (S3 encryption + bucket policy + audit access).

  Best practice for secrets:
    - Use AWS Secrets Manager / SSM → reference with data source
    - Pass as environment variables (TF_VAR_*) → not committed to code
    - Never hardcode secrets in .tfvars files committed to Git

CONCEPT: Moved Blocks (Refactoring Without Destroy)
-----------------------------------------------------
  When renaming a resource or moving it into/out of a module,
  use moved blocks to update state without destroying the resource.

  # Rename a resource:
  moved {
    from = aws_instance.old_name
    to   = aws_instance.new_name
  }

  # Move into a module:
  moved {
    from = aws_s3_bucket.main
    to   = module.storage.aws_s3_bucket.main
  }

  # Move out of a module:
  moved {
    from = module.app.aws_instance.web
    to   = aws_instance.web
  }

  terraform plan → shows moves without destroys; verify before apply.

CONCEPT: Terraform Testing (terraform test, Terratest)
-------------------------------------------------------
  terraform test (native, Terraform 1.6+):
    Write test files in tests/ directory.
    Each .tftest.hcl file runs a series of assertions.

    # tests/vpc.tftest.hcl
    provider "aws" { region = "us-east-1" }

    variables {
      vpc_cidr    = "10.100.0.0/16"
      environment = "test"
    }

    run "creates_vpc" {
      command = apply                  → actually creates infrastructure

      assert {
        condition     = output.vpc_id != ""
        error_message = "VPC ID must not be empty"
      }
      assert {
        condition     = length(output.private_subnet_ids) == 3
        error_message = "Must create 3 private subnets"
      }
    }

    run "plan_only_check" {
      command = plan                   → no real resources created
      assert {
        condition     = aws_vpc.main.enable_dns_hostnames == true
        error_message = "DNS hostnames must be enabled"
      }
    }

    terraform test                     → runs all .tftest.hcl files

  Terratest (Go-based integration testing):
    # vpc_test.go
    func TestVPCModule(t *testing.T) {
      t.Parallel()
      opts := terraform.WithDefaultRetryableErrors(t, &terraform.Options{
        TerraformDir: "../modules/vpc",
        Vars: map[string]interface{}{
          "vpc_cidr":    "10.100.0.0/16",
          "environment": "test",
        },
      })
      defer terraform.Destroy(t, opts)
      terraform.InitAndApply(t, opts)

      vpcID := terraform.Output(t, opts, "vpc_id")
      assert.NotEmpty(t, vpcID)

      vpc := aws.GetVpcById(t, vpcID, "us-east-1")
      assert.Equal(t, "10.100.0.0/16", aws.GetVpcCidr(t, vpc))
    }

CONCEPT: Import Block (Terraform 1.5+)
-----------------------------------------
  Declarative import: add import block, run plan → see generated config.

  import {
    to = aws_s3_bucket.main
    id = "my-existing-bucket-name"
  }

  # Generate config from import:
  terraform plan -generate-config-out=generated.tf
  # Review generated.tf → add to your config → run apply

  # Multi-resource import with for_each:
  locals {
    buckets = {
      logs    = "my-logs-bucket"
      backups = "my-backup-bucket"
    }
  }
  import {
    for_each = local.buckets
    to       = aws_s3_bucket.buckets[each.key]
    id       = each.value
  }

CONCEPT: Provider Parallelism & Performance
---------------------------------------------
  terraform apply -parallelism=20   → default 10; increase for large infra

  Target specific resources (use sparingly — can cause drift):
  terraform plan -target=module.eks
  terraform apply -target=aws_security_group.app

  Refresh state without apply:
  terraform refresh                  → sync state with real world (deprecated)
  terraform apply -refresh-only     → modern equivalent (shows drift)

  Lock file (.terraform.lock.hcl):
  Locks provider versions + checksums.
  Always commit .terraform.lock.hcl to Git.
  terraform providers lock           → update lock file
  terraform init -upgrade            → upgrade providers to latest allowed versions

HANDS-ON TASKS (Advanced):
  1. Write terraform test for a VPC module; run with terraform test
  2. Refactor: use moved blocks to reorganize resources without destroying
  3. Use import block to adopt 5 existing S3 buckets into Terraform
  4. Handle sensitive database password: use Secrets Manager data source
  5. Create ephemeral environments using workspaces; clean up after PR merge
  6. Implement lifecycle prevent_destroy on production RDS

================================================================================
SECTION B: TERRAGRUNT
================================================================================

CONCEPT: Why Terragrunt
------------------------
Problem with raw Terraform at scale:
  1. Backend configuration duplication (every module needs S3+DynamoDB config)
  2. Provider configuration duplication
  3. No DRY way to pass outputs between modules
  4. Running modules in dependency order (manually)
  5. Workspace per environment gets messy for 50+ environments

Terragrunt solves this with:
  - DRY backend configuration (define once; inherit everywhere)
  - Auto-generate provider and backend blocks
  - run-all: apply all modules in dependency order
  - dependency blocks: explicit cross-module output references
  - generate blocks: generate provider.tf, backend.tf automatically
  - locals + include for DRY configuration

CONCEPT: Terragrunt Directory Structure
-----------------------------------------
  infrastructure/
  ├── terragrunt.hcl                  → ROOT config (DRY backend + provider)
  ├── _envcommon/                     → common configs (shared by envs)
  │   ├── eks.hcl
  │   ├── vpc.hcl
  │   └── rds.hcl
  ├── prod/
  │   ├── terragrunt.hcl              → env-level config
  │   ├── vpc/
  │   │   └── terragrunt.hcl
  │   ├── eks/
  │   │   └── terragrunt.hcl
  │   └── rds/
  │       └── terragrunt.hcl
  └── staging/
      ├── terragrunt.hcl
      ├── vpc/
      │   └── terragrunt.hcl
      └── eks/
          └── terragrunt.hcl

CONCEPT: Root terragrunt.hcl
------------------------------
  # infrastructure/terragrunt.hcl
  locals {
    account_vars = read_terragrunt_config(find_in_parent_folders("account.hcl"))
    region_vars  = read_terragrunt_config(find_in_parent_folders("region.hcl"))
    env_vars     = read_terragrunt_config(find_in_parent_folders("env.hcl"))

    account_id  = local.account_vars.locals.account_id
    aws_region  = local.region_vars.locals.aws_region
    environment = local.env_vars.locals.environment
  }

  # Generate backend.tf in every module:
  generate "backend" {
    path      = "backend.tf"
    if_exists = "overwrite_terragrunt"
    contents  = <<EOF
  terraform {
    backend "s3" {
      bucket         = "my-terraform-state-${local.account_id}"
      key            = "${path_relative_to_include()}/terraform.tfstate"
      region         = "${local.aws_region}"
      encrypt        = true
      dynamodb_table = "terraform-locks"
    }
  }
  EOF
  }

  # Generate provider.tf in every module:
  generate "provider" {
    path      = "provider.tf"
    if_exists = "overwrite_terragrunt"
    contents  = <<EOF
  provider "aws" {
    region = "${local.aws_region}"
    assume_role {
      role_arn = "arn:aws:iam::${local.account_id}:role/terraform-role"
    }
    default_tags {
      tags = {
        Environment = "${local.environment}"
        ManagedBy   = "terragrunt"
      }
    }
  }
  EOF
  }

  # Common remote_state config:
  remote_state {
    backend = "s3"
    generate = {
      path      = "backend.tf"
      if_exists = "overwrite_terragrunt"
    }
    config = {
      bucket         = "my-terraform-state-${local.account_id}"
      key            = "${path_relative_to_include()}/terraform.tfstate"
      region         = local.aws_region
      encrypt        = true
      dynamodb_table = "terraform-locks"
    }
  }

CONCEPT: Module terragrunt.hcl
---------------------------------
  # infrastructure/prod/vpc/terragrunt.hcl
  include "root" {
    path   = find_in_parent_folders()    → auto-find root terragrunt.hcl
    expose = true                        → access parent locals
  }

  include "common" {
    path   = "${dirname(find_in_parent_folders())}/_envcommon/vpc.hcl"
    expose = true
    merge_strategy = "deep"              → deep merge with local config
  }

  terraform {
    source = "git::https://github.com/org/terraform-modules.git//vpc?ref=v2.3.0"
    # OR local:
    source = "../../../modules/vpc"
    # OR registry:
    source = "tfr:///terraform-aws-modules/vpc/aws?version=5.1.0"
  }

  inputs = {
    vpc_cidr    = "10.0.0.0/16"
    environment = include.root.locals.environment
    private_subnets = [
      { cidr = "10.0.10.0/24", az = "us-east-1a" },
      { cidr = "10.0.11.0/24", az = "us-east-1b" },
      { cidr = "10.0.12.0/24", az = "us-east-1c" },
    ]
  }

CONCEPT: Dependency Blocks
---------------------------
  # infrastructure/prod/eks/terragrunt.hcl
  include "root" {
    path = find_in_parent_folders()
  }

  dependency "vpc" {
    config_path = "../vpc"
    mock_outputs = {
      vpc_id             = "vpc-00000000000000000"
      private_subnet_ids = ["subnet-00000000", "subnet-11111111", "subnet-22222222"]
    }
    mock_outputs_allowed_terraform_commands = ["validate", "plan"]
  }

  dependency "kms" {
    config_path = "../kms"
    mock_outputs = {
      key_arn = "arn:aws:kms:us-east-1:123456789:key/00000000"
    }
  }

  terraform {
    source = "git::https://github.com/org/terraform-modules.git//eks?ref=v1.5.0"
  }

  inputs = {
    vpc_id             = dependency.vpc.outputs.vpc_id
    private_subnet_ids = dependency.vpc.outputs.private_subnet_ids
    kms_key_arn        = dependency.kms.outputs.key_arn
    cluster_name       = "prod-${dependency.vpc.outputs.vpc_id}"
  }

  mock_outputs: used when dependency hasn't been applied yet.
  Allows: terragrunt validate/plan without dependencies applied.

CONCEPT: run-all Commands
---------------------------
  # Apply all modules in dependency order:
  terragrunt run-all apply

  # Plan across all modules:
  terragrunt run-all plan

  # Plan with diff from last state:
  terragrunt run-all plan --terragrunt-out-dir /tmp/plan-out

  # Only apply changed modules (based on git diff):
  terragrunt run-all apply --terragrunt-modules-that-include terragrunt.hcl

  # Apply specific environment:
  cd infrastructure/prod && terragrunt run-all apply

  # Exclude certain modules:
  terragrunt run-all apply --terragrunt-exclude-dir prod/legacy-app

  # Run in specific order (respects dependencies automatically):
  # vpc → eks → apps (eks depends on vpc; apps depends on eks)
  terragrunt run-all apply --terragrunt-parallelism 5

  run-all order:
    1. Build dependency graph
    2. Apply modules with no dependencies first
    3. Apply next layer once dependencies complete
    4. Fully parallel within each layer

CONCEPT: generate Blocks & Hooks
-----------------------------------
  generate "versions" {
    path      = "versions_override.tf"
    if_exists = "overwrite_terragrunt"
    contents  = <<EOF
  terraform {
    required_version = ">= 1.6.0"
    required_providers {
      aws = {
        source  = "hashicorp/aws"
        version = "~> 5.0"
      }
    }
  }
  EOF
  }

  # Before/after hooks:
  terraform {
    source = "..."
    before_hook "validate_env" {
      commands = ["apply", "plan"]
      execute  = ["bash", "-c", "test -n '$TF_VAR_environment' || exit 1"]
    }
    after_hook "notify_slack" {
      commands     = ["apply"]
      execute      = ["bash", "-c", "./scripts/slack-notify.sh 'Apply complete'"]
      run_on_error = false
    }
    error_hook "on_failure" {
      commands  = ["apply"]
      execute   = ["bash", "-c", "./scripts/slack-notify.sh 'Apply FAILED'"]
      on_errors = [".*"]
    }
  }

CONCEPT: Terragrunt Locals & Read Functions
---------------------------------------------
  # account.hcl (per-account config)
  locals {
    account_id   = "123456789012"
    account_name = "production"
  }

  # region.hcl (per-region config)
  locals {
    aws_region = "us-east-1"
  }

  # In module terragrunt.hcl:
  locals {
    account_vars = read_terragrunt_config(find_in_parent_folders("account.hcl"))
    account_id   = local.account_vars.locals.account_id

    # Read YAML config:
    app_config   = yamldecode(file("${get_terragrunt_dir()}/app_config.yaml"))

    # Expose Terraform outputs of current state:
    state_config = run_cmd("--terragrunt-quiet", "terragrunt", "output", "-json")
  }

  Terragrunt built-in functions:
    find_in_parent_folders()         → walk up to find file
    path_relative_to_include()       → relative path from include root
    get_terragrunt_dir()             → current terragrunt.hcl dir
    get_repo_root()                  → git repo root
    get_env("VAR", "default")        → read environment variable
    read_terragrunt_config(path)     → parse another terragrunt.hcl
    run_cmd(cmd, args...)            → run shell command; use output in config

CONCEPT: Atlantis — GitOps for Terraform
------------------------------------------
Atlantis automates Terraform via Pull Requests.

  Workflow:
    1. Developer opens PR
    2. Atlantis comments: "Ran Plan for module X:\n<plan output>"
    3. Team reviews plan in PR
    4. Reviewer comments: "atlantis apply"
    5. Atlantis runs apply; posts result
    6. PR merged automatically or manually

  atlantis.yaml:
    version: 3
    automerge: false
    delete_source_branch_on_merge: true
    projects:
    - name: prod-vpc
      dir: infrastructure/prod/vpc
      workspace: default
      terragrunt_version: 0.54.0
      autoplan:
        when_modified:
        - "**/*.tf"
        - "**/*.hcl"
        - "**/*.tfvars"
        enabled: true
      apply_requirements:
      - mergeable
      - approved
      - undiverged

  Apply requirements:
    approved:     at least 1 approval
    mergeable:    all checks passing
    undiverged:   branch up to date with base

CONCEPT: Production Terraform Architecture
-------------------------------------------
  Repository layout for enterprise:
  terraform-infrastructure/
  ├── modules/                         → reusable modules (versioned via git tags)
  │   ├── vpc/
  │   ├── eks/
  │   ├── rds/
  │   └── iam-role/
  ├── accounts/
  │   ├── management/                  → org mgmt account
  │   ├── shared-services/             → shared tools (ECR, DNS, logging)
  │   ├── production/
  │   │   ├── account.hcl
  │   │   ├── us-east-1/
  │   │   │   ├── region.hcl
  │   │   │   ├── vpc/
  │   │   │   ├── eks/
  │   │   │   └── rds/
  │   │   └── eu-west-1/
  │   └── staging/
  └── terragrunt.hcl                   → root config

  Security separation:
    - Terraform state bucket: separate account (shared-services)
    - Each environment: separate AWS account (AWS Organizations)
    - Terraform role: least-privilege; separate per environment
    - State bucket: strict bucket policy + KMS encryption

HANDS-ON TASKS (Terragrunt):
  1. Set up Terragrunt with root config auto-generating backend + provider
  2. Create 3-layer structure: vpc → eks → apps with dependency blocks
  3. Use run-all apply to deploy full environment; observe ordering
  4. Implement mock_outputs for plan without dependencies
  5. Add before_hook for environment validation
  6. Set up Atlantis locally with Docker; test PR-based apply workflow

REAL-WORLD SCENARIOS:
  SCENARIO A — Terraform state corruption recovery:
    Symptoms: "Error acquiring the state lock" (stale lock from failed apply)
    Diagnosis:
      terraform force-unlock <LOCK_ID>   → unlock after verifying no apply running
      # LOCK_ID from: aws dynamodb get-item --table-name terraform-locks --key '{"LockID":{"S":"<bucket>/key"}}'
    Symptoms: state drift (resource exists in cloud, not in state)
      terraform import resource.name resource_id  → bring back under management
    Symptoms: state shows resource, cloud doesn't have it
      terraform state rm resource.name   → remove from state; re-apply to recreate
    Prevention:
      Always use remote state + locking
      Never run terraform apply from multiple places simultaneously
      Use Atlantis/Spacelift (serialized applies)

  SCENARIO B — Zero-downtime infrastructure refactoring:
    Task: split monolithic VPC module into vpc + subnets + routing modules.
    Plan:
      1. Write new module structure
      2. terraform plan → identify what would be destroyed/recreated
      3. Add moved blocks for all existing resources
      4. terraform plan -target=module.vpc → verify no destroys
      5. Apply → resources moved in state; no infrastructure change
      6. Run full plan/apply → verify clean
    Key rule: moved blocks prevent destroy + recreate during refactoring.

  SCENARIO C — Multi-account Terraform with AWS Organizations:
    Structure:
      Management account → Terraform CI role (can assume roles in all accounts)
      Each account → TerraformExecutionRole with trust to management account
    Terragrunt root:
      generate "provider" with assume_role for each account
      Each account directory has account.hcl with account_id
      Terragrunt automatically generates correct assume_role per module
    CI/CD:
      GitHub Actions → OIDC → management account role → assume child account role
      No long-lived credentials anywhere

################################################################################
#      PHASE 12: OBSERVABILITY — PROMETHEUS, GRAFANA, LOKI, PROMTAIL,         #
